# Inheritance of Enforced Colour Changes

## IV. Communication:

## The Colour Dress of the Fire Salamander (*Salamandra maculosa* Laurenti) in its Dependence on the Environment.

By

### Paul Kammerer.

*(From the Biological Experimental Institute in Vienna.)*

With Plates II–XVI.

Received on 8 December 1912.

*Archiv für Entwicklungsmechanik der Organismen*, vol. 36 (1913).

## I. Introduction.

Just like the experiments to induce *Salamandra atra* to give birth to gill-bearing, water-dwelling larvae, and *Salamandra maculosa* to give birth to lung-breathing, land-dwelling fully-developed newts, the experiments on the influencing of the salamander colour by the nature of the ground reach back into my boyhood: as early as 1892 — I was then 12 years old — I kept spotted earth-newts partly on yellow loam soil, partly on black garden earth, and observed changes in the distribution of the black ground-colour and yellow markings.

The value of scientific experiments, conducted in conscious awareness of their theoretical significance and exploitable for publication, those cultures of mine of course only acquired during my student years, from 1900 onward; at any rate, in my larger first work (Kammerer 1904) on the influence of yellow and black earth almost all of this is already contained which I am today in a position to demonstrate at the hand of the plate illustrations. Experiments and control experiments have indeed since then been built out in manifold directions and frequently repeated, — much of what I stated in 1904 is to be supplemented, to be modified, to be expressed more cautiously, or even to be corrected, and this last applies especially to the statistically gathered correlations between colour dress and conditions of residence in free-living (see pp. 24–29) —, but the principle, the lawfulness, itself stood firm of old.

While now the content of my work of that time (1904), insofar as it concerned the propagation of the salamander, became widely disseminated through discussions and citations, that portion which dealt with the salamander colour has become little known.

As the next fruit of those long-standing breedings I delivered, at the Naturalists' Assembly in Salzburg (Kammerer 1910 d)¹), a lecture which soon passed over into the literature referenced [there]; even more, I saw the case so far advanced that I — all the sooner since a preliminary communication (a reprint of this lecture *in extenso*) had now also appeared at a competent place of publication (Kammerer 1911/12)²) — [reported] on it at the International Zoological Congress in Graz.

> ¹) In a joint session of the Sections for Botany, Zoology, Anatomy and Physiology, five members of the Biological Experimental Institute in Vienna presented principal results from eight experimental-morphological investigations conducted there; in the "Verhandlungen der Gesellschaft Deutscher Naturforscher und Ärzte", however, these lectures appeared under III. "Abteilung für Botanik", where, since they were almost all of zoological content, they will surely seldom be sought and overlooked in general. What my own lecture concerns, its reprint took place in full (Kammerer 1910 a) and in part (Kammerer 1909) in popular journals, a circumstance which naturally likewise did not contribute to making it accessible to the colleagues in the subject.

> ²) Unfortunately, here (Zeitschr. ind. Abst.-Vererb.-Lehre) [Journal of inductive descent and inheritance theory] the plate illustrations turned out very badly; indeed Fig. 1 and *b* of Plate III, as a consequence of a confusion of the originals on the part of the reproduction establishment, do not at all depict the animal to which the description on p. 280 refers. Corrections were not submitted to me; I was surprised, on the appearance of the work, with the *fait accompli*!

Since the appearance of this preliminary communication a small literature has indeed already accrued in this regard, and I shall also avail myself of this here, in order not to have to come forward myself with detailed data; for the abstracts and conclusions drawn from my brief preliminary reports have conjured up such a wealth of misunderstandings and interpretations far beyond the goal, that I, no longer being able to wait it out, would gladly have completed especially the crossbreeding and transplantation experiments. The actual induction experiments are for that reason today, I believe, brought to so far-reaching a conclusion as is at all attainable in such works, which can and shall never be "finished" before the definitive publication. — With the utterances made by other authors about my findings I shall occupy myself only in Chap. IX "Criticism and counter-criticism", X "Theoretical evaluation", partly also in Chap. III "The normal colour dress".

Still a second ground urges to this — to bring my hitherto results before the public without further hesitation: several colleagues, who inspected my animals and the experimental arrangement, declared to me that they would now turn to the same problem — others wrote it to me, and also the treatise of E. G. Boulenger seems to announce it. Now there are, however, in carrying out such experiments certain preconditions which, if one does not know them, easily become insurmountable obstacles and frustrate the entire outcome of the experiment. So simple, indeed downright artless does the arrangement of the experiments appear to be when one reads it in a brief, dateless description or hears it presented, — yes even when one inspects the experiments already in progress on the spot: there belongs nevertheless very much experience and practice to them, in order to set them going correctly and to maintain them. In particular the experimenter must be familiar with the husbandry technique as a self-acquired, sourly and dearly bought experience; the living habits and necessities of life of the animals, their whole peculiar manner, even beginning from the way of grasping them, and on the occasion of the revision or of the colouring [the comparison of several individuals] must again, when so sown, have so passed into flesh and blood that one may apply oneself to it as something one's own and experiment with sure success; for which one must devote a good piece of one's life to it; under one to two decades it does not go at all! How often, though, do I see zoologists despite better, written or even practical guidance bring all their experimental animals to ruin in short order; or they languish for a long time, but then of course did not react normally in such a condition. Also in this field tradition does not vouch for genuine progress, but solely one's own acquisition or the happily inherited disposition.

The continuation of my experimenting in respect of the salamander colours, to leave it to others, is, despite the difficulties which I foresee, actually my intention: although I have the feeling, with regard to the explanation of the observed phenomena, of standing rather at the beginning than at the end, I would probably still be occupied with this problem — which has become more than any other my problem-child [Schmerzenskind] — for a long time. Since it, as indicated above, seems in gratifying manner to make a school, I need at its further pursuit not despair: sooner or later still to bring to general acknowledgement what on the ground of my experimental experience has up to now become to all too few besides myself an unshakeable conviction: the power of the Milieu and the high significance of the individual for the history of its tribe.

## II. Technique.

### 1. Husbandry technique.

The earth-salamanders are at bottom quite sensitive pupils [to rear]. It is indeed true that, under very simple care, one succeeds in keeping a salamander alive for years, perhaps in a little box with moss kept somewhat moist and rainworms thrown in from time to time; but one may not count on keeping, under such conditions, larger quantities of experimental animals permanently in good health, still less on bringing them to propagation and seeing their offspring grow up again to sexual maturity and reproductive fitness; all this becomes once again at the least precarious when certain experimental factors, which deviate in some direction from the natural living conditions, in any case make strong demands on the vitality of the animals. Were it not for other properties — the variability of the simply composed colouring and marking, the prompt reacting in propagation and colour to the various influences of the colour-world, the relatively comfortable raising of a tolerably numerous descendancy — which predestine the salamander to the experimental subject of experimental biology, its keepability and breedability in captive-life alone would rather scare one off from using it for durability- and namely inheritance experiments.

It cannot here be my task to give instruction on the care of the fire-salamander at all; in order to orient oneself thereupon, the budding salamander-breeder must absolutely read a guide to terrarium-lore, best one of the two works by Krefft; I myself have in my work 1904, as well as in the description of our Institute (Przibram 1908/9), finally in a recently appeared booklet "Terrarium and Insectarium" (Kammerer 1912), more or less in detail expressed myself thereupon.

At the first-named place (1904) I also described the most frequent illnesses of the fully developed salamanders and their larvae; in the meantime my practice in this regard has been enriched, in an undesired manner, by two new, repeatedly occurring pathological phenomena, one each on the full-grown animal and on the larva. The first consists in this, that the salamanders cannot properly moult: the hardened skin remains in wart-like crusts on the body, thereby effects a darkening, indeed sometimes a near-disappearance of all the yellow spots, which without closer investigation could easily be taken for a wasting-away of the yellow pigment and thus mislead into disastrous deceptions. The adhering of the old skin draws after it a hindrance of the skin-respiration: the animals consequently often open the mouth wide, raise themselves up high upon their legs as if in cramp; finally their body becomes stiff as wood, and then within at most one day death sets in, — the animals that have perished of the illness look as if smoked and do not change for days, whereas otherwise decay makes rapid progress. Fahr has observed this illness only on animals that came into the world in the terrarium, — I just as often on those that still stemmed from free-living. Incorrect nutrition is not, as Fahr supposes, to blame for it; but rather an insufficient or untimely moistening of the skin. In containers whose ground, for the sake of the experiment, is drier than agrees with the normal living habits, further in containers without a water basin, in overwintering boxes, where indeed not regularly water is poured and sprayed, the illness is most frequent. Also chance circumstances which hinder the animal from using its moistening- and bathing-opportunity can be partly responsible: for example the salamanders remain, when it is very cold, in their hiding-corners, do not seek out the water basin; if this lasts for days and the hiding-corners are then not themselves very damp and the animal stands shortly before moulting, then the mishap is already complete. With dryness as the cause of origin it also agrees that the illness fortunately does not appear epidemically, but always only arises from the same external causes, yet is not transmissible from one specimen to another. In one case Fahr achieved a cure through warm baths, which accelerated the moulting; presumably this was in the initial stage of the affliction. I saw every such case end lethally despite lukewarm washings.

A second new illness sometimes works havoc among the larvae; in distinction from the first-described one it appears epidemically. The first sign consists in this, that the mobility of the limbs, especially the hinder ones, and of the tail conspicuously decreases, so that the larva remains on the bottom and voluntarily no longer swims. Then it loses its balance, lies on its side, but can through prodding still always be moved to dart a stretch further, under obviously great exertion. Finally it becomes wholly motionless, and yet, as one recognizes from the contractions of the heart, days can still pass before it is dead. I believe that this illness, or rather its pathogen, is introduced with the food, the red brook-tube-worms (*Tubifex tubifex*), especially when a slight contamination of the water occurs thereby. Frequent change of water, otherwise by no means to be recommended, is then also, alongside transition to flesh- and *Daphnia*-feeding, the best means to save at least several specimens of the affected brood. To a definite season and water temperature I did not see the illness bound.

As may already emerge from these illness-pictures, the chief and normal requirements of the salamander larvae are clean water no more than 12 cm deep, no warmer than 17 degrees C., for the fully developed salamander an even moisture (not wetness) of the ground, 60–75 % relative air humidity, a roomy water basin with comfortable opportunity (stones) for climbing in and out, and above all things once again the most meticulous cleanliness. Never may the terrarium-air exhibit a musty mould- or even rot-smell; it should either be odourless A hygienic condition will establish itself and regulate itself automatically only if the terrarium possesses arrangements for **air and soil ventilation**, as well as for **soil drainage (drainage)**; the installation of these arrangements is partly already a matter of construction, and partly a matter of the first interior fitting-out, which is left unaltered for many years. It was chiefly for this reason that I had to refer to the technical writings cited above, since here I absolutely cannot go into the matter. The ready-made terraria available for purchase do not, as a rule, possess those arrangements; one is therefore mostly compelled to have them first made according to the model of the terrarium "Model KAMMERER", as described and illustrated especially by PRZIBRAM (1908/9). Repeatedly, for economic reasons — since the number of available experimental terraria was no longer sufficient — recourse was had to containers of simpler and older construction, e.g. large, wire-grated and glazed wooden terraria; but every single time this housing, even of well-acclimatized animals, ended in failure.

2. **Experimental technique.** Various considerations determined me to begin each experimental series — i.e. each experiment whose objects, owing to influence in the same sense, may be kept together in one and the same container — with 40 specimens. Although this was as a rule done with smaller animals, on average 70 mm long (cf. e.g. Pl. II, VII and XII), which are nevertheless considerably older than freshly metamorphosed ones, one nonetheless requires for this a terrarium of 100 cm length, 80 cm width, 60 cm height, if one does not wish from the outset to count with certainty on an opening loss-account, which is to be attributed to the transfer from free life into captive life. After years, when the number of experimental animals had in any case diminished somewhat from other causes, or when the animals had become so well acclimatized that a greater restriction of space no longer harmed them so easily, or finally, when it was a matter of accommodating approximately as many specimens of the freshly metamorphosed offspring — only about 45–48 mm long — I resorted, more in obedience to necessity, to identically constructed terraria of only 60 cm length, 36 cm width, 50 cm height, in which the salamander cultures by no means throve so well and, above all, by far did not multiply so strongly as in the large ones. But one simply cannot, unfortunately, have an unlimited number of the very costly large containers at one's disposal; it is already an advance that the breedings take place at all in such small terraria — the salamander has, with us, simply become completely a domestic animal. Originally I could bring about the breeding only under the open sky, in open-air terraria (see their description in KAMMERER 1908, 1912 and in PRZIBRAM 1908/9, here together with a picture), where, for reasons still to be discussed, the experimental conditions, and therefore their results, also the possibilities of control, turn out far less exactly than inside a building.

That base number — 40 — tends, as said, to diminish somewhat in the course of the first induction years, then to rise temporarily strongly through reproduction, yet to be restricted again, through the mortality of the larvae and young, to the initial state; but on the whole one can, with careful tending, count on being able to keep an overview, permanently and on average, of about half a hundred specimens of each experimental series.

Already to the experimental technique proper belongs the feeding. The best food for salamanders in general consists of earthworms and slugs. For a twofold reason I had to refrain from this: firstly, to forestall the objection that the observed colour changes — increase of the yellow on yellow, of the black on black ground — were to be attributed to an uptake of colouring substances, that is, to a kind of vital staining. Today I know that this is not the case, and, on account of the convenience and naturalness of this mode of nourishment, I no longer hesitate, at least in the warm season, to feed the small field slugs (*Limax agrestis*). But a second ground holds me back, now as before, from administering earthworms: sand and loam soil is so contaminated by them through the excrements that the bright tone of these substrates soon becomes dark, indeed in places blackish. If this forbids the use of earthworms on a bright underground, then, for the sake of equality of the conditions, they ought not to be used on black ground either, where the contamination would be without object. In order to avoid the introduction of earthworms even at the new setting-up of the experiment, the earth to be filled into the bottom part of the terrarium was carefully sieved through beforehand. In the open-air terraria, by contrast, the settling-in of earthworms could not be avoided — here they were also fed with them, and above all to this circumstance I must attribute it that there the loam soil, within 1–2 years, could scarcely be distinguished any longer from humus soil and had to be heaped up afresh. In the meantime, however, the salamanders had of course already lived for a fairly long time on a mixed soil, and the contrasts of their colours to those of the black culture were therefore less great than in the room-breedings.

So for the latter, then, there remained no other food than the so universally used "**meal-worm**", the larva of *Tenebrio molitor*. It is not the ideal of a salamander food, especially at the beginning; yet the animals accustom themselves to it, just as they must also first accustom themselves to feeding out of dishes. Since this is more convenient than the rummaging-through of the whole terrarium, which on the earthworm- and snail-hunt must precede the acquisition of prey, they finally prefer meal-worms to everything else and thrive quite well on this fare poor in variety. With many another animal such a demand would directly frustrate the course of the experiment and of the breeding. For the setting-up of meal-worms in animal containers I used otherwise to recommend the so-called mustard- or sugar-bowls of white porcelain, on account of their flatness and of the smooth, even curvature that nevertheless prevents the escape of the meal-worms; but in the colour-breedings I saw myself, since white bowls here could call forth a disturbance of the colour effect, compelled to use little glass dishes, which are buried up to the upper rim into the ground, so that the salamanders do not snap from the side in vain. For they never come to know the glass; the frequent knocking of the snout against the hard obstacle does, however, finally make them unlearn the appetite for feeding. In the earth-breedings, where the factors are not isolated and where it therefore matters not so much that one bring into the experimental space substances whose effect one does not know, I used as meal-worm dishes on loam earth also the yellow crocks of Strasbourg goose-liver pâté, naturally without lids — on garden earth, glass cups which had been varnished black on the outside. The feeding-bowls are not to be stocked too densely, but rather, in return, more frequently with meal-worms; out of a teeming mass the salamanders do not feed gladly, but rather most prefer to seize a meal-worm that rears itself up at the rim of the bowl and therefore for the moment lies somewhat isolated. Otherwise too it is of advantage if the meal-worms do not remain lying too long in the little bowl: for, firstly, they have nothing to feed on there and thereby become themselves less nutritious; the gut of a meal-worm freshly fetched from the breeding-box is bursting with white, starch-containing chyle, after a few days only black faeces are to be seen in it. Then the meal-worms remaining in the bowls soon grow weary in their movements, the latter of which, however, in turn provide the most irresistible appetite-stimulus for the salamander. One thus saves nothing thereby if one fills the bowls quite densely with meal-worms and believes that one may, in return, look in less frequently.

As food **for the salamander larvae** I have already long held exclusively to *Tubifex*, despite the danger of epidemic which was described above. For, firstly, with correct feeding — which should always be so sparing that no remains are left over — and keeping the larvae-containers clean, it [the danger] is not so great that the total destruction of an experimental series is to be feared; secondly, through other feeding-stuffs (Daphnien, *Cyclops*, mosquito larvae) parasites can likewise be introduced; thirdly, the feeding-animals last enumerated are, because of their hard body-covering, less liked, and also not so easily to be had at every season; fourthly, they occasionally bite off the gills and fin-seams of the salamander larvae, thus becoming themselves robbers. But to feed with raw flesh-fibres or cut-up earthworms is, on account of the time-expenditure bound up with it — one must hold the morsel before the snout of each individual animal on the forceps — with such quantities of experimental larvae as the colour experiments require, an impossible undertaking.

And now I come to the most important requirement which must be fulfilled in order that reactions of the salamander coloration on differently coloured grounds may become recognizable at all: copious, vigorous, light falling from above! The terraria with the colour experiments all stand in a long passage furnished with skylight-windows (see its illustration together with the experimental set-up in Fig. 22, Pl. VIII in PRZIBRAM 1908/9); besides this, large windows also open at the side onto a second passage, covered exclusively with glass, so that beside the overhead light a fairly large amount of side-light also falls in. The terraria stand there upon shelf-stands (Etagèren), but exclusively their topmost standing-surfaces come into consideration for the experiments — the higher and nearer to the glazed-over ceiling, the better. Already one compartment lower, where only side-light falls in, the reaction takes place many times more slowly, with older objects apparently not at all any more, and still further down, changes are scarcely any longer to be awaited, at least within the time-spans available to the experimenter — by which I do not mean to assert that they do not proceed at all, only just so infinitely slowly that one would really require the "innumerable years" claimed by the descent-theory hitherto in order to make them visible after ever so many generations. K. v. FRISCH (oral communication — I cannot yet cite in the bibliography his work, which presumably appears about simultaneously with the present one), who checked over my investigation on the action of a yellow and black paper underlay upon freshly metamorphosed *Salamandra maculosa* and had no overhead light at his disposal, replaced it by mirrors, with which he reflected the necessary quantities of light into the experimental containers, and thereby arrived very quickly at results which agree with mine.

The demand for glaring light is so undeniable because the salamander body does, after all, expose itself only to a small fraction of what the location offers in brightness. To be sure, the salamander is not, as is often believed, a night animal, but it is indeed a **twilight animal**, and specifically an animal of the morning twilight. This is more favourable for the experiments than if its chief activity-time fell, as e.g. with the toads, upon the evening twilight; for it remains outside its hiding-places until the sun's rays reach the ground — but before that it has, after all, already become completely day. Like many other captively kept animals which are fed and tended by day, the salamander too, moreover, passes gradually over to an ever more complete diurnal life, so that it then avoids only direct sun; this re-accustoming, to be sure, again proceeds with the salamander especially slowly, with the toads compared above, for example, much more quickly — also, as with all psychic reactions, becoming-tame and the like, very strong individual differences are to be noticed.

As the most radical expedient, the complete leaving-out of hiding-means would be to be taken into consideration. K. v. FRISCH chose this method and, since he worked with quite young animals, had success. Older ones, however, soon perish if one does not at least offer them a small moss-nest framed by stones, in order to prevent the dragging-about of the moss-twigs over the rest of the floor-surface; I did not succeed, in the long run, in keeping healthy and — what was indeed here the most important — breeding-capable specimens, when I made it impossible for them to creep into moss. For this reason I refrained from it also with the small specimens, which do not make so much of it: I granted them hiding-places, firstly for the sake of the uniformity of the conditions, secondly in order not to deprive myself of the judgment as to in which stage the colour-transformation proceeds most rapidly. This happens, as is to be expected, in the youthful stages, but if I expose the latter more strongly to the light than half-grown and grown ones, then one does not know whether it is to be ascribed to the more constant action of the factor or to the age. For that reason I refrained from an eventual acceleration of the process through more permanent illumination, and granted old and young the stone-framed moss-nests.

In order nonetheless to compel the animals to linger as much as possible upon the coloured underlay, I moved the water-basin and the feeding-bowl into opposite corners of the terrarium, so that between them and the moss-nest a longer stretch of the ground lay bare. I could indeed then also observe that this path is traversed daily by all the specimens; and inasmuch as they, according to their habit, do not exactly hurry very much over it and on the way often remain lying for hours, there was thereby attained the **highest measure of light-exposition** that could be carried through without danger to the well-being of the animals.

This well-being — I cannot say it often enough — is, beside sufficient illumination, simply the most important and the sole precondition for the expected colour-reactions actually to set in: a sick animal shows them either not at all or in a quite different course. It requires a practised eye to recognize an animal as sick: under-nourishment, weak digestive disturbances and skin-affections occasion at times so slow a decline that death sets in only after many months. Already in the case of a lean animal, which is not at all yet to be called sick in the pathological sense, the metabolism is naturally diverted into quite other channels than those in which displacements of the colour-dress-pattern could be accomplished. Or, if they were accomplished, they find, as soon as the under-nourishment continues, their premature conclusion. Without the organism of the experimental animal functioning completely normally during the period required for the course of the experiment, nothing certain is thus to be achieved — to maintain the organism in this correctly functioning state, therein consists the difficulty and the art of the long-term experiments to be described, whose arrangement is otherwise simple, whose outcome is almost to be called schematic.

The special technical arrangements of the individual experimental series shall be given only further on, where the discussion is of these themselves.

## III. The normal colour-dress of *Salamandra maculosa*, its development and maintenance.

### 1. The kinds of pigment and their ontogenesis.

What, in the case of so variable an animal as the fire-salamander — of which surely no two specimens exist that are quite identically marked — is to be regarded as "normal" can naturally only be a matter of an arbitrarily defining delimitation. By "normal colour-dress" I wish to understand, firstly, that which we encounter in the free-living animals in their natural state; secondly, that which has not altered itself in captive life, that is, which remained equal to that at the observation-beginning, as we had encountered it at the natural places of abode. Consequently, in this chapter the conditions will also have to be discussed under which we are able to attain that constancy of the original distribution of ground-colour and marking; finally, the possibilities will have to be discussed of inferring, from the physical conditions of the free life, something regarding their effectiveness upon the colour-pattern.

The fire-salamander appears especially suited for experiments on morphological colour-change, i.e. on increase, diminution, new-formation and alteration of pigments, in so far as

> Archiv f. Entwicklungsmechanik. XXXVI.   2 The black pigment, a melanin, appears in healthy, vigorous specimens at all non-yellow body sites in densely crowded quantity and, in the *forma typica*, most predominantly as the ground colour.

The yellow pigment appears in several shades as chrome-, orange-, straw-, sulphur-yellow and in the form of points, flecks, flourishes [Schnörkeln] and stripes, either in unsymmetrical or in bilateral-symmetrical arrangement. Specimens with symmetrical marking — no matter whether they are longitudinal rows of flecks, two or four stripes — E. G. Boulenger groups together under the name var. *taeniata*, whereas Dürigen designates under this name only the two-banded specimens, denoting the four-banded ones separately with var. *quadrivirgata*. The more or less unsymmetrically marked specimens E. G. Boulenger calls *forma typica* and is inclined to reckon to it the Spanish var. *gallaica* Seoane, which is said to be distinguished from the former only by still more intense black and the small number of the yellow flecks, and likewise the varieties *corsica* Savi and *algira* Bedr. (As Poche sets out, *Salamandra maculosa* ought, in nomenclatural correctness, to be called *Salamandra salamandra*, but its *forma typica* in turn *Salamandra salamandra salamandra*! But who can go along with that?!) — In old, unprinted manuscripts from my Gymnasium years the symmetrically marked form is designated, without knowledge of the literature, as »var. *vittata*«, incidentally a proof to me that Boulenger's reproach (p. 324, 341) — that the authors had not distinguished this form and had in general laid too little value on the distribution and style of the marking — did not even then apply to my person; nor need Dürigen feel touched by it. Later, indeed (Kammerer 1901), I expressed myself, in supplement to a communication by Schnee concerning the colour-dress of the fire-salamander, to the effect that a separate naming of the striped form, despite its conspicuousness, is not admissible because of the continuous transitions from striped to spotted specimens.

As Leydig has found, the yellow of the metamorphosed animal does not arise out of the yellowish, iridescent pigment-corpuscles present in the larva, but entirely on its own. Yet the definitive yellow (and likewise the melanin) is already present in older larvae; the first-appearing flecks, at first quite pale-yellow, appear on the ear-glands (parotids), the eyelids and the insertions of the limbs; the latter can be recognized even in very small larvae and are by this most easily distinguished from triton larvae. According to Gadow there are in the skin thick deposits of lime salts: »where these appear, the black pigment is displaced and only on these melanin-free sites does the yellow, possibly orange or red colour appear«. At all events this process is not to be conceived as though black and yellow pigment unconditionally and immediately excluded one another, as though the former could not exist at least for some time where the latter establishes itself; the two colouring matters also occur mixed and then produce a peculiar dark olive-green interference colour, which one would, without microscopic examination, be inclined to take for a pigment of its own. Although these dull-green flecks, mostly round but washed-out in their contours, are ultimately to be regarded as preliminary stages of sharply set-off yellow flecks, they nevertheless often persist for a considerable time, under some circumstances years. In very melanin-rich, freshly transformed salamanders all the flecks can often still show that tone, but here pass over quickly into a luminous yellow; in older specimens they are most frequently present on the belly side and remain standing very long at their intermediate stage, because there the genesis of yellow sets in latest, takes longest, but also continues on into the highest age.

The greenish interference colour arises only when yellow and black lie layer-wise one over the other, usually yellow deeper than black: the former migrates upward, spreads out in the most superficial layer and there displaces the black. More rarely it can be the reverse, with yellow occupying the high layer from the outset and being replaced by black migrating upward. — Not the greenish, but a dirty, dull-yellow colour arises when the two kinds of pigment maintain themselves beside, instead of one over, the other: and indeed not in that usual juxtaposition such as is always the case at the boundary of a yellow fleck and the black ground colour, but in the special manner that a yellow blotch is, quite at the surface, interspersed with little melanin-islets. Viewed with the naked eye, there then appears such a blotch, as mentioned, in impure yellow (with greyish, not greenish tone), and under the magnifying lens, finely and densely dotted black. On the panel-figures I have therefore brought out the difference between over- and beside-, perhaps better, intermingled-layering in a coarser manner by this, that the fleck in the former case was lightly brushed over with black India ink (esp. Pl. II, III, V, IX, XII), but in the latter case was dotted as small as possible with the drawing-pen (Pl. IV—IX, XIII). Whereas the over-the-other mostly ends with the victory of the yellow — thus, as said, the olive-green colour as a rule represents a preliminary stage of the glaring-yellow — the beside-the-other almost always signifies a victory of the black: the yellow fleck is more and more eaten away, the dark little dots accumulate, flow together into larger black piles, and from the edges too invasions of melanin come to meet them, until this became sole ruler over the whole surface. It is an exception and can come about only through special factors when melanin that has arisen within a yellow blotch itself again passes into disappearance and the darkened blotch then again shines forth in original purity.

There are thus met, then, manifold colour-nuances — pure yellow in its diverse shadings, deep-black, dull-yellow, olive-green — solely out of the two colouring matters described up to now, of which the colour-dress of the salamander in general consists exclusively; yet there are in some specimens still one or two other, really self-standing kinds of pigment.

1) A red pigment, which is present in the most varied shadings (cinnabar-, brick-, blood-, purple- and wine-red), especially on the ear-glands, on the brow and crown, in the surroundings of the eyes, at the corners of the mouth and at the throat, but occasionally also over the whole rest of the body. Exactly like the yellow, it can either be overlaid by the black and then appear dully purple, or be interspersed by it and then appear grey- or brown-red. But also in the midst of the yellow pigment, with which it is evidently closely related according to form and emergence of the chromatophores, it appears and forms in the midst of the yellow blotches red islands and marble-markings. A quite rare colour-variety, which has been named var. *coccinea* by Schweizerbarth, which in its geographical distribution is not bound to particular regions but occurs sporadically everywhere among the normal ones, possesses even only red flecks, which according to shape and distribution take the place of the yellow ones entirely, that is, are present precisely there where one otherwise expects to see yellow flecks.

2) A yellowish-white pigment, which appears in the form of very small flecks especially on the flanks and the underside, and indeed almost always in larger heaps together. It is to be emphasized as very characteristic for this pigment that, at least in *Salamandra*, it always occurs only in quite small accumulations, in dots of at most 2 sq. mm of surface-extent. It seems to be the same pigment that, in very many, namely primordial urodele genera (*Siren, Proteus, Spelerpes, Plethodon, Desmognathus, Amblystoma*) and indeed usually likewise only in point- or speckle-form interrupts the black ground colour, and it thus seems to represent a primeval character of the tailed amphibians. Only in isolated species, such as the artificially illuminated *Proteus*, as well as in *Amblystoma opacum* Bd. and *tigrinum* Green. (= *mavortium* Baird), does the now-discussed pigment take on the shape of larger flecks and bands, whereas in still very nearly related species, thus in *Amblystoma punctatum* Bd. and *mexicanum* Cope (= *tigrinum* Laur.), it keeps to its rule — strictly observed also in *Salamandra* and the remaining genera enumerated above — of not exceeding about 2 sq. mm of surface-space.

It has befallen me and others to confuse the previously described red pigment with pigmentless spots, which then appear red in consequence of the capillary vessels shining through. The decision about this is naturally easily made; one need only put on a good magnifying lens; but already to the unarmed eye two points of reference present themselves for correctly recognizing the red coloration caused by lack of pigment. Firstly, the latter expresses itself not in so saturated a red, but a paler pink; secondly, it is usually accompanied, in less blood-rich skin regions, by spots which, in consequence of the looseness of the melanin, are grey or brown instead of black. Both apply to the Portuguese var. *molleri* De Bedr., whose investigation brought E. G. Boulenger (p. 336) to the conviction that the grey and red drawing here is owing to the small quantity, respectively the absence, of pigment. The conclusion that the latter always holds true for red drawings is inappropriate, for there does in fact exist a red pigment of its own.

In the present experimental investigation we shall occupy ourselves almost exclusively with the two principal pigments of the fire-salamander, the black and the yellow, and also pay attention to the various degrees of intensity of the latter only seldom and occasionally, since the conditions of their appearance have for the most part remained unclarified. Of the relations communicated in this respect in 1904 I have, in my later experiments, never again been quite able rightly to convince myself.

### 2. The Retention of the Normal Colour-Dress in the Control Experiments.

It is now a matter of preserving the »normal« colour-dress of the fire-salamander in its original state, more precisely said, of restricting it to that developmental possibility which by nature inhered in it. This happens by our keeping the animals away from all factors which are apt to accelerate the natural developmental direction abnormally or even to switch it over: moisture- and temperature-extremes, as well as surrounding colours which agree, at least approximately, with those of the animals themselves. The measures to be followed in this are incidentally identical with those which aim in general at as normal, nature-conformable a keeping as possible, and were for the most part already discussed in the preceding chapter (II. Technique). Here let it be supplemented that the temperature, in the warm season, should not often exceed 20 degrees C., and in the cold may fall so far that a winter sleep, though not so long and uninterrupted as in the open, comes about, thus temporarily down to near the freezing point. As regards the air- and ground-moisture, it suffices that it does not constantly reach near 80% and does not sink below 60%.

Finally, as concerns the surrounding colour, every colour except black and yellow is, in our sense, to be regarded as neutral. I kept salamanders on all conceivable underlays: in containers whose floors, walls and sometimes also ceilings — in the form of slanting screens which permit the overhead light entry, but reflect it back in the colour concerned — were laid out with paper in all spectral colours and mixed colours: either only a single colour in each container, or one and the same cage provided mosaic- or star-wise with all the colours — besides red, green, violet, blue also grey, brown and white. The latter, as well as very light, almost white river-sand, does indeed have a bleaching effect on the saturation of the yellow flecks (cf. p. 80), but none on their size and sharpness of boundary. Even the otherwise so effective colours yellow and black need not be avoided, if out of them one either produces so uniform a mixture — to be ascertained empirically — that promoting and inhibiting influences hold the balance to each other, or covers them completely with moss and other vegetation, so that nowhere does light fall upon them. As is to be expected according to these findings, a substrate of coloured, wet brook-pebbles — very agreeable and hygienic to the animals — likewise serves for the conservation of the original colour-state. But there is yet a simpler means to this end: all colours had after all been applied for experiment's sake too, for example in order to decide whether in the observed colour-shifts we have before us actual genuine colour-effects or only effects of the degree of brightness; and since really only the animals' own colours, when they appear in their surroundings, release changes, this already speaks sufficiently against a non-specific intensity-effect. But yet a second fact, which we already know now, speaks against it: namely the fact that we, in every case where we wish to attain colour-changes, require the highest light-intensities, be it on yellow for the increase of the drawing, be it on black for the spreading of the ground colour. And precisely out of this there finally results of itself the simplest means for excluding every specific change both on yellow and on black: for this it is only necessary to place the container in the dark, not even necessarily in the dark chamber completely shut off from light — which after all corresponds no longer to »normal« relations, but already again to special experimental conditions — but simply in a half-dark room accessible only to lateral light. It will depend on the particular constitution of the respective experimental series how one wishes to furnish the control experiment belonging to it, which reproduces the normal relations: either with the same light-conditions as prevail in the actual experiment, and then naturally with floors other than yellow and black; or with the same substrates as in the experiment itself, but then these must receive less light or be covered. I have made abundant use of all these possibilities and beg my referees and reviewers, in their kind objections, not again to forget completely these control experiments, which are missing in the »Summary« at the end.

If one brings older, fully grown salamanders into any one of these control containers, one will, even after years, perceive no kind of changes in the distribution of the yellow and black.

Young salamanders which have not yet completed the natural development of their colour-pattern will, on the other hand, naturally show certain, relatively trifling changes, but these scatter apart irregularly — unless the constitution of the place of finding lets a particular direction continue to act: some tend toward expansion, others toward restriction of the yellow drawing; in numerous others the one fleck enlarges, while a neighbouring one diminishes, possibly almost disappears, while elsewhere new flecks emerge. But all this so slowly that in salamanders which have only a few months after metamorphosis behind them, actually no change strikes the eye, and without drawing, periodic control the slight changes would surely have remained unnoticed. The characteristic of all these control-cultures consists, then — again apart from a one-sidedly inducing origin of the material — in this, that there are finally found in each of them specimens with much and with little yellow, without either the much or the little overstepping a very moderate variation-breadth.

### 3. On the Merely Hypothetical Value of Field-Observations.

That is, however, quite the same picture which meets us at average finding-places in the open as well: much- and little-spotted salamanders, those with large and small, saturated- and pale-yellow flecks, with and without red flecks and yellowish-white speckles, live colourfully intermingled in one and the same forest-area. Whoever does not yet know the laws of action of the life-conditions — and this holds for many contemporary biologists — would easily draw from this the conclusion that all those factors are independent of external factors. The same conclusion he would draw if, at localities of apparently very different physical conditions, he encounters animals of the same colour-type; thus E. G. Boulenger says: »Specimens from hilly regions of Belgium, from most French finding-places, both at the sea-shore and at considerable height (Pyrenees), agree completely in the general style of the flecks and of the colours and thus show that the climate has no influence on this character¹«. In order

> ¹ In the English original printed spaced [emphasized]: ». . . thus showing that climate has no bearing on this character«.

to characterize the precipitateness of such an inference, one need merely point to the complexity of the milieu-conditions, further to the countless possibilities of milieu-change, to which the animals themselves are able continually to expose themselves. One and the same wood, indeed one and the same thicket, by no means represents uniform conditions, but rather comprises factors which seek to shift the relation of coloration and drawing partly in a positive, partly in a negative respect, in consequence of which they hinder one another where they act simultaneously, bringing forth opposite effects in their salamander-population when the one party remains exposed to the positive, the other to the negative. Suppose a salamander-population finds itself on clay soil, but several specimens choose decaying tree-stumps for their abode, which, in consequence of continuously dry weather, they do not leave for months; the rest have crept away into earth-holes and must there be content with a smaller supply of moisture, which compels them to bathe every morning in the dew and to expose themselves to the light reflected from the yellow surroundings: thus this difference of abode, which actually often occurs, would alone already suffice to rear small-flecked specimens in the stumps, large-flecked ones in the earth-caves. Yet another part of the same population has settled in the vicinity of the brook-edge, wades daily through shallow puddles and exposes its whole skin to the water; from the neighbouring hillock the animals do not make this journey, they are dependent on dew and rain, therefore keep themselves on the whole more concealed, and thereby have — apart from the drier milieu — less opportunity to make prey, etc. If we leave aside the attendant fluctuations of nutrition and illumination, then the moisture-difference alone would already cause to arise there numerous roundish, light ocelli, here larger, irregularly bounded, but, through contamination with melanin, dull-coloured flecks.

If we now further add that all these inductions unfold an after-effect, so that a salamander migrating from the hillock to the brook-edge, from the tree-stump into clay-holes, does not at once lose the characters of its first dwelling-place, indeed under some circumstances even bequeaths them to its descendants, then there results an endless series of mixings and differentiations. In freedom a particular factor must already appear very dominantly, must very uniformly and constantly overbid the remaining factors in energy, in order to impress a uniform type upon the colourful race-mixture — Young salamanders, which have not yet completed the natural development of their colour pattern, will by contrast naturally show certain, relatively trifling alterations; but these — in so far as the constitution of the find-place allows a definite direction to take effect afterwards — proceed irregularly with respect to one another: some incline to the expansion, others to the restriction of the yellow drawing; in numerous others one spot enlarges, while a neighbouring one becomes smaller, in some cases almost entirely vanishes, while elsewhere new spots appear. All of this, however, so slowly that in salamanders which have only a few months of post-metamorphic life behind them, the periodic control of the colour-changes would surely have remained unnoticed. The characteristic feature of all these control cultures consists therefore — apart again from a one-sidedly inducing origin of the material — in this, that in each of them there are in the end exemplars with much and with little yellow, without either the much or the little overstepping a steadily narrowing variation-breadth.

### 3. On the merely hypothetical value of field-observations.

That, however, is quite the same picture which presents itself to us in the cross-sectional findings even out in the open: small and large, salt- and pale-yellow flecked salamanders, such with large red flecks and yellowish-white sprinkled, lie mottledly intermingled in one and the same forest-region. For the operative law of the living being one might easily derive from this — and this holds for very many contemporary biologists too — that climatic conditions are without influence; one looks, however, at a more southern latitude, and almost imperceptibly at unfavourable factors. With the same physical conditions, however, certain other factors play in; thus said E. G. Boulenger: »Exemplars from hilly regions of Belgium, from most French find-places, both at the sea-shore and at considerable altitude (Pyrenees), agree on the whole completely in the style of the flecks and of the colours, and so show that the climate has no influence upon this character¹). To

> ¹) In the English original not printed spaced: ›... thus showing that climate has no bearing on this character‹.

characterize the over-hastiness of such a conclusion, it needs merely the pointing-to of the complexity of the milieu-conditions, further to the countless possibilities of the milieu-change, to which the animals themselves continually seek to adapt — the same wood, indeed the very same thicket, by no means presents uniform conditions, but rather encompasses factors which strive to differentiate the relationship of colouring and drawing partly in a positive, partly in a negative respect, consequently hindering one another where they occur simultaneously, bringing forth opposite effects upon their salamander-population when the one party strives toward the positive, the other toward the negative effect. Suppose a salamander-population finds itself on loam-soil, but several exemplars choose rotten tree-stumps as their place of sojourn, which, in consequence of persistently dry weather, they do not leave for months; the others have crept into earth-holes and must there content themselves with a smaller supply of moisture, which compels them every morning to bathe in the dew and to expose themselves to the light reflected from the yellow surroundings: thus this difference of sojourn, which in fact often occurs, would alone already suffice to rear small-flecked exemplars in the tree-stumps, large-flecked ones in the earth-holes. Yet another part of the same population has settled in the vicinity of the brook-bank, wades daily through shallow puddles and exposes the whole skin to the water; the animals do not make this way to the neighbouring hill, they are referred to dew and rain, keep themselves therefore generally more concealed, and thereby — apart from the dryer milieu — have less occasion to deposit residues, etc. If we leave aside the accompanying nourishment- and illumination-fluctuations, then the moisture-difference there would alone already let arise numerous roundish, light ocelli, in their larger ones irregularly bounded, but through union with melanin dusky-coloured, flecks.

It does not yet follow from this that all these inductions unfold an after-effect, so that a salamander migrating from the hill to the brook-bank, from the tree-stump into loam-holes, does not at once lose the characters of its new dwelling-place, indeed under certain circumstances still bequeaths them to its descendants; thus there results an endless series of mixings and differentiations. In freedom a definite factor must already appear very dominantly, very uniformly and constantly outbid the remaining factors in energy, in order to impress upon the motley race-mixture a unitary type; this will be more possible in desert-, water-, high-mountain-animals than here in the forest-dwellers, where eo ipso the diversity of the milieu is greater than mostly elsewhere.

From these discussions it follows without compulsion why not merely, as hitherto explained, the dwellers of one locality can be unequal, but also dwellers of strongly different and distant localities can be equal. This is quite apart from migrations, which in salamanders cannot assume any too great extent. But suppose the case that a southerly warm but dry region harbours a similarly large-flecked colour-type as a northerly damp one with rougher climate: is then the factor — moisture — as effective upon fleck-increase, after E. G. Boulenger, led ad absurdum? By no means, for the same effect which here was to be ascribed to the wetness might there have been brought about by the warmth, etc., etc.

And now let one picture to oneself how the prospects are constituted of an undertaking that wishes to judge the connection of salamander-colour and environment out in the open! How that dry hill, that damp shore in our fictive examples used just now would act, I know not merely from experiments, where in captivity I had isolated the factors in question; to recognize such things in nature I hold today quite simply as impossible; it is a hundred to one to wager that one thereby goes astray. Why this must be so, I set forth more fully in my writing on the effects of the external life-conditions [Kammerer 1910c]; field-observations can therefore (and this holds generally, not, say, only of the salamander-dress) at most claim the value of working-hypotheses. Hardly has anyone hitherto fared better with the correlations I established in 1904 by the path of variation-statistics (at least causality!) between salamander-colour on the one hand, soil- and rock-constitution, temperature and moisture on the other hand. They have been partly confirmed, partly refuted by the experiments to be presented in the present treatise; but the decision concerning their correctness could be brought about exclusively through analytical experiments, as E. G. Boulenger has done.

In the light of my experimental experiences since then, his objections present themselves as follows (the individual points correspond to those in E. G. Boulenger, p. 344 ff., and to those of my work 1904, p. 234):

1) My hypothesis that warm climate supports the drawing and its saturation, that cold suppresses it, is to be corrected to this, that the temperature always merely strengthens the developmental direction immanent from the outset to the exemplar in question, but takes no specific influence. The temperature is, in Roux's sense, an activating, not a determining factor. If the exemplar had the tendency to increase its drawing, then it becomes yellow more rapidly and more completely in high temperature than in low. If it had the tendency to restrict its drawing, then it remains within a low temperature still longer and more pronouncedly yellow than in high [cf. p. 94]. Thereby many contradictions fall away, also such as are enumerated in later points.

2) My supposition that regions with strong air- and soil-moisture, many watercourses, favour the number, size and saturation of the flecks, whereas dryness and water-poverty make them recede, is to be made precise thus: that in wetness at first only the number of the flecks is increased, their size grows more pronouncedly, or only through the flowing-together of several dots arising close together; dryness darkens the flecks to the point of complete disappearance, thereby gradually reduces their number, but lets their size, so long as they remain at all visible, persist almost as at the beginning. If E. G. Boulenger here objects that salamanders are at all conceivable only at localities of the first-named constitution, then he at least does not know the Dalmatian find-places, where at times (for example on the island of Bua) salamanders appear positively in stony deserts, without one's comprehending how they attain the moisture indispensable to their skin. That South-French, South-Italian and Asia-Minor animals are often yellower than small-flecked ones from damper localities of Austria-Hungary and Romania, can find its explanation in the subsoil and the temperature. One is at first inclined to hold exemplars with small flecks for poorer in yellow than such with larger, conspicuous flecks, which is by no means always the case, as E. G. Boulenger himself, correcting me, emphasizes (p. 341). Precisely for moisture-animals it is characteristic that their flecks remain small, but, because they increase strongly in number, nevertheless finally help their bearer to a considerable richness in yellow.

3) With rock-sorts I have conducted no experiments. With regard to the limestone-mountains there lies before us a confirming opportunity-observation on the part of v. Schweizerbarth (1909). In any case nothing is thereby proved when salamanders of various rock-sorts are equally coloured: Boulenger's sentence, gained on the ground of a longer enumeration, »this shows clearly that the geological subsoil, at least in France, has nothing to do with the colouring of the salamander«, settles itself, before the setting-up of the experiments, through my above more general expositions as unwarranted.

4) The relation between yellow and dark colour of the soil on the one hand, yellow drawing and black ground-colour of the salamander on the other hand, has shown itself experimentally as accurate, in so far as it concerns the quantity-ratio of the two pigments. The irregularities occurring in the open in consequence of the heterogeneous concurrence of the factors were by me in 1904 — as, according to a wording cited also by E. G. Boulenger, one ought to have expected — clearly enough set apart: when the Belgian salamanders twice brought forward by him appear richly drawn on dark soil and in cold climate, then it may not therefrom be concluded that these two factors must summate themselves in the removal of the drawing, but rather that there a third, still mightier factor outbids both factors hindering it: probably the moisture.

G. E. Boulenger finally plays out his last trump, in that »contrary to Kammerer's results, our blackest specimen of the typical form is from a hot locality (Ticino) at an altitude of only about 600 metres, our yellowest from the highest altitude on record, 1600 metres (Calabria). It might be objected that in the case of the latter the altitude is compensated by the latitude, but that will not answer, since at a more southern latitude, and almost at the sea-level, on the coast of Africa, paucity of yellow markings is the rule. In the var. taeniata, a totally black specimen has been recorded from the Siebengebirge (Rhenish Prussia) whilst the opposite extreme of almost wholly yellow specimens is on record from as distant and dissimilar localities as the Harz, Bilbao, Toulouse, and (?) Rome«. All these finds agree indeed excellently with my pre-suppositions and above all with the results of my experiments; we need only assume that the heat of the Ticino find-place worked reaction-accelerating upon a pre-existing tendency to the increase of the melanin, the warm climate of Calabria, however, mitigatingly upon the great sea-altitude of the second find-place — Boulenger himself takes this into consideration, rejects it again, however, because animals living at the coastal-altitude of North Africa as a rule bear few flecks; with the dryness prevailing there this is extremely plausible! To the same lawfulnesses the »var. taeniata« is just as subjected as the »forma typica«.

Possibly the paradoxes held out against G. E. Boulenger resolve themselves so, possibly also otherwise — but certainly not in the sense intended by him, that all those external influences have no validity. Not in the expectation that something positive would thereby come out have I carried through the whole disputation; but rather in order to show that nothing thereby comes out, so long as one does not take better methods to aid. On the ground of mere nature-observations one can indeed turn things as one will, can deny one and the same thing and find it confirmed; this holds of the depiction just given by me, in so far as it does not already anticipate experimental-results, quite as much as of that of Boulenger: and therefore it is time that we turn ourselves to the actual decisions.

## IV. Induction- and inheritance-experiments.

### A. Loam-earth.

#### 1. The starting-material.

The experiment with the two earth-sorts, yellow loam-earth and black garden-earth, is the one upon which I have expounded myself most fully in the preliminary communications (Kammerer 1904, 1908/9, 1909, 1910a–d, 1910/11, 1911a–c, 1911/12), and which also has up to now been most frequently discussed by others (Baur, Bendl, Boulenger, v. Fejérváry, F. M., Goldschmidt 1911, v. Hanstein, Secerov 1912a, c, Semon 1910, 1912, Thilo etc.).

As starting-material serve Salamandra maculosa [Salamandra salamandra] of the Vienna Woods, mostly collected in the beech-woods of Tullnerbach and Purkersdorf (on the Western-railway-line). The fleck-drawing is here throughout a quite irregular, unsymmetrical one; as a great rarity it must be designated when several flecks lie more or less in one or two rows; yet this is always very far removed from one's being able to speak of a bilateral arrangement of the drawing-elements. It is self-evident that the flecks on eyelids, parotids and limb-bases form herefrom an exception: these former, becoming distinct at the metamorphosis, are indeed always, in all the colour-varieties of the fire-salamander, symmetrically arranged.

Not from the loam-experiment, but also for most of the other experiments, the irregularly flecked salamanders of the Vienna Woods, and indeed of the nearest surroundings of Vienna, have served as origin; only where it is more the case will therefore, in the further course of the depiction, the origin of the material be specially noted.

If we grasp the irregularity of the flecking as a »character«, as an element-property or hereditary-unit — and indeed again as one tearing sufficiently in the cross-direction, in order to show that here altogether a hereditary tendency is present —, then we may here take the material in this regard as fully equal-arting. From such »equal-artingness« there follows of course the greatest unequal-artingness in respect of the quantity and special distribution of the drawing-elements. It is impossible to find two exemplars which look alike to each other. On the contrary one finds, as was already emphasized in the previous chapter [III. The normal colour-dress], very strongly and very weakly flecked exemplars intermingled at one and the same find-place. It is therefore impossible and, as the crossings show, unnecessary, to begin the experiment, say, with animals which externally look very similar to one another.

#### 2. The Protocolling.

In one regard the previously-mentioned circumstance is even to be designated as favourable: namely as far as control and protocolling are concerned. Were all exemplars confusably similar, then one would have to tend each one separately in a special container, which would be approximately equivalent to the unfeasibility of the whole investigation in its present extent — at least if European means be taken into consideration. So, however, one can keep a protocol for each single one of them and need not thereby fear that one would not, with ease and certainty, recognize each one again out of the troop of its some 40 cage-companions. Since the changes proceed very slowly, no doubt is possible even after a half-yearly or year-long pause in the control. In order to exclude this measure of subjective judgement, which we must indeed safeguard for ourselves — our scepticism shall not lead to unfruitfulness —, I nevertheless reared a number of animals in special containers on variously coloured soils in isolation and had thereby the opportunity to confirm in an objective manner on them the regularities observed in the mass-culture. Of these isolations there will yet be discussion: they later serve the crossing-experiments.

The protocolling was carried out in all the experiments in such a way that I produced for myself (cf. the plates II—XIII) sheets with schematic outlines of salamanders in ascending size, seen from above, below and the side; into the outlines the fleck-drawing is entered as soon as the animal in question has reached the size which is pre-drawn by the respectively following outline. Since it appeared desirable to undertake the control within regular time-intervals, slight millimetre-differences in consequence of the resulting growth-irregularities were neglected, that is to say, apart from stronger deviations, it was as a rule assumed that the animals increase in size approximately equally over equal times. In the last experimental-year (1912) we had diagrams drawn by Herr Przibram multiplied, into which the content of those graphic protocols was entered. In order to accommodate three rows of outlines (upper side, under side, flanks) on one plate-sheet, it was necessary to fill out not quite so many belly-side and flank-views as upper-side ones; thereby no loss arises, since the changes of the upper side, at least where light-effects are concerned, proceed by far the most rapidly and most thoroughly.

Perhaps I should have decided, instead of the simple outlines, which no longer divide the respectively presented surface of the salamander-body into narrower districts, to introduce E. G. Boulenger's diagrams (p. 325, fig. 99) into my protocols, since it is in fact often difficult to render the spots on the whole free surface correctly in their actual position, size and relation to one another. Through division of the surface into several longitudinal- and transverse-regions the entering-in is certainly facilitated. Meanwhile my protocols, at the appearance of Boulenger's work (1911), were of course already so far advanced that the following of another system could now hardly appear advisable; with the glandular-rows between, which Boulenger uses for the division of the longitudinal-regions, I had in any case still oriented myself, and under five fictive transverse-lines through neck-constriction, trunk-half, tail-root, the middle between neck-constriction and Possibly the paradoxes held up against me by G. E. Boulenger are resolved in this way, possibly also otherwise — but certainly **not** in the manner intended by him, namely that all those external influences have no validity. **Not** in the expectation that something positive would come of it have I carried through this whole discussion; but rather in order to show that nothing comes of it so long as one does not call better methods to one's aid. On the ground of mere observations of nature one can after all turn things as one will, can deny one and the same thing and find it confirmed; this holds of the presentation I have just given, insofar as it does not already anticipate experimental results, quite as much as of that of Boulenger: and therefore it is time that we turn to the actual decisions.

## IV. Induction and Inheritance Experiments.

### A. Loam Soil.

#### 1. The Starting Material.

The experiment with the two kinds of soil, yellow loam and black garden soil, is the one upon which I have spread myself most fully in the preliminary communications (Kammerer 1904, 1908/9, 1909, 1910a—d, 1910/11, 1911a—c, 1911/12), and which also (Baur, Bendl, Boulenger, v. Fejérváry, F. M., Goldschmidt 1911, v. Hanstein, Secerov 1912a, c, Semon 1910, 1912, Thilo, etc.) has up to now been the most frequently discussed.

As starting material there serve *Salamandra maculosa* [*Salamandra salamandra*] of the Vienna Woods, gathered mostly in the beech forests of Tullnerbach and Purkersdorf (on the Western railway line). The spot-pattern is here throughout a quite irregular, asymmetrical one; it must be designated a great rarity when several spots lie to some degree in one or two rows; yet this is always very far removed from one's being able to speak of a bilateral arrangement of the pattern-elements. Self-evidently the spots on the eyelids, parotids and limb-bases form from this an exception: these first spots, which become distinct at metamorphosis, are after all always, in all the colour-variations of the fire-salamander, symmetrically situated.

Not only to the loam-soil experiment, but also to most of the other experiments, the irregularly spotted salamanders of the Vienna Woods, and indeed of the nearest surroundings of Vienna, have served as origin; only where this is not the case will the provenance of the material therefore be specially noted in the further course of the presentation.

If we conceive the irregularity of the spotting as a »character«, as an elementary property or hereditary unit — and our crossing experiments to be discussed later show that there is every justification for this —, then we may accordingly regard the material in this respect as entirely homogeneous. From such »homogeneity« there follows, to be sure, the greatest heterogeneity with respect to the quantity and special distribution of the pattern-elements. It is impossible to find even merely two specimens that look alike. On the contrary one finds, as was already emphasized in the previous chapter (III. The Normal Coloration), very strongly and very weakly spotted specimens jumbled together at one and the same locality. It is therefore impossible and, as the crossings show, unnecessary to begin the experiment, say, with animals that look outwardly very similar to one another.

#### 2. The Keeping of Records.

In one respect the last-mentioned circumstance is even to be designated as favourable: namely as regards control and the keeping of records. Were all specimens confusingly similar, then one would have to tend each one by itself in a special container, which would be approximately equivalent to the impracticability of the whole investigation in its present extent — at least considering European means. As it is, however, one can indeed keep a special record for each individual specimen and need not thereby fear that one would not with ease and certainty recognize this same specimen again out of the troop of its some 40 cage-companions. Since the changes proceed very slowly, no doubt is after all possible even after a half- or whole-year's pause in the control. In order to eliminate this measure too of subjective judgment — against which we must surely guard ourselves, so that our scepticism shall not lead to barrenness — I nevertheless reared a number of animals in small containers on the variously coloured soils in isolation, and thus had the opportunity of confirming on them, in an objective manner, the regularities observed in the mass-culture. Of these isolated animals there will be still further mention: they later served the crossing experiments.

The keeping of records, now, is carried out in all the experiments in such a way that I had prepared for myself (cf. Plates II—XIII) sheets with schematic outlines of salamanders in ascending size, seen from above, from below, and from the side; into the outlines the spot-pattern is entered as soon as the animal in question has reached the size that is prefigured by the respectively following outline. But since, already for reasons of energy-economy, it appeared desirable to undertake the control within regular time-intervals, slight millimetre-differences as a consequence of the growth-inequalities that arise were neglected, i.e. then, apart from stronger deviations, it was as a rule assumed that the animals increase in size approximately equally in equal times. In the last experimental year (1912) we had diagrams drawn by Herr Przibram reproduced in multiple copies, into which the content of those graphic records was entered. In order to accommodate three rows of outlines (upper side, under side, flanks) on one table-sheet, it was necessary not to fill in quite so many belly-sides and flank-views as upper sides; from this no loss arises, since the changes of the upper side, at least where it is a matter of light-effects, proceed by far the most rapidly and most thoroughly.

Perhaps I might have decided, instead of the simple outlines, which no longer divide the respectively offered surface of the salamander-body into narrower districts, to introduce the E. G. Boulenger diagrams (S. 325, Fig. 99) into my records, since it is in fact often difficult to render correctly the markings over the whole free surface in their actual position, size, and posture relative to one another. By the division of the surface into several longitudinal and transverse regions the entering-in will certainly turn out easier and more exact. Meanwhile my records were, at the appearance of Boulenger's work (1911), naturally already too far advanced to let the following of another system still appear advisable now; by the three dorsal gland-rows, which Boulenger uses for the partitioning of the longitudinal regions, I had in any case oriented myself, and thus left out of account only the five fictitious transverse lines through neck-constriction, mid-trunk, tail-root, the middle between neck-constriction and mid-trunk, the middle between mid-trunk and tail-root. Decisive for this omission, however, only the following consideration became: the region-division and especially the numbering of the spots is evidently practical only there, where it is a matter of registering preserved museum-material without lengthy description so exactly that one can already gather from the catalogue approximately what the animal looks like. But in the living, changing animal too many transgressions of spots from one region into the other, new formation and fusion of spots occur, which at one stroke overthrow the numbering, whereby the practicality of the surface-subdivision appears for the most part annulled again.

In the first years of the experimental course each individual experimental animal was registered in the manner described, its colour-changes further followed up; when this could later no longer be coped with, owing to the increasing experimental series, only individual specimens within each series were any longer drawn, which had been chosen from the outset arbitrarily and self-evidently without knowledge of whether they would be especially well suited to it, hence also without regard thereto. All the rest were indeed each time controlled along with them, but observations on them that agreed in principle were no longer graphically recorded in the protocols. Only when something unusual, possibly interesting, showed itself on such a specimen was the observation entered into a diagram of fitting size and continued onward from then on.

#### 3. The Course of the Experiment.

##### a. Parent Generation [*P*]. (Plate II.)

The great range of variation of the starting material — much- and little-spotted specimens at one and the same locality — granted me, besides the advantages just depicted for the record-keeping, yet a further favour, namely the excluding of the objection that the hereditary intensification of a colour is to be traced back to unintentional selection. The impossibility of beginning the experiment with approximately equal-looking specimens that stand in the middle with respect to their pattern compelled me to practise counter-selection, negative breeding-choice. For the experiment now under discussion, on yellow soil, I therefore chose animals that had little yellow. Not exactly the very blackest did I seek out, but yet such as would by anyone be designated as predominantly black (Plate II, Fig. 3a—c).

And naturally it was, as was to be foreseen, expedient to begin with young specimens. For the stem-experiment I did indeed not take quite freshly transformed animals — these are very seldom to be found in the open, because they live exceedingly hidden, and the rearing of the larvae makes more difficult in this case the selection of a sufficient number of correctly, i.e. little-yellow coloured, animals. At all events I took such animals as could be only about 1 year old, and as equal-sized as possible. Fully grown specimens do indeed also not lack any plasticity whatever in the respect to be depicted, but it goes too slowly for one to be able to expect it, also probably comes prematurely to a standstill, in some earlier, in others later, and the final result after a years-long experimental course then looks very uneven, namely as though only a part of the animals had changed, the remaining part not. But it was desirable to me to show that here no accidentalities are present, but rather that every single healthy specimen kept on yellow soil must unconditionally take part in the changes to be described.
