The use of young animals offers yet the further advantage that they are virginal, the females thus do not, say, already store the sperm of a male whose hereditarily transmitting properties would cross those acquired in the experiment; but rather the females of the experiment can be fertilized only by males that took part in the same changes as they themselves.

The process setting in on all the young animals on yellow soil consists in this, that the already present spots — few in number and size, simple in form — enlarge, send out manifoldly directed and curved processes, and thereby gain an articulated, indented shape. The processes possess the distinct tendency to lengthen and to shift themselves in such a way that they come nearer to corresponding processes of neighbouring spots and finally meet together with them: in this way there arise irregular transverse and longitudinal bands and loops. Between the old ones, which have become thus extensive, there form, however, also isolated, entirely new spots, at first always in point- or droplet-shape, which thereupon enlarge themselves once again with the formation of lobed processes. The manner in which an unarticulated spot sends out lobes recalls, naturally apart from the slowness, strikingly the pseudopodium-formation of an amoeba; and the circumstance that a spot usually sends out an exactly similar process precisely also at the place where a neighbouring spot had already developed such a one, recalls the chemotactic behaviour of the amoebae. Of chemotaxis one cannot indeed speak here, but perhaps of chemotropism, for it is a matter not of a movement-process, but of a growth-process, not of expansion, but of multiplication of the yellow pigment, not of physiological, but of morphological colour-change. To what extent this takes its starting-point from physiological processes shall be told in a later chapter (VII). To the morphological process corresponds the long duration: there elapse after all 3—4 years before one can, under the most favourable circumstances, speak of a somewhat striking, thorough re-colouring of the animal. The process is especially slow on the under side, which holds less for the new formation than for the enlargement of spots; this remarkable doubleness of the phenomenon the reader will find explained in a satisfying manner only on S. 77.

If at the beginning of the experiment, in the specimen in question, preliminary stages of yellow spots in the shape of greenish markings (see on this Kap. III, S. 19) were present, then the melanin lying over it now comes to disappearance, the yellow pigment soon emerges purely in the whole extent of this spot. The size-enrichment of the remaining spots, which were from the outset purely yellow, and their fusion-zones, are either at once melanin-free, or show at first that greenish interference-colour, so that for a longer time one can distinguish the new acquisition from the old pigment-stock.

The time of the relatively most rapid re-colourings is the warm season; in winter the process often stagnates entirely. It is obvious to make responsible for this in the first line the greater light-intensity in summer, since we have indeed heard (S. 14, 15) that strong light is a fundamental condition for the success of the experiment; but also the greater warmth contributes to it, since it accelerates the cell-divisions, as the temperature-experiments to be discussed later (S. 93) teach.

##### b. Child Generation [*F₁*]. (Plates III—VI.)

In order to establish beyond objection whether and to what extent the surplus of yellow colour acquired in the parent generation would be inherited, the offspring had to be divided into groups from two points of view: first according to the conditions under which it was begotten, second according to the conditions under which it was reared.

As to 1, fertilization and birth could take place either under the conditions of the respective experiment (thus in our present case in the container with yellow loam-soil) or under neutral conditions or under such as effect a counter-induction. Or one could let one part of the begetting-phase, whose beginning we take with the fertilization, whose end with the birth, run its course under the experimental conditions, another part under the neutral or opposite conditions. — As to 2, the growing-up of the offspring from birth onward could likewise be accomplished either under experimental or neutral or counter-conditions.

All these ways were actually trodden in most of the conceivable combinations. As regards first again the conditions of the begetting (fertilization up to birth), there resulted as the most frequent procedure that the fertilization took place in the experimental container, but the females recognized as gravid were set in part on gravel (neutral), in part on black soil (counter-induction), where they fully carried out and bore their young. If one awaited a larger mating-drive and then brought, on the off-chance, all the females of the container in question into a container with gravel or black soil, then this naturally meant a transfer of the females already quite at the beginning of the gravidity-period; one indeed thereby risks that a part of the females is not mated, but the rest become gravid after all, and one has withdrawn the whole embryonic development from a possible induction of the embryos by the yellow soil.

In order to do this still including the fertilization-process, I had to make the whole male and female population of a »yellow« experimental terrarium move over, shortly before the rut to be expected, into a »black« or »neutral« one (gravel, river-sand); but since the rut is often disturbed, indeed abolished, by the moving-over, further its foresight is easily disappointed, because (especially in captivity) it does not run off strictly enough periodically, and finally signs of rut already entered upon (chasing and tussling of the animals) do not let already-occurred matings be excluded beyond objection, I proceeded to artificial insemination, which lets itself be carried out reliably and easily under the sacrifice of a male, somewhat more difficultly without this sacrifice. If one kills a male, then the ripest sperm is to be found free in the Siebold sacs in the cloaca as well as in the urinospermatic duct, the next-best in the grey district of the testicle; if one wishes to leave the male alive, then one places it in light ether-narcosis, then exerts on belly (in antero-posterior direction) and cloaca (laterally in dorso-ventral direction) a relatively vigorous pressure-massage, whereupon some ripe sperm tends to collect in the cloaca. The massage may last 2—3 minutes, but the maximum of sperm-collection is reached only about 10 minutes afterward, thus only after the animal has awakened from the narcosis; yet it is not necessary for the rest of the procedure that it be narcotized. The seminal fluid — there need be only vanishingly little of it — is in both cases sucked up into a small injection-syringe (in the second case, with the living male, one must introduce the syringe-end into the cloacal opening without injuring it), then at once, likewise with all caution to pierce nothing through, injected into the female cloaca. One need thereby not go deep at all, for soon there fill up in the cloacal space, as I established through dissection of females that had been virginal before the injection, the Siebold sacs, from which then the oviducts are automatically infected with sperm.

Cross-bands (Querbinden) also extend from the flank-bands to the dorsal bands of the same side, and indeed, with time, ventralward from one flank-band over to the other flank-band. Whereas the cross-bridges (Querbrücken) between the dorsal bands, where only a short distance has to be covered, usually stand fairly perpendicular upon the latter, that is, run straight across, those between the flank-bands — where on the breast and belly a much broader dark zone must be overcome — often run obliquely and crookedly, whereby the originally strictly symmetrical arrangement finally appears not inconsiderably disturbed.

The partial abolition of symmetry — again predominantly on the underside, for on the upper side by now there remains almost no room for it — is, moreover, still further reinforced by the appearance of isolated yellow ocelli (Ozellen), which represent no derivatives of the already-innate, merely enlarged drawing, but rather appear independently, enlarge themselves independently, and eventually then fuse with the original drawing.

The final goal which the animals of $F_1$ attain on yellow earth thus consists in a considerable gain in yellow, far beyond the maximal measure of what the $P$-generation had attained in this respect. One may accordingly very well speak of a cumulative effect extending over these two generations, and may see the criterion of the inheritance that has taken place in precisely this accumulation, this strengthening, of the yellow drawing-elements.

Let us now look at the wanderings (Wandlungen) of the siblings transferred to black earth after metamorphosis (Pl. IV). The effect of the black earth need not be discussed here in greater detail; a special experimental series is devoted to it (this chapter under B, pp. 50–52); for now it suffices to establish that it represents straightforwardly a counter-induction (Gegeninduktion) of the yellow earth, that is, possesses the property of increasing the black pigment at the cost of the yellow. Nevertheless, in such $F_1$-animals, whose parents had become strongly yellow on yellow earth, here too at first the yellow color-substance takes an upswing, which expresses itself (on the back, not on the sides) in the fusion of the nearby row-flecks and thus brings about the stadium of the interrupted striping. Then indeed (often, but not necessarily at the same places where the fusion had taken place) a secondary disintegration occurs; the animal returns to the stadium of the fleck-rows. The individual flecks become smaller, duskier (i.e. intermixed with melanin at the same altitude [Höhenlage], see Chap. III), round themselves off; the smallest finally vanish entirely, so that those remaining over do indeed still clearly show the serial arrangement, but, the more flecks are absorbed, come to stand all the farther apart from one another in the longitudinal direction. In this it is never to be mistaken that this retrogressive process proceeds very sluggishly, that a certain stock of yellow stubbornly maintains itself, at least in $F_1$, however high an age the animal may reach on black ground. It is still relatively richly spotted when, of the spotting of an animal whose parents had not lived on yellow earth, almost nothing more remained over.

If one brings such animals — whose flecks do not yet stand too far, say not farther than 8–10 mm, apart from one another — back from the black to the yellow earth, that is, into the same milieu that their parents had inhabited, then longitudinal-elongation and -fusion of the flecks sets in; this leads, as a matter of course, back to the stadium of the interrupted striping, and under favorable circumstances (if the animal is not yet too old) even to the stadium of the uninterrupted striping. But if the flecks have already moved more than 10 mm apart in the longitudinal direction, then there follows, instead of the longitudinal-elongation that then fails to appear, a transverse-elongation and -fusion, whereby cross-banded salamanders arise (Pl. V). With full purity and regularity, however, this drawing-type, still rare in $F_1$, is to be seen only in $F_2$, after further losses of flecks had occurred, which guaranteed the still-reparable continuity of the longitudinal chains¹).

> ¹) Since the completion of the manuscript (in autumn 1912), $F_2$-descendants of those salamanders already cross-banded in $F_1$ have come to metamorphosis, and they all show beautiful, regular cross-banding — in 16 specimens already completed, in the remaining 11 already extensively indicated by parallel-standing flecks with processes toward the middle, and progressing vigorously even on non-yellow ground, so that the definitive median fusion can be only a question of time. The number of cross-bands varies between four and five. Hence a proof that even a specific drawing-form is faithfully inherited, as soon as it is symmetrical; and only with unsymmetrical drawing is usually not its whole style in detail (with preservation of the form and place of the flecks) transmitted, but only its surface-area (Flächenraum).

$F_1$-salamanders that are reared on the various, repeatedly enumerated neutral grounds agree with one another in principle, i.e. show no lawful differences whatsoever, whether they be now kept on gravel, sand, moss, on violet, blue, red, green ground. Between the two extreme cultures (on yellow, on black) they take, on average, a middle position: they show extension of the fleck-rows in the longitudinal and cross direction, reach without exception the stadium of the interrupted striping, without sinking back from it again to the stadium of the mere fleck-rows. A few even advance to almost uninterrupted (dorsal) longitudinal stripes, so that — and indeed within each single such culture — considerable individual differences become apparent.

### c. Grandchild generation ($F_2$).

The grandchild generation is still very young and not yet available from all, but indeed from the most important experimental series into which $F_1$ had to be split.

α) $F_2$-descendants of yellow earth, on continued rearing on yellow earth: here, then, this kind of ground has acted uninterruptedly upon three generations, that is, upon the first generation beginning from the point in time at which one took it into the experiment, and interrupted only by the larval periods spent on gravel-surfaces in the water. The births I allow, in this experimental series, to take place without further ado in the experimental container itself, since an avoidance of renewed induction upon the descendants is not at all intended.

Already in the freshly transformed little animals the further progress of the yellow showed itself: they no longer possess actual fleck-rows, but rather already little-interrupted longitudinal bands on both sides of the back, which (and indeed, on neutral ground too, without exception) already soon fuse into entirely uninterrupted ones. The cross-bridge formation and direct cross-fusion of the bands by transverse growth also proceeds here considerably more energetically — one specimen, though still small, has used up its black median zone down to a fine, interrupted longitudinal line, a second has used it up altogether without remainder. The latter therefore appears, seen from above, completely yellow; only on the sides and below have districts of black pigment remained over.

I spoke of how the enlargement of the present and the appearance of the new flecks had with time disturbed, and ventrally almost effaced, the innate symmetry in $F_1$; $F_2$, however, appears with fully restored symmetry: all the new acquisition of yellow which the children had to show in comparison to their parents appears in the grandchildren once again subjected to bilaterality, that is, tertiarily apportioned, if one wished to designate the apportionment of the yellow-stock in $F_1$ as a secondary one. — Upon the significance of these secondary symmetries with regard to the bilaterality-problem in particular and the inheritance-problem in general, it will be necessary to return in Chap. X ("Theoretical Evaluation").

β) $F_2$-descendants of black earth ($P$ on yellow earth) on continued rearing on black earth: the dissolution-process, by which the remaining row-flecks move ever farther apart from one another in the longitudinal direction, without, however, their row-wise, symmetrical arrangement ever being able to be mistaken on that account, is continued in $F_2$ where it left off in $F_1$. There result, therefore, predominantly black salamanders with few, small, far-apart-standing dorsal flecks (laterally and ventrally without flecks), which one could imagine connected by two straight lines drawn from the ear-glands (Ohrdrüsen) out to both sides of the back.

* Transfer of series β to mixed earth (Pl. XIV, Fig. 7).

From almost equal parts of loam-earth and garden-earth (with a slight preponderance of the former) a mixture was prepared, the one time a uniform one, the other time a mosaic-like one. Both times, only in the second case somewhat more intensely, the following appearance showed itself: the flecks remaining over on the black earth, which had become round, send out small, strikingly weak and narrow processes, which, because they find no corresponding processes of neighboring flecks (there are simply no flecks in sufficiently near neighborhood), cannot fuse, but instead grow out relatively long and thereby acquire a scroll-like (schnörkelförmig) form. It looks as though most of the flecks had got little tails.

** Transfer of series β to pure yellow earth.

Here the growing-out of the flecks still left standing on the black earth had naturally to proceed much more energetically. According as how many flecks had remained standing and how near to one another they accordingly still find themselves, that is, according to the more or less advanced absorption-process of the yellow earth, we can distinguish three quite different outcomes of the newly setting-in formation-process of yellow pigment:

1) Formation of cross-bands (Pl. V and Fig. 6 on Pl. XIV): since the longitudinal-distance between the flecks has mostly become too great, but not the cross-distance across the back, the flecks standing opposite one another send out corresponding processes growing toward each other, so to speak purposefully (zielbewußt), which meet roughly at the half-way point. As a rule the larger fleck thereby produces the stronger and longer process, which consequently comes a greater stretch to meet the process of the smaller fleck than the small one does the large one. By suitable selection of salamanders whose flecks are throughout separated by the black ground-color more than 1 cm wide in the longitudinal dimension, but not so far in the cross dimension, I was able on yellow earth to obtain purely cross-banded animals, which are drawn just as symmetrically as the longitudinally-striped ones.

2) Mixed mode; formation of cross-bands combined with the emergence of short longitudinal stripes (Pl. XIV, Fig. 5, 5a): that all the flecks, as was still possible in the preceding generation, could after being set back onto yellow earth flow together again lengthwise and re-establish the pure longitudinal stripes, no longer happened here at all — for there were simply far too many flecks that had reached complete disappearance out of the longitudinal rows; the rows had become too gappy; for a regular, continuous fusion lengthwise there would have been far too great gaps to bridge over; but individual flecks combining the longitudinal rows could nevertheless still have remained near enough together to unite now in an antero-posterior direction; others, on the contrary, had been near enough together in a lateral direction and fused into a cross-band. This is by far the most frequent case. If three or more flecks belonging to not the same dorsal row lie so that two of them can fuse crosswise, one of the latter with the others lengthwise, then there arises a right-angled bent band, of which the one limb represents the cross-band, the other the longitudinal band, which have joined through the mediation of the corner-fleck (Eckmakel). Two cross-bands too can fuse with a longitudinal band lying between them and thus produce an open quadrangle; very manifold, sometimes letter-shaped drawings come about in such a manner. By this process there is finally, just as on mixed earth by scroll-formation, the symmetrical drawing destroyed to the point of unrecognizability; we have here the peculiar case that, by combination of two symmetrical systems — of which the one, in consequence of minimal, primarily present asymmetries, is incompletely formed — an unsymmetrical one arises, whereas formerly it had simply been a matter of an abolition of symmetry by quite disordered increment. Since on yellow earth, moreover, as repeatedly mentioned, new flecks come to light, and indeed by no means incorporated into the symmetrical system, but rather at arbitrary places, the animals in the end strongly resemble strongly but completely unsymmetrically spotted specimens, such as the so-called forma typica of the fire-salamander, which occurs exclusively in the Vienna Woods, represents.

3) Formation of very long, obliquely and crookedly running bands (Pl. VI, Pl. XIV, Fig. 8): This mode is followed by animals which had previously lost most of their flecks on the black earth, in such a way, indeed, that not only — as with 1) and partly with 2) — no fusion any longer comes about in the longitudinal direction, but also not in the cross direction, for the simple reason that flecks almost nowhere any longer stand opposite one another; the rows have rather become so gappy that, if a fleck still remained preserved on the right, its counterpart is almost certainly vanished, and vice versa. But since now the transfer to yellow earth has a vigorous growing-out of the few remaining maculae as its consequence, there arise processes, scrolls (Schnörkel) which, figuratively speaking, wander aimlessly into the surrounding black ground-color, roam about in it, since nowhere is opportunity given to strike out in a definite direction that could lead to uniting with neighboring flecks. In principle the processes resemble in form those that arose on mixed earth, where it was not necessarily the distance of the neighbors, but rather their weak growth, that took from them the definitely directed character, for they could in consequence not have reached even nearby flecks either; but here the processes are much more vigorous, take their start from the whole breadth of the originating fleck, and therefore soon no longer appear as tail-like appendages of the originating fleck, but rather let the latter go fully up into themselves, so that one, if one has not followed the process in its becoming, could no longer distinguish where the scroll had its beginning and where, for the time being, it had its end taken. Only the one criterion remains available and can serve one, e.g. with similarly drawn salamanders that one has caught in the open, for orientation: the scrolls did after all proceed from symmetrically situated, albeit sporadic-become, flecks of the original dorsal longitudinal rows; they accordingly all begin at places that have shifted only a little beyond the presumable, usual position of such row-flecks; if one covers the scroll, so that the yellow of the places-of-origin remains free, then one again catches sight of the animal with the gappy fleck-rows.

The same experiment succeeds even much better when, instead of onto yellow earth, one transfers the animals onto yellow paper (Pl. VI), because this attends only to the enlargement of the flecks present at the time of the experiment's beginning, but in a more energetic manner than does the, after all not so intensively, yellow earth. Yet about the mode of action of the yellow paper-underlay we shall not anticipate further now. But it is to be remarked additionally that a mixed mode of irregular scroll-formation, as was just described, and regular cross-band formation, in quite rare cases also between the former and longitudinal-band formation, or all three, can come about on yellow earth or other yellow grounds, when precisely most of the flecks were indeed already so far apart from one another that only "aimless," oblique, crooked scrolls could still grow, while a few flecks did nevertheless still give occasion, longitudinally or transversely, for "purposeful," straight-line process-formation and fusion.

When irregular scrolls fuse with longitudinal- or cross-bands, there arise, naturally, again the most manifold, hieroglyph-like drawing-formations, such as one not seldom encounters also in free-living salamanders, but can now follow exactly in their emergence.

## 4. Control-experiment with primarily longitudinally-striped salamanders.

### (Pl. XIV, Fig. 1, 2).

The preceding experiment had, among other things, shown that the descendants of originally spotted, so-called "typical" salamanders, in case a considerable increase of yellow color had been forced upon the latter, turn out symmetrically drawn, spotted or striped. We learned, furthermore, already in Chap. III, that such animals not merely as an artificial product of the experimental cultures, but I wished to see how salamanders caught in the wild, and already marked symmetrically from the outset, behave under the influence of the yellow earth. The simplest course of events results when one places specimens with rows of spots, or with interrupted stripes, onto yellow earth: the spots fuse lengthwise, all the interruptions are here filled in with time, and the result is the continuously longitudinally striped form. At the same time there again appears the broadening of the stripe familiar to us: at favourable places (nape, upper side of the tail) the stripes come so close to one another, already through their uniform growth in breadth, that they fuse; where they are farther apart (back), they nevertheless form corresponding processes, and in further sequence cross-bridges. Moreover, isolated spots keep being added.

## 5. Selection and induction experiment with extra-yellow *typica* and *taeniata*.

Experiment No. 4 was thus indeed begun with already regularly marked, yet — at the start of the experiment — not exactly predominantly yellow animals; and the course of the experiment is readily surveyed, since it proceeds just as it does with animals to which we have already imparted, by the experimental route, the drawing-style there given. Somewhat more difficult to judge is the outcome when we select extra-yellow-coloured — that is, uninterruptedly and broadly banded — animals and place these on yellow earth. I had originally hoped that this would be the readiest way to arrive at the creation of totally yellow salamanders. But it turned out that a considerable increase of the yellow could here no longer be achieved — and indeed not even when the selected specimens were still sufficiently young and gave reason to presuppose enough plasticity. To be sure, the familiar transversal fusion appeared in the nape and tail-root region and, where it did not there already exist anyway, over the entire upper side of the tail, as did the formation of cross-bridges on the upper side of the back and head; to be sure, almost the entire under side became coated, between the considerable, already existing spots, with a yellow patina; but my expectation — that the animals so well predisposed to apparently complete yellowing would outdo the negatively selected ones of the principal experiment — was disappointed. Although I have, as in particular the F₂-generation described above shows under the continued action of yellow substratum, not yet come to the actual limit of modifiability — this limit being rather possibly given only with the total displacement of the melanin — yet in the present experiment it was indeed not to be mistaken that the advances always become slower the more had already been attained, that is, not only in dependence on the increasing age, but incidentally also still in dependence on the increasing yellow stock of the experimental animal. A residual stock of melanin defends itself, as it were, stubbornly against its extirpation.

A homologous weakening of the modifiability I noticed not only when I used specimens of the var. *taeniata* marked as richly as possible as starting-material, but also when I used the most amply spotted *forma typica* possible — of which, since they occur very frequently around Vienna, incomparably larger quantities were at my disposal, so as to seek out the very yellowest from among them. A small difference, which one does not arrive at if one reckons with single specimens, but only if one takes the average into account, does however exist between var. *taeniata* and *forma typica* with respect to their modifiability in the direction of becoming yellow; in the latter it is, at any rate, still by a trifle greater, even though the animals placed on yellow earth were the very yellowest among many hundreds.

Interesting is the F₁-generation of such extra-yellow chosen animals, whether descended from *taeniata* or *typica*; it comes namely almost up to the F₂-descendants of our principal experiment, and is thus able, at least on the upper side, to become totally yellow. That in this it falls somewhat short of the F₂-generation of the principal experiment is again to be recognized not in the single specimen, but only in the average outcome. That such high degrees of the yellow colouration were here, with direct instead of contra-selection, attainable a whole generation earlier, does indeed speak for the fact that an actual limit of modifiability does not exist, but rather that the alteration — even if under obstacles, which in the temporally limited experiment are all the more difficult to overcome — can advance up to that self-evident limit where the induced character has taken possession of the entire district of the animal accessible and proper to it (here the entire skin surface). But whence it comes that the modification undergoes any delay at all is likewise easy to explain: this comes from the resistance of the most similar tissue or cell-material occupying the remaining space at the beginning and in the course of the process, here the epidermis cells containing the black pigment.

## 6. Inheritance experiments on animals with weak induction and on predominantly yellow *forma typica* without induction.

Once more I take up the fact that an ample new acquisition of yellow pigment in the offspring is distributed in a secondarily symmetrical manner; and that this happens again, tertiarily, also in the young of those animals which were already symmetrically marked, but which, as a consequence of new acquisition of yellow, had more or less forfeited the symmetry. To clarify this phenomenon, which had come to me unexpectedly, I undertook two further control experiments.

Since indeed in the Wienerwald [Vienna Woods], the source of supply of my principal material, very richly spotted animals also occur, but never striped ones, the first thing to investigate was how their young, born in captivity, would look. Was the secondary bilaterality perhaps a consequence of domestication or of degeneration? I therefore selected from among hundreds the most richly spotted specimens of the *forma typica*, fed them on a substratum that is reliably neutral with respect to inductions of the colour-dress, and let them reproduce there: their young were, almost throughout, richly but without exception just as irregularly spotted as their parents.

Thus the conclusion appeared inescapable that yellow pigment must be added anew individually in order, generally, to give the impulse to the symmetrical distribution. It was now only the question whether for this any new acquisition whatever, even if a trifling one, would suffice, or whether the phenomenon sets in only from a certain quantity upwards. From the outset the latter appeared more probable; for in the course of a salamander's life some new spots do indeed mostly appear, even when it does not move on pronouncedly yellow ground; admittedly it cannot thereby be decided whether the "spontaneous" appearance is owed to an external induction or to an inherited tendency. To obtain enlightenment, the terrain and the state of colouration attained in relation to this terrain must be varied, on which experimental animals are withdrawn, in the direction of increase of yellow, from the conditions of induction and are bred in a neutral environment. For the ideal solution of the task an enormous number of such variations is required; I have solved it only imperfectly, and have found that a slight increment of yellow, gained on yellow ground, releases in the offspring no symmetrical arrangement of it, but rather that precisely for this purpose a very considerable, externally acquired increment of yellow is needed. By means of sufficiently numerous isolations one would have to be in a position, *ceteris paribus*, to fix quite exactly the point from which onward the symmetrical rearrangement sets in; and if it were not so difficult to determine the area of surface of the spots, one would have to be able to determine the boundary between unsymmetrical and symmetrical transmission of the colour-possession even numerically. It would be easier to calculate the surface content of the regular stripes, but there the symmetry is already given, the calculation therefore without object.

In order to be rightly understood, I must especially bring out the following circumstance: whether the colour-stock is transmitted symmetrically or unsymmetrically depends not on its absolute magnitude, but merely on whether it was considerably increased in the individual existence of an unsymmetrically spotted animal. I can therefore compare cases in which relatively more weakly spotted animals had symmetrically marked young, but very strongly spotted animals had unsymmetrically spotted young; the former had simply, in their youth, been still less spotted, and had lived on a substratum that had been favourable to the increase of their juvenile spot-stock; the latter, on the contrary, had brought their rich drawing-possession into the world already approximately finished. How predominantly yellow, but withal unsymmetrically marked specimens can come about may be explained in the following way: their life-milieu does indeed increase the yellow pigment, but in each generation only little, so that the extent of the new acquisition, for the symmetrical regrouping on the occasion of the ontogeny of the next generation, is not yet sufficient. Through summation of the scanty new acquisitions over many generations, a salamander finally comes about that possesses very much yellow, but nevertheless inherits it in unsymmetrical form. Were this not so, then there could be no race at all with unsymmetrical marking; rather, wherever strongly spotted animals grow up, spot-rowed and striped ones too would have to appear forthwith, which is not the case. Conversely, as we shall see (Chap. VI), the unsymmetry sits so firmly that, in crossing with the symmetry, it behaves as an elementary property, as an allelomorphic and indeed "dominant" character, provided nothing comes in between — that is, no "gene-induction" leading to considerable new acquisition of yellow pigment.

To yet one more apparently incidental circumstance, which has especially forced itself upon me in the present experiment, I must direct attention: namely, that it is impossible to fix an unsymmetrical spot, in inheritance, at a definite place on the body. This stands in agreement with the breeders' experiences which McCurdy and Castle have made on unsymmetrically piebald guinea-pigs, Lutz on flies (*Drosophila*) with unequally developed wings. A definite spot is, in the descendants of the specimen in question, never again to be recognized at all, neither according to form nor according to position: what is inherited, then, is only the yellow pigment (more precisely, the capacity to develop it) and the general drawing-style, but not further local details. I believe that this experience is not without significance for the understanding of the essence of inheritance; in connection with an objection that H. E. Ziegler has raised against Fischer's butterfly experiments, I shall return to it in the chapter "Theoretical Evaluation".

## B. Garden earth.

### 1. The principal experiment.

#### a. Parent generation [P].
#### (Pl. VII.)

Starting-material and record-keeping do not differ from those of the previous principal experiment (A. Loam earth), so that I can begin straightaway with the description of the course of the experiment. Here too, attention was again paid above all to excluding the breeding-selection objection and consequently rather to practising negative breeding-selection straightaway; this time, for such a purpose, fairly richly yellow-spotted, roughly one-year-old animals had to be (not exactly the extremes of the yellow colouration — Pl. VII, Fig. 3 *a–c*) selected, before they were placed on black garden earth and there permanently fed.

All specimens so treated show the reciprocal phenomenon of the loam-earth experiment: the initially large, richly articulated spots grow smaller, round themselves off into circular and dot form, finally to disappear altogether; larger drawing-complexes constrict themselves first — almost like a protozoon engaged in division — at various places, move apart, and then fall prey to the further process of diminution, rounding-off and dissolution. Any olive-green spots that may be present — as is well known, preliminary stages of the yellow ones — are rapidly reduced *in toto*. Hand in hand with the diminution goes darkening; whereas the diminution is owed to the circumstance that the black pigment displaces the yellow from outside, the darkening arises through this, that in the midst of the yellow region melanin granules, and thus tiny black islets, appear. As these uniformly gain the upper hand, the blemish often sinks before it had been eaten away to zero from the margin inward; sometimes, however, the melanin increases unevenly, condenses in places more than elsewhere, and then appears as a black dot, or even as a lobed spot, which for a while still possesses a yellow halo or border. All the small ring- and horseshoe-shaped spots which one finds in salamanders (also in the wild) are likely to come about in the manner described.

As in the loam-earth experiment with respect to increase of the yellow, so in the garden-earth experiment the tempo which the upper and under sides adopt with respect to the disappearance of the yellow is not the same; the under side, from the outset more sparingly spotted, does indeed come entirely to an end with its restricted yellow stock more quickly, but nevertheless the reaction itself is accomplished more slowly. The concentric narrowing of the spots from the margin inward plays a very subordinate role; although, as said, proceeding exceedingly slowly, the disappearance of the spots through darkening up to final "submergence" dominates — that is, the origin and accumulation of isolated melanin islands. Quite otherwise on the upper side: here the centripetal diminution dominates, even though the other process too is not lacking, indeed even achieves more, proceeds more quickly than on the belly side — but the narrowing of the spots from the margin inward is precisely still stronger and quicker. The reasons for this behaviour we shall soon come to know, until we arrive at isolating the factors which compose the total reaction (cf. especially pp. 77, 78).

As with the increasing, so the decreasing yellow colouration too has more rapid advances to record in summer than in winter, and for the same reasons as given under A.

#### b. Children's generation [F₁]. (Pl. VIII, IX.)

In what manner the germ-plasm of the F₁-generation is withdrawn, as long as possible — if feasible inclusive of the presumable "sensitive period" — from direct induction, may be read up in the loam-earth experiment (p. 35). At least the birth, and therefore the individual colour-experiencing of the new generation, which in truth only sets in with it, were always accomplished in a neutral or even oppositely acting environment, unless it was intended to let the conditions of induction sum themselves over more than one generation, in which case the larvae may of course without further ado be born in the experimental terrarium. The rearing of the larvae then takes place again on the neutral gravel bottom; the freshly transformed animals — and indeed the siblings of each litter, each by half — come, on the one hand, onto yellow, on the other hand again onto black earth. The surplus specimens of the litters with an odd number of siblings are used for this purpose, to set up a neutral culture on a substratum leaving the colours unchanged.

From the day of the becoming-visible of the yellow spots (thus already on older larvae) up to advanced age-stages, the following may be said in common of all F₁-descendants, for all their other diversity: 1) they are very poorly marked; 2) the yellow marking, perhaps better said the black ground-colour, is — in contrast to that of the parents — arranged in a bilaterally symmetrical manner.

Freshly metamorphosed young show a longitudinal row of spots which, beginning in the middle between the parotid spots, somewhat behind them, runs down the back; they thereby keep fairly exactly to the median line, by which they are at least always touched (Pl. VIII, IX, Fig. 1 *a*).

The further pigmentation shapes itself differently, according to whether the animals grow up on black or on yellow earth (Pl. IX and XIV, Fig. 9). Here the spots fuse lengthwise, so that a centrally situated longitudinal stripe results from them, but it proceeds more slowly than we are otherwise accustomed to from the increase of yellow on yellow ground, and quite often interruptions remain preserved for life. A few specimens make an exception herein, in that the increment of yellow not only helps the whole length of the back, and possibly of the tail, to a continuous median longitudinal band, but that this stretch apparently no longer suffices to let the band run straight; it then begins to curve, and, while it still keeps to the vertebral line, there results from it a wavy, or blunt zigzag, line. This comes about through the fact that the originally straight median stripe shows a not inconsiderable growth in breadth, but the black pigment, despite the keeping on yellow earth, again and again successfully eats bays into it, now from the right, now from the left. — Spots on the trunk-sides, if such are present at all or newly appear, likewise have the tendency to arrange themselves serially. If, then, the yellow pigment of the F₁-animals, which descend from a P-generation that has become predominantly black on black earth, experiences an upswing on transfer to yellow earth, then every such specimen, even though it should at the maximum bear a fairly broad, wavy median band, will still always evoke the impression of relatively poor marking and of the predominance of the black ground-colour, especially when compared with the descendants, living on the same ground, of the loam-earth culture from "P" onward.

Thus, if the yellow pigment of the F₁ animals—which descend from a P-generation that has become predominantly black on black earth—experiences an upsurge upon transfer to yellow earth, then every such specimen, even if at its maximum it bears a fairly broad, wavy median band, will still always produce the impression of a relatively impoverished marking and of the predominance of the black ground-colour, especially when compared with the descendants of the loam-earth culture from *P* onward living on the same soil.

Let us now consider the transformations of the **siblings that, like their parents, remained on black earth after metamorphosis** (Plate VIII). Here, of course, it stays with the median row of spots; no band develops from it; indeed, on the contrary, the spots that appeared at metamorphosis undergo a further reduction, darkening, and restriction, in that some of them fall victim entirely to these processes. There result specimens with only very few spots, all situated on the vertebral column, which often look blurred almost to the point of unrecognizability (overlaid with melanin), but which, on account of their median position, always allow their membership in a row to be recognized. On the underside and on the flanks—here, at most with the exception of a few quite insignificant dots and speckles—this time no marking-elements whatever appear.

If one disregards a few specimens that have carried the reduction of the yellow very far, and instead takes rather the average into account, then it must be said that the yellow pigment shows itself—at least on coloured substrates, not as against other conditions, which we shall come to know—essentially more resistant than the black. The advances of the yellow on yellow earth are greater than those of the black on black earth; the regressions of the yellow on black earth are smaller than those of the black on yellow earth. The descendants of yellow animals on black soil at first still show a transient upsurge of the yellow before the counter-induction is given effect; the descendants of black animals on yellow soil show no analogous upsurge of the black. In the cases indicated, the changes of the yellow correspond to a curve that rises and then descends again, those of the black to a curve that, though slowly, yet only descends continuously.

Wholly unexpected and surprising to me was the peculiar secondary symmetry of the colour-regions, which had also set in, in this principal experiment too, in both daughter-series of the black-earth culture. At first glance, indeed, such salamanders appear less strictly symmetrically marked than the two-rowed or even two-striped ones, and E. G. BOULENGER, as is evident from his pertinent figures *a*, *b*, and *c* of his text-illustration 110, p. 332, reckons them to the *forma typica* with an unsymmetrical marking-style. They are, moreover—be it remarked in passing—fairly rare in the wild, whereas under breeding conditions they can be produced in any desired quantity with absolute certainty.—I now believe that the lesser regularity is only apparent; they are in reality just as regularly marked as the *»taeniata«*: only it is not, as in the latter, the yellow colour that has divided itself symmetrically, but the black. The latter, as the now predominant colour, now occupies the flanks and leaves to the suppressed one a narrow dorsal zone. The animals are exactly reciprocal to the *taeniata*-forms; if on a picture of the var. *taeniata* one paints the yellow region black and the black region yellow, the result is a specimen such as the one we are now discussing, and now all at once it likewise seems to have lost much of its symmetry. The garish yellow of the *taeniata* is, after all, far more decisive for the impression of regular bilaterality; the irregular appearance of the black areas left over in between does not catch the eye; conversely, the slightest irregularity of the remaining yellow regions—when, so to speak, the flank-bands are black—is in turn highly decisive, precisely because of their more garish prominence. Both forms of the F₁-generation—the descendants with rich yellow, those with median-band symmetry, as well as those with black bilateral symmetry—are distinguished thereby; in neither case do the sides of the back, in neither case do the flanks, draw symmetrical marking to themselves: in the former case yellow cross-bridges, in the latter case necessarily black cross-bridges.

For the sake of the demonstrative force of the breeding-experiment shown in its details, I have here noted, out of conscientiousness (unnecessarily—for to what end would all the control-breedings then be?), that medium-striped and median-fleck-rowed animals also occur after all in the wild, namely among the source-material collected in the Vienna Woods—something that was not the case in the double-striped and lateral-fleck-rowed animals of the yellow-experiment. From this we may perhaps conclude that the Vienna Woods population at present tends rather toward a decrease of the yellow; yet the symmetry of such natural specimens is not so pure, so strictly bilateral, as in the experiment.

A grandchild-generation of the principal experiment exists as yet only in the young larval state; one can therefore as yet say nothing about its colour-condition. I make use of this residue, as against the principal experiment of the loam-earth culture, in order to establish that I learned, quite generally indeed, how very much better loam-earth suits the amphibians than garden-earth; there they grow larger, as v. SCHREIBERS already knew, grow faster and longer, feed more abundantly, live longer, and propagate more readily and more often. This is partly explained by the fact that the yellow earth, under equal conditions, always preserves a somewhat greater moisture-content (see the moisture-experiment, p. 67); more important, however, is likely the greater bacterial richness of the black earth, among which there are certainly some microbes pathogenic to salamanders.

### 2. Transfer of black-kept F₁-animals onto yellow earth.

In order not to lose the overview, I must here, I suppose, already recapitulate, by way of catchwords, the whole experimental line: thus, *P*-generation on black earth, become predominantly black; F₁-generation again on black earth, become still blacker, with the few, dark spots—becoming ever fewer and darker—along the middle of the back. Such animals are now transferred for the first time onto yellow earth.

Their behaviour is different according to whether one undertakes the re-transfer with specimens whose dorsal fleck-row is still relatively intact, that is, situated fairly close together (at about 8–10 mm distance), or whether the gaps are already wider. In the former case there simply takes place a longitudinal fusion, at least of the spots situated closest to one another, so that the culture of those concerned can become almost the same as the one that from the outset, in F₁, was on yellow earth.

In the second case there takes place—since the spots lie too far apart for a »purposeful«, rectilinear fusion—a directionless, unsymmetrical outgrowth of the remaining spots, which thereby develop into oblique, crooked, or jagged loops, similar to those from which the transfer onto yellow earth proceeds in the double rows of the black-kept F₁-generation of the loam-experiment. Only the growth-energy here, in the case of the like F₁-portion of the garden-earth experiment, is less great, as one can see particularly well on mixed earth, where there (in the loam-experiment) it nevertheless yields pronounced flourishes, but here scarcely any beginnings of such. Isolated newly appearing spots help here too to destroy the symmetry given by the median position of the yellow spots. A possible, unfortunately non-existent, F₂-generation of this portion would probably again yield young with irregularly distributed marking, that is, a quite characteristic *»forma typica«*.

Especially pure loop-formation, which proceeds from the median spots in indeterminate directions, is obtained if one transfers the hitherto black-kept F₁-animals onto yellow paper instead of onto yellow earth, since the yellow here grows faster, appears more purely at once, and the new formation of spots is absent. There then arise only the loops, as the expression of a longitudinal, though otherwise un-directed, enlargement of the sparsely remaining median fleck-stock.

One will perhaps ask why the spots that have remained in place on black earth show, upon transfer onto yellow earth, no all-round uniform surface-growth, but precisely the loops and flourishes just described; the answer must provisionally run thus: that such concentric growth in all directions actually never otherwise takes place either, but rather that the increase in size of each spot is accomplished through the formation of processes with subsequent filling-in of the bays. Only do processes arise not merely, as here, from one side, but from various sides of the fleck-periphery; that this happens, and where this happens, is determined by the chemotropic attractive force—touched upon several times, not yet quite clarified—which the neighbouring, enlarging spots exert upon one another. In our present case such spots are lacking in sufficiently near neighbourhood, and so the spot grows, under the compulsion of the external factor, after a process has formed at some arbitrary point of the periphery, from this process into the directionless.

### 3. Keeping of the var. *taeniata* on black earth.
*(Plate XIV, Figs. 3, 4.)*

This experiment thus begins with material of departure other than the two experiments on black earth described hitherto. And indeed it is again striped and fleck-rowed specimens of the var. *taeniata*, which had been caught in the Vorwohle (Braunschweig, northern Germany). In loam-experiment No. 4 (p. 46) we heard of fleck-rowed, uninterruptedly striped animals, that the interruptions are filled in on yellow earth, so that they advance to continuously banded specimens. Here, first of all, the exactly reciprocal process may be reported: specimens with uninterrupted stripes show a narrowing of these stripes, which at first stubbornly cling to their continuity; but finally they break apart into individual pieces (Plate XIV, Fig. 4), and the pieces break apart once more, so that a fleck-rowed specimen then appears to be produced.

If one began the experiment already with such a one, that is, if one placed a specimen with fleck-rows onto black earth, then there again comes into play the process of diminution and darkening already abundantly known to us, which eliminates one link of the chain after another—always, naturally, the smallest spots first—and finally produces specimens whose spots, though still always ranged on both sides of the back, stand at very great distances from one another and by no means always still opposite one another; that is, to a spot in the left dorsal row a spot in the right row no longer always corresponds; but rather, if the latter was at first smaller, then it is by now already eliminated, or vice versa. To be sure, spots standing *vis-à-vis* one another are nearly equal in size, so that they nevertheless often either remain preserved together or disappear together at almost the same time.

### 4. Selection- and induction-experiment with extra-black specimens.

Just as, in the loam-experiment, we investigated the effect of selection and the eventual limit of modifiability in at least two generations, so this necessary supplementary experiment was to be carried out in the garden-earth experiment as well. It therefore begins with salamanders that, among many hundreds, had been selected out as the blackest, and lets them grow up on black earth. Whereas, in the selection of the yellowest-possible animals and in the endeavour to drive the yellow-colouration to its peak by corresponding induction, a resistance, a slowing-down, and (for our means of observation) an approximate standstill of the reactions had come to light, this came to light in the second generation also for the culture now under discussion. One does not get the animals much blacker than they already are anyway at their commencement. This here stands in accord with the experience reported a little earlier, namely that the yellow offers more stubborn resistance to the displacing influence of a black underground than does the black to the displacing influence of a yellow underground. A certain advance of the black-colouring, falling far short of expectations, was nevertheless still to be achieved after all; it speaks once more for the fact that limits of modifiability do not in reality exist, and are only feigned by the slowing-down of the modification toward the infinitesimal as well as by the forced brevity of the experimental duration in comparison with the time-spans really required. In particular, the F₁-generation reared from black-selected, black-kept animals permits such a conclusion, for it is decidedly blacker than the like-named F₁-generation of the yellow-selected source-animals of the principal experiment. Up to the limit of evidence, the unrestrictedness of modifiability lets itself be proven here only when, besides the black earth, one takes to one's aid four further factors as well, which are likewise unfavourable to the yellow pigment, partly directly favourable to the black, and this happens in the following experiment.

### 5. Production of totally black *Salamandra maculosa*.

Already in my work of 1904, and still more in that of 1908, I had indicated that young *Salamandra maculosa* [*Salamandra salamandra*] which one had let develop after the manner of the wholly black alpine *Salamandra atra*, that is, in such a way that their entire larval development is transferred into the interior of the uterus, exhibit a smaller quantity of yellow pigment than those developing normally in the water. They are thus brought nearer to *Salamandra atra* not only in their reproduction and development, but also in their colouration. In the course of their growth this is, to be sure, equalized again, provided they live on neutral soil, and they are then no longer to be distinguished from ordinary spotted fire-salamanders.

It is otherwise when such little animals—which, be it noted, originate from a black-selected F₁-generation of the black-earth culture—are kept on black earth from their transformation onward. Then, naturally, a further reduction of the taken-over stock of marking sets in, and since, by reason of their developmental history, they brought little enough of it into the world anyway, they resemble especially the dark-selected ones, but, on account of their youth, are still very well suited as plastic experimental specimens. By way of anticipation I must here just very briefly remark that I am, moreover, in a position to destroy the yellow pigment through relative deficiency of moisture—kept quite dry, no amphibian endures it—(p. 72); to diminish it through scanty nourishment; and finally to strengthen and accelerate the tendency to lively increase of the black through relatively high temperature of about 25–30 degrees—much more than this no salamander endures.

To be sure, the experimental conditions, under which great mortality was not to be avoided, were such that there remained to me only **few**, but for that all the more interesting, very decisive specimens **alive**, which I describe separately:

1) Two specimens completely black, except for one small spot each on the eyelids, ear-glands (in one specimen, on the right one split into two), [and] beginnings of the extremities. It is to be emphasized that these are the spots which, as we have heard (p. 19), are the very first to make their appearance in the larva. By the bright, whitish-yellow extremity-beginnings, even quite young salamander-larvae are easily to be distinguished from *Triton*-larvae. And these same spots now prove themselves to be those which, in the developed animal, when it is subjected to conditions extremely unfavourable to the yellow pigment, most stubbornly ward off complete dissolution.

2) One specimen whose spots, to a relatively large extent, still contain grains of yellow colouring-matter between the overgrowing melanin, so that they are still visible with difficulty, although so dark that the animal appears totally black at some distance.

3) A specimen which on the throat and upper lip possesses a few yellowish, tiny little speckles, but is otherwise — including the parotoids, eyelids, etc. — totally black. The speckles can scarcely be considered remnants of an earlier yellow coloration, since they do not belong to the ordinary yellow pigment but to the whitish-yellow, fundamentally different one described in Chapter III, such as can be seen as a fair rarity even in *Salamandra atra* now and then. We may therefore safely say that in the animal now mentioned every trace of its original yellow spots — which constitute a pronounced specific character of *Salamandra maculosa* — has vanished.

Are we still to designate this specimen as *Salamandra maculosa*, or have we transformed it into *Salamandra atra*, whose coloration and reproduction it heritably possesses? I shall attempt to decide this question only in Chapter X ("Theoretical Evaluation", p. 182). For now let only the purely experimental finding be pointed out: the differences in colour and reproduction by no means stand alone in the salamander cultures, but to them there are at any rate already beginning to be added such [differences] of body form, even if for the time being only in the shape of non-heritable, fluctuating modifications: the animals of the extreme yellow culture retain their normal size, grow rather above average dimensions, are plump and broad-headed; the animals of the extreme black culture are (without reaching the nanism of *Salamandra atra*) somewhat dwarfed, stunted, narrow, all this even without their being poorly fed, as indeed was, to superfluity, actually practised in the present experiment.

## 6. Inheritance experiments on animals with weak induction and on predominantly black animals without induction.

In order to complete the parallelism of the black-earth experiment with the loam-earth experiment, there now still remained to be made the test of the **closer conditions of the appearance of secondary bilateral symmetry**. We know, after all, that a copious new acquisition of black pigment in the offspring is distributed symmetrically, in such a way indeed that this [pigment] occupies the broad regions on both dorsal sides and the flanks, while the yellow can be made to crowd together into a more or less narrow, more or less interrupted zone in the dorsal midline.

For since in the Vienna Woods too, the source of supply of my chief material, very sparsely spotted animals occur, it was first of all to be investigated how their young would look that were born in captivity without induction of the parents, that is, on neutral ground. They were almost throughout very poor, but without exception just as irregularly spotted as the parents.

Also a slight increment of black, which was won from moderately spotted animals when kept on black earth — whereupon, in order to keep this increment within bounds, they were transferred onto neutral ground — releases in the descendants **no symmetrical arrangement of the same, but for this purpose precisely a very considerable increment of black, acquired from the environment, is absolutely required**. Therefore specimens which are very black, but in the course of their life have not added much more to it, but rather have taken over the deficiency of patterning heritably, can produce unsymmetrically patterned young; whereas relatively still more copiously patterned specimens which during their individual existence have suffered a great loss therein bring symmetrically patterned young into the world.

### C. Yellow Paper.

*(Pl. X and XIV, Fig. 10, 11.)*

It is evident that ever so many experiments and control experiments on black and yellow earth cannot elucidate the phenomenon of the corresponding morphological colour change: for in the case of the earth-types we know too little as to what kind of effective factor we have before us. The earthy environment doubtless allows, after all, different kinds of energy to act upon the salamanders living in it: between black garden earth and yellow loam earth there exist, after all, by no means only the differences given by the colours, but besides these naturally chemical [ones], moisture differences on account of the different hygroscopicity, slight temperature differences on account of the greater warmth-absorption on the darker ground, finally perhaps even differences in the tactile stimuli conveyed. Possibly only one of these factors could call forth the observed colour change, but possibly also more than one or all. It was therefore first and foremost my most important task to isolate the enumerated factors.

The most obvious thing, because it is most associated with the effects, nevertheless naturally remained the colour differences. And I will therefore first describe how I isolated the **colour effects** as far as possible. For this purpose the same terraria came into use as in the earth experiments; but their floor part, which is intended to receive the substrate, is filled with river sand, that is, with a mass which in respect of colour effects is demonstrably neutral and which, moreover, was identical in both parallel rows (C and D). Of chemical and moisture differences there could here be no question. The river-sand layer, also a piece of the side walls of the terrarium, was then in experiment C covered with yellow, in experiment D, which we shall describe separately, with black paper, so that nothing more remained visible of the sand surface, but rather the whole floor surface of the terrarium represented a uniformly yellow or black plane. The paper coming into use is, in order to forestall a hindering effect of damage, tearing, and the like, coloured on both sides and spread out in a double layer. It makes no particular difference whether it is matte or glossy paper; the latter becomes, once the salamanders have crawled over it a few times with their moist bodies, matte anyway, so that a possible disturbing effect of the mirror-gloss at once falls away — and I chose for the most part glossy paper, because it comes onto the market in more saturated colours, the black too in a much deeper tone, than the matte [paper].

In order that the animals should feel halfway at ease, namely insofar as they remain permanently alive and reproduce, the arrangement must now be further completed by moss-nests, large enough that they can burrow into them, small enough that much coloured floor surface remains over. To prevent a dragging-about of the moss, the nests are surrounded with stones, which are of the same kind in all the containers (some quartz or other). Water-basin and food-dish are placed at the end of the container opposite to the moss-nest, in order to compel the salamanders to wander to and fro over the coloured paper.

By the described arrangement we have therefore first of all **eliminated** the moisture and the chemical and tactile differences inherent in the earth-types. The latter happens still more perfectly if one uses vessels with a glass bottom, leaves them quite empty, and lays the paper not over, but under the bottom. Thus did Šečerov (1912e), v. Frisch, and others handle it in their re-examinations, and methodically improved my results; only one cannot, in empty glass vessels with a paper underlay, keep the salamanders probably long enough to observe distinct colour effects; one can, however, very probably not breed them therein, on which, after all, it had to be of prime importance to me.

Let us now occupy ourselves with the **colour-change phenomena** that appear on the yellow paper. As starting material there serve typical salamanders, namely such as were selected with quite few spots. The spots begin to enlarge in the manner already known to us, to send out processes, whereby originally round spots receive a rich articulation, and finally, where processes touch one another — and to this they have decidedly a great inclination — to fuse together. Thus far, then, the phenomenon resembles that on yellow earth, only that it is to be remarked that the increase of size in yellow pigment appears almost at once in full colour-purity, [and that] transitional stages, which result from gradual displacement of the black and mixing of the latter with the yellow, scarcely come to observation (Pl. X and Fig. 11 on Pl. XIV). This is a function of the greater speed with which the yellow spots here grow out, and I am probably not mistaken if I seek the cause in the more saturated colour of the substrate: yellow paper is, after all, considerably more intensely yellow than yellow earth.

One partial phenomenon, however, which on yellow earth had shown itself with the greatest regularity, here failed entirely to appear on the paper: **the isolated new appearance of at first punctiform, then likewise enlarging spots**. New spots arose not at all, at most quite rarely one on the underside, which from grounds that will become intelligible later is more inclined to it; these would here probably have arisen anyway, in consequence of an inherited tendency to bring forth individual such little spots. Had I therefore not practised negative selection, but used animals which already carry within themselves immanently a stronger multiplication of the yellow, then in any case still more new spots would have arisen — but as it was, it was downright an exception and a great rarity. There result therefore at the end of the experiment salamanders which are very richly yellow-patterned, but owe this richness not to their numerous, but to their large spots. Fundamentally regarded, they possess only very few spots — not more than at the beginning — but these spots cover a good part of the entire body surface.

In few words I can also at once dispose of the child-generation, which I possess from these salamanders that had become strongly yellow on yellow paper (but transformed only 1½ years [ago]): they show again the secondary symmetry, but, I should like to say, even purer, even more regular than the child-generation from yellow earth, because intermediate patterning-elements, ocelli, points, are completely lacking and, on continued keeping on paper underlays, also did not appear. The animals growing up again on yellow paper possess quite simply, so to speak schematically, 4 yellow longitudinal bands (2 on the flanks, 2 on the back) or 2 longitudinal bands and 2 rows of spots (the latter on the flanks) of **extraordinarily regular, entire-edged contours**. The animals growing up on black paper possess 2–4 longitudinal rows of spots (those on the flanks may be lacking or very weakly developed). Both groups make on the whole the impression of possessing much yellow; the group on yellow paper to a higher degree than the parents, that on black paper to a weakened degree.

Further experiments which made use exclusively of the yellow paper were not carried out, since indeed the result obtained had been elucidating in every desirable way. There is still only to be recalled those **experiments on yellow paper which took over their material from the earth cultures**: the growing-out of oblique, crooked, "directionless" flourishes and loops in two-rowed (*F*₁, *F*₂ of the *P* from yellow earth on continued keeping on black earth — Pl. VI) and in one-rowed (*F*₁ of the *P* from black earth on continued keeping on black earth) salamanders, whose spots already lay too far apart from one another to permit direct fusion. It had already been said that here too the yellow-increment is accomplished by far purer, more beautifully, than on transferring the animals in question onto yellow earth.

### D. Black Paper.

*(Pl. XI and XIV, Fig. 12–15.)*

As to what we isolate by the use of the paper and how we set up the containers, there is nothing new more to say; the reader may consult it under C (yellow paper). I can report at once the effects observed on black paper in the salamanders selected as quite richly yellow.

The effects consist in a **becoming-smaller of the spots** (Pl. XI, XIV, Fig. 14). From the margin the melanin penetrates inward, and indeed only insofar irregularly as processes which by their nature, because they are narrower than the trunk of the spot, are more rapidly snatched away than the more entire-edged stretches of the spot-periphery. The consequence of this is that the spot first rounds itself off more and more. Once it has approximately assumed the circular form, then its diminution proceeds so regularly that it can be directly represented by concentric circular rings. Finally the round spot is reduced to a point and disappears entirely. But as long as anything at all of it still remains visible, **its yellow pigment is quite pure, because it lies immediately at the surface** — it is here not intermixed with melanin and therefore not clouded. From this rule there are, with appropriate treatment of the culture, namely regular dispensations of moisture, just as rarely exceptions as from the mere enlargement of spots, [or] the failure of spot-multiplication in the yellow-paper culture. Only if one, in order, say, to spare the paper underlay (which naturally, when it has become too wet, must be exchanged, which becomes necessary roughly every two weeks), had not applied the atomizer sufficiently, so that the substrate had dried out strongly, do spot-cloudings occur more frequently. One can, however, by raising the moisture, make them recede again, [and] make the yellow come to the fore anew in unclouded purity. In the interest of experimental purity such fluctuations are naturally to be avoided if possible, and in my new cultures have also always been successfully avoided, so that transient, weak, and rare spot-cloudings may only be ascribed to the inherited tendency to this kind of melanin-appearance and retained no further significance for the course of the experiment. There result, then, animals with very few, very small spots, which however, so long as they are present at all, and even if represented only any longer in point-form, remain visible far and wide through their luminous yellow.

What could be said of the yellow-paper culture in comparison with the yellow-earth culture, namely that the increase of the yellow proceeds there more energetically than here, can reciprocally also be stated of the black-paper culture: the **decrease of the yellow is a more rapid one than on the black earth**. So great, to be sure, the difference is not as between the result on yellow paper and yellow earth, just as also the intensity-difference between black paper and black earth is not so great.

I note at once of the daughter-generation (Pl. XIV, Fig. 15, to be compared with Fig. 13) that they transformed themselves, to be sure, into few, large, little-articulated or round spots, which almost all together stand along the vertebral line, thus quite as in the descendants of the black-earth culture, but without darkening of these spots. The portion further kept on black paper too remained so [unchanged], only that the spot-row, through the elimination of spots, acquired still greater gaps; the portion transferred onto yellow paper let the median spot-row flow together into an interrupted or sometimes also uninterrupted longitudinal band. In both rows, the black-kept one too, the yellow is very glaring, because completely melanin-free. Even a brief sojourn of such animals — with some of them this side-experiment was undertaken — on black earth or in a relatively dry milieu causes, however, clouding melanin-islands to arise within the spot-area; and even an equally brief sojourn — 4–5 months suffice — on yellow earth or in a very moist milieu causes new ones in dapple-form to be added to the old, enlarging spots.

As a subsidiary, ecological result I could here too again — for which I already had occasion on p. 55 — point to the special hygienic properties of the loam earth: for whereas everywhere where parallel experiments with loam earth and garden earth run, there the better successes and in particular the more timely breeding successes are to be seen, this regular occurrence is at once broken through on application of the colour-papers, which in hygienic respect are surely without difference: here it is (this time probably only by chance) the culture on black paper whose offspring-rearing is already older and could therefore already be attested in preliminary communications (Kammerer 1911a), when from the yellow paper no child-generation yet lay before [us].

### E. Moisture.

Already from much of the foregoing, but especially from the concluding words of the last experiment-description, the attentive reader will probably guess wherein the effect of moisture (toward its two extremes) consists.

I give first the reason that moved me to set up a separate moisture-experiment, and the special experimental arrangement. The two earth-types used in the main experiments A and B, yellow loam earth and black garden earth, differ namely, under otherwise equal conditions, also Among them, the loam soil is considerably more hygroscopic; the difference is indeed so great that one can perceive it subjectively, for instance by testing with the finger. Since, despite this, the contrary opinion is at times held even in gardening circles—namely that garden soil draws and retains more atmospheric moisture to itself—I saw myself compelled to let the exact experiment decide. I took equal volumes (1 cubic decimetre each) of the two earth-types and dried them out completely in the desiccator with the aid of calcium chloride (CaCl₂); I then weighed them, let them stand side by side, that is, in the same space and at the same temperature, for 20 days, and ascertained, after the elapse of this period—both by renewed weighing and with the hair-hygrometer—that the yellow earth was moister than the black. At least for the earth-sorts used by me—they were the same in all experiments and of almost the same provenance—I must therefore assert with all definiteness the *ceteris paribus* greater water-content of the loam soil. One recognizes this, as said, straightaway already through coarse-qualitative, subjective observation, through the sense of touch or even through the sense of sight.

The experimental terraria that were to serve for the isolation of the moisture-factor were set up exactly like those of the two preceding experiments (C and D), only with the omission of the paper substrates: the floor-filling therefore consisted, in all of them, of river sand; to this is added the stereotyped little mound of moss (always built up using the same sort of quartz), the water-basin and the fooddish. It would have suggested itself, in the desiccation experiment, to leave out the water-basin entirely; the following grounds determined me to retain it: firstly, it means demanding too much of a salamander which is not compensated by a very moist milieu (as in my experiments, in inducing the fire-salamander to give birth to full newts instead of larvae) to deprive it, in a relatively dry milieu, of the daily bathing-opportunity as well. Certainly, I might thereby perhaps have been able to shape the results more markedly, but it would have been impossible to keep this desiccation culture permanently capable of life and, above all, capable of reproduction. This ground therefore coincides with the one that determined me, in the paper experiments, to add a little moss-nest, which naturally had to withdraw the animals from the colour-induction for a considerable part of the day. Almost still weightier is the following motive for retaining the water-basin in the "desiccation culture" too: its presence or non-presence signifies not merely a variation of the moisture-factor, but also one of the molar factor; quite other tactile stimuli are conveyed to a salamander that daily climbs into the water than to one that cannot do this. I should thus at most have had to leave out the basin from the "moisture culture" as well, which in turn would have been bound up with too great a loss in water-uptake in this latter and, as said, with too great a loss in life-capability in the parallel desiccation culture.

The difference between my moisture culture and my desiccation culture thus consisted solely in this, that in the former the floor (river sand) was daily and copiously moistened with the watering-can or the atomizer, whereas here it was not subjected to this treatment at all.
