The sand of the desiccation culture, likewise the moss-nest there, could at most take up some moisture from the air, or that which the salamanders climbing to the bath dragged across. For eye and hand, however, sand and moss-nest, brought to an equal degree of dryness, presented themselves like the floor of a pure desiccation terrarium. Since, moreover, the ventilation-flaps of the container were here kept closed—except for short airing-pauses during tending—this considerable moisture-difference, although the evaporating surface of the water-basin was present in the desiccation culture too, communicated itself to the air and could be measured with the hygrometer. The relative atmospheric humidity amounted, in the open desiccation culture, to on average 40—50%; in the closed moisture culture, to 80—90%; the difference, therefore, was always at least 30%. With this I could content myself—before installations and apparatus for keeping the moisture constant and for its continual registration existed at our Institute—in spite of other shortcomings, such as the moisture-dispensing judged disparately and subjectively. I believe that the factor moisture was thereby sufficiently investigated and isolated for qualitative purposes of observation.

I should not like to omit emphasizing especially, before all else, that in this experiment too the fertilized and parturient females were kept away, as far as possible, from a direct induction, and were placed in complicated or contrasting milieus at the latest before delivery. For the appearance of the offspring this measure, or its omission, remained just as immaterial as in all earlier inheritance experiments.

### 1. Moisture.
*(Plates XII and XV, Figs. 16—18.)*

I again chose young animals with few spots and set them on wet sand. On the upper side no change was apparent for a long time, so that I was already inclined to believe in the ineffectiveness of the factor. The belly-side, however, surprised me by the quantity of the small yellow points and drops which had in the meantime newly been added (Plate XII, Figs. 4*b*, 6*b*, 8*b*). Little by little the back-side too took part in this new formation of isolated districts of the yellow pigment (Plate XII, Figs. 6*a*—9*a*). The old spots, on the other hand, already present at the time of the experiment's beginning, remained in their original condition; that is, neither did their size change to any considerable degree, nor their colour-saturation. The relevant changes—quite inconspicuous and slow—keep themselves almost throughout fairly within the limits of that which goes on in the natural growth of every salamander; one can therefore assert that the moisture has little to do with the increase in size of the spots already present. In a few cases, where this did not appear to hold, one or more new spots had arisen close beside an old one and had fused with it: by this an increase in size of the old spot was simulated. This is evidently not to be understood as though the new formation of yellow pigment promoted by wetness restricted itself to the previously black-coloured territory and did not take place at all in the yellow territory; that new formation and multiplication rather proceeds upon the whole skin-surface, only that in the region of the like-coloured colour-substance it is, on account of the lacking contrast, less striking and is constituted almost solely by a condensation of the already deposited pigment-grains and -cells, the gaining of the upper hand of which, however, does not after all suffice to bring about any considerable enlargement of the zone in question. This condensation is microscopically well demonstrable either in quite young animals undergoing metamorphosis, or in older ones that have taken in no food for some weeks: for both possess their pigments in a looser arrangement, more accessible to observation. Solely in this—in the more universal spread of the multiplication of a particular pigment—rests the difference as against the colour-effective floor, which permits the multiplication of the pigment it favours too not in the unlike, but only out from the like-coloured pigment, here however in an especially energetic manner, which therefore leads to the already macroscopically distinctly demonstrable zone-enlargement.

The preformed spots, too, appearing in tiny point-form in the midst of the black region, enlarged themselves only up to the ocellus-(drop-)form, whereupon their growth stands still. They take on no articulation whatever, so that the circular shape predominates; at most 1—2 short projections form and give the small spot a pear- or horseshoe-shape. There result, accordingly, salamanders whose predominantly black ground-colour is interrupted by unusually numerous, unusually small and simply shaped spots; and while thus, both otherwise in the normal course of events and under the influence of yellow-promoting surroundings, the dorsal side is more richly marked than the ventral side, here the reverse is the case. But because the spots remain so small, the animals (which from the beginning were after all assigned to be as black as possible) nevertheless make no especially yellow impression.

If one brings them onto yellow earth, or still better onto yellow paper, the small spots forthwith begin to become larger, and, since on account of their great quantity they mostly stand close beside one another, especially many spot-fusions set in. Naturally the animals must still be correspondingly young, otherwise the reaction fails to appear, or it is weak.

If one brings them onto black paper, or still better onto black earth, rapid spot-disappearance sets in, since the melanin penetrating in from all sides, from the centre as from the margin (on the paper only from the margin) naturally soon clears away spots of such slight extent.

The freshly transformed young of the wet-kept parents all show the same phenomenon: remarkably many, but very small, small-remaining, round spots (Plate XV, Fig. 18). One might here perhaps object that this has its cause in a renewed moisture-induction, since they did after all live as larvae in the water. But the rearing of young salamanders from so many other experimental series taught that the possession of numerous spots in the freshly metamorphosed little animal is, in my experiments, something unusual. As a rule it possesses rather few, at most relatively very large, spots, or else few small spots; many small spots are a rarity, which, however, in our present experimental series fell to all the young animals without a single exception. Only with the keeping under different conditions now setting in does the development of the marking too strike out along different paths.

If it remains at the wet surroundings (80—90% relative atmospheric humidity), it remains at the described marking-type too, under continual new appearance of points and dots. The animals are still not old enough for it to be decided with certainty whether in this an increase will set in as against the parent-generation. On the back-side of the most richly marked parent specimen one counts 109 spots, on that of the most advanced young animal 106 spots. Yet this latter has only been under the different conditions for 1¼ years, whereas the former had been so for over 4 years. The average spot-number, obtained from 100 normal, typical specimens, amounts on the other hand on the upper side to 20—25, and on the belly-side the difference between them and the moisture-animals would be far greater still.

In a medium-moist environment (60—75% relative humidity) the "wet" marking-type is likewise retained to such an extent that on superficial inspection no weakening at all is noticed as compared with the strongly moist culture. Only when one applies oneself to counting the spots does one obtain, in the medium moisture, a lower average. The most extremely spotted specimen possesses 97 spots on the back-side, the least-marked 88 spots.

In the relatively dry environment, however (40—50% humidity), the counter-induction is too strong. Very quickly there begin to form, therefore, in the young animals within their spots the familiar melanin-accumulations that darken the yellow, and given the smallness of the spots it does not last long until a great quantity of them—being thus filled in while remaining small—becomes discoloured. Within about 8 months this culture has then lost its original character, which had at first reappeared by inheritance.

A secondary bilaterality such as in the experiments with coloured floors—that is to say, here, corresponding to an increase of the yellow, 2—4 longitudinal rows of yellow spots—has nowhere come to light in the offspring of the moisture-experiment; evidently that increase was too slight for this; but perhaps the specific transmission of the small- and many-pointed marking-type, which could be reconciled with it only ill, also plays a part.

### 2. Dryness.
*(Plates XIII, XV, Figs. 19—23.)*

I chose young animals with many and large spots, set them on dry sand. On the upper side the changes here proceeded, as a rule, more rapidly than on the under side. They consist in a darkening of the spots without appreciable loss in their size. When here a spot attains complete disappearance, this happens not because it is constricted ever further from the margin and on this path is finally reduced to nil; rather, in the spot-surface itself there arise, quite in isolation, melanin-grains, which along all paths finally condense and in this way finally displace the yellow pigment (Plate XV, Fig. 23*a*). The macroscopic aspect cannot be better characterized than if one says: the spot seems to sink into black depth, to submerge in the black, until one no longer sees it. The following cases of this process are possible: the more frequent case is when the melanin multiplies evenly over the whole surface of the yellow spot, so that the entire spot at first appears as though flown over by a faint dusk, then ever gloomier, finally so gloomy that scarcely traces of the yellow remain. But it can also happen that at some point of the spot-centre the multiplication of the black pigment is especially brisk, so that the gapless joining-together of the melanin-grains comes about there sooner than more peripherally. Then, at this stage, we have gloomy-yellow spots with a black middle-ocellus, that is, eyespots with a dirty-yellow border before us. The black middle-field can then somewhere break through to the black ground-colour of the spot-surroundings; the circular yellow spot-halo has then become a horseshoe, half-circle, quarter-moon; and, finally, the spot-forms not infrequent in salamanders caught in the open have here arisen in quite another way than in the moisture-experiment, where they came about through projection-formation from a small yellow dot. In general it must further be said of this culture that the yellow pigment knows how to defend itself very stubbornly against its definitive extirpation, even within one and the same spot. The characteristic of the culture therefore consists far less in this, that very many of the initial spots have disappeared, than rather in this, that in particular all the in any way large spots remain, but display a very dirty grey-yellow, which scarcely retains any more resemblance to the original luminous colour. This effect is especially beautifully to be observed when one begins the experiment, as I did with only a few specimens, not with the *forma typica* but with the regularly and broadly striped var. *taeniata*. There result then gloomy-striped salamanders. If, then, it could be asserted of the moisture culture that, despite numerous spot-multiplication, it does not confer upon the animals subjected to it the appearance of generally richly marked specimens, so it may be said of those of the desiccation culture that at the close of the experiment they usually do not really act upon the observer as sparsely marked, predominantly black salamanders. And as in the wet culture the properly characteristic thing consists in this, that the yellow can come to development everywhere upon the skin-surface, not merely (as on yellow paper) out from already existing yellow zones; so the dry culture is characterized by this, that that unrestricted possibility of origination now holds for the black colour-substance, which is multiplied not (as on black paper) out from black regions already existing in any case, but can be generated at any arbitrary place. Within the unlike-coloured territory this naturally comes more easily to perception, whereas within the like-coloured (this time black) essentially only a condensation of the colour-substance-elements, scarcely macroscopically demonstrable, takes place. Microscopically it is easily demonstrable if one—animals undergoing metamorphosis, or also older, fasting ones (the former do not yet carry the pigment so densely, the latter underwent a loosening of it)—sets onto dry ground.

Especially the spot-darkening under dryness can, in consequence of this unevenness of the total process, lead to error, as though, when one now sees, e.g., several spots already dirty-coloured but the rest still pure-yellow, a **qualitative** differentiation between them had set in, or as though in general (when, for instance, the experiment is broken off prematurely) a part of the spots remained unaffected; but it is a matter, as has been said, only of **quantitative** temporal differences in consequence of the gradually unequal possibility of attack on the part of the effecting factor.

Herewith there explain themselves, without forcing, all the phenomena of the morphological colour-change observed on the various kinds of substrate (compare, e.g., what hitherto remained unexplained in the description, pp. 34, 52). And one further conclusion may already be ventured from the hitherto available factual material: as we saw, the phenomena observed on the coloured papers and in the media varied with respect to their moisture supplement one another in such a way that together they reproduce the totality of the phenomena which take place on the earths. Reaction of the yellow paper plus moisture gives the reaction of the loam-earth; reaction of the black paper plus dryness gives that of the garden-earth. Other phenomena, deviating from these or not observed there, the colour change on the kinds of earth does not contain. **Therefore it is also not probable that factors other than colour and moisture are to be held responsible for the established colour-displacements in the earths.** Their chemical, molar, and thermal differences are, for the increase or decrease of one of the two pigments out of which the colour-dress of the fire-salamander is chiefly composed, either ineffective, or they do not suffice to produce, by our means, controllable differences therein.

In two respects the investigation of the colour change has here nevertheless remained gap-ridden; we do indeed know the external conditions that call it forth, and are able to let the process run at will. In fact it would scarcely be conceivable to get the variations of a character better into one's hand than the quantity and form of the yellow drawing-elements in the foregoing experiments; I can at will produce salamanders with longitudinal stripes and transverse bands, with symmetrically and asymmetrically placed, large and small spots; such as bear a longitudinal band in the middle, two to four at the sides, or irregular, crooked, wave- and zigzag-lines, letter-like flourishes; such in which all the drawing-forms, be they stripes or spots, look pure- or dirty-yellow; and I can finally determine fairly exactly the extent to which all this is to be manifest.

A closer causal connection between the factors of the external world and the changes produced by them in the animal we shall indeed, then, not be able to attain so soon; this and that condition must come about if this or that pigment is to increase. That is for the time being an axiom beyond which we cannot go. But the processes running their course **in the inner world of the animal** during the change-process are still accessible to us for closer investigation. In a word: our hitherto existing experiments furnish no physiological explanation of the phenomenon. That is the one respect in which the experiments to be described in what follows had to intervene supplementarily.

Then it would up to now not be exactly decidable whether the effects observed on yellow and black earth, isolated on yellow and black paper, represent **specific colour-effects** or are owing only to the **different light-intensity**. Ščerbov (1912a) has established by measurement that the yellow experimental paper reflects back ¹⁄₂₀ of the incident light, the black ¹⁄₆₀. And in the concluding passage of his treatise written in Serbian (1912c) he infers from this difference in light-intensity that it "could be assumed as the cause of the effects." Up to now there speaks against this conjecture the fact that both kinds of effect do not set in on substrates coloured otherwise than black and yellow; just as much, that for their attainment a high intensity of the incident over-head light is in every case required. As already emphasized in Chapter II, "Technique" (p. 15), the experiments succeed no longer if the terraria stand in the second tier instead of on the topmost supporting surface of the racks. Were the colour change only a function of the light-intensity — so that the black underlay would thus merely amount to a weaker intensity — then all salamanders exposed to twilight would have to become increasingly black, which, however, is by no means the case. Rather, their original spot-complement remains preserved. At all events, the blinding- and light-exclusion experiments to be discussed shortly serve for the definitive refutation of that explanation; these were not undertaken expressly on that account, but because they promised much information besides, and merely had to bring along with them, incidentally, the answering of the question now raised.

Before I pass over to their description, I must still briefly, as it were only in parenthesis, take into consideration an observation which appeared to some extent on the sands used in the moisture-experiments, but which I did not mention there in order not to complicate the presentation unnecessarily. For, as soon turned out, it — which at first caused me not a little perplexity — has nothing whatever to do with the moisture-reactions, but only with the light, almost white colour of the river-sand employed.

### F. White grounds.

Several times, and last of all only in the concluding paragraph of the previous experiment, it was emphasized that **yellow and black alone** bring about the effects, described in detail, upon the colour-dress of the salamander, whereas, on the contrary, all other grounds are to be regarded as **neutral** in respect of their colour.

Among the neutral grounds, sand too had been repeatedly enumerated. And it is indeed such, as far as the quantity and spread of the black pigment is concerned, so that consequently also the yellow drawing-elements interspersed between them neither may nor can change in their number nor in their extent and form.

But since the sand employed in the moist-dry experiments — originating from the Danube — was of a very light, almost white colour, there nevertheless went on a certain change with the yellow spots, which indeed in fact brought with it no displacement in their quantity and shape, but modified the tone of the pigment, which otherwise remained constant. After a more than year-long sojourn on the said substrate it could scarcely be called yellow any longer, so strongly had it been bleached out.

Does this already make it more than probable that the phenomenon was only noticed by me, by chance, precisely in the moisture-experiments first, but in fact has nothing whatever to do with the moisture itself (otherwise it would surely scarcely have appeared in both extremes of moisture), I still did not content myself with this, but set up two cultures, of which the one used the snow-white glass-sand purchasable for aquarium purposes, the other white paper in an empty glass vessel (without a sand underlay) as substrate.

Had the fading occurred only on the glass-sand, but not on the white paper, then one could have concluded from this that the tactile (molar) stimuli, which are indeed mediated in high degree by the sand-grains, had something to do with it; but the effect was entirely concordant in both cases, in both moreover far more beautiful, more marked, than on the not-so-purely-white river-sands. The yellow spots now in fact retained only a scarcely perceptible tinge toward the yellowish, looking — without contrast of the white underlay and without very close inspection — really white. Had

> *(footer:) Archiv f. Entwicklungsmechanik. XXXVI. 6* the culture lasted longer — it was conducted for only 1½ years — then perhaps total white-becoming would have been attained.

Thereby it appears proved that the change goes back to the very bright soil as its cause. And here — demonstrably not in my other experiments — the explanation is presumably to be given by the light-intensity, not by specific colour-adaptation. It is clear that the so strongly reflecting white soil, at the same height and in the same space where, under equal conditions, the remaining Salamander colour-experiments also stand (on yellow, black soil, the moisture experiments etc.), had to supply by far the strongest light-intensity. If, then, the Salamanders living on non-white soils did not show the fading, although equal quantities of overhead light penetrated into their containers, this comes about because on the white soil far more reflected rays are added than on the others, so that the light-intensity is summed. If the terraria fitted out with sand and white paper stand one compartment lower on the étagère, where the specific colour-effects (but not yet the moisture-effects) also cease, then the spots remain unchanged not merely with respect to extent, quantity and shape, but also with respect to their saturation, however bright the soil may be, even pure white.

Quite recently I have exposed Salamanders that do not live on white soils, in empty glass vessels, to a still stronger light-intensity than is present in the experimental rooms for the colour-adaptation experiments. I exposed them, namely, without the possibility of creeping away, daily for some time (varying according to the prevailing temperature) to the direct rays of the sun under the open sky. As a consequence of this I obtained already in the course of a single spring and summer, even in those specimens which did not fall victim to this — for Salamanders somewhat violent — treatment, but were healthy at the end of the experiment, the same spot-fading as before. Hereby I believe I have now definitively shown that the fading of the drawing on white or nearly white substrates has nothing to do with the other phenomena brought to light in my experiments — which, so far as specific colour-effects are concerned, represent genuine adaptations (cf. pp. 23 and 168) — and that it can be eliminated from the discussion in what follows, just as I had already neglected it in the description of the moisture-experiments, where by chance I first became aware of it.

Only this one thing do I wish to remark about it, that one would go astray in seeing in the fading a destruction of the pigment-cells in question through too much light. Microscopically, the pigment-granules present themselves quite unchanged, only just no longer so deeply yellow. And furthermore, every destruction of the yellow pigment has as its consequence an advance of the black, which forthwith strives to fill up the gaps that have arisen, and conversely, a perishing of the black has the spreading of the yellow as its consequence. The spot-region could not remain so constant in the bleaching, if the latter were equivalent to a destruction of the chromatophores.

## G. Darkness and Blinding.

The experiments — to rear Salamanders under complete exclusion of light in the (only quite transitorily, for control purposes, red-illuminated) dark-chamber, as well as to deprive a further party of them of their power of sight by blinding and then to keep these too both in the light and without light — had hovered before me very early on as inescapably necessary supplements to the remaining colour-change experiments; nonetheless their fortunate execution is a result of comparatively most recent date. Still during the Graz Zoologists' Congress (1910 — see the proceedings of the Congress, KAMMERER 1911/12) I was able to enter into the matter only with a quite general remark, as answer to a discussion-question; and only when I returned from that same Congress out of Graz to Vienna had the cultures concerned in the meantime got going so well that, in publishing the Graz lecture in the journal for inductive theory of descent and heredity, I was able to express myself, according to the roughest outlines, more definitely, but still very cautiously, with explicit reference to the fact that the communicated results still very much require confirmation.

In the meantime this too has been made good. I have improved my methods so far that today I no longer need fear the great mortality of the dark- and blind-animals — this was the cause of the obstinate failure of the experiments. But it will be instructive to touch, with a few words, upon the causes of the mortality, which in the blinding-experiments were other than in the dark-experiments. In the blinding-experiments I had chiefly to contend with terrible infections, although I of course sterilized the operating instruments before

> *(footer:) 6\** each use; but in the expectation that the animals, in the empty glass vessels in which they were always accommodated immediately after the operation, would not be exposed in the region of the eyes to any contact with pus-pathogens, I had omitted to disinfect the vessels, and furthermore had evidently set the stay of the operated animals there too short, in the endeavour to give them back as soon as possible (for their relief as for mine) to the natural milieu, the earth, the moss and the fresh vegetation. Finally, the animals used for the operation were too old, and the extirpation of both bulbs on one and the same occasion had been too great a demand. So then strong bleeding set in, the wound did not heal, but began to suppurate strongly, the whole eye-socket region to swell mightily, and after few weeks — one might say, despite everything only after weeks — all the experimental animals were again and again corpses.

I removed the deficiencies here enumerated only successively, always only one after the other, because each time I already hoped to have done enough, whereas they were all to be removed before success could be reckoned upon. So it is today completely comprehensible to me why precisely here I was afflicted by such failures. A further, wholly unexpected difficulty, however, which I now still have to add for the blinding-experiments, is identical with that which frustrated the dark-experiments for me for such a very long time — I can therefore discuss it for both experimental series in common.

One would not hold it possible, in so shade- and twilight-loving, sun-shy an animal as the Salamander is, that in the blinded state, even after the wounds had long since healed, but further also in the wholly uninjured state, it should starve in absolute darkness, but, when dry-culture was applied, should already much earlier dry up, because it could not be moved either to seek out the food-dish, or the feed-animals creeping about freely in the container, or the water-basin. The animals simply remained — as soon as they did not see, be it now because they were robbed of their eyesight or were altogether shut off from daylight — lying quietly, no longer concerned themselves about anything, starved, dried up. Thus it proves that a certain, even if slight, quantity of light is absolutely necessary in order to let the Salamanders pursue their vital instincts and functions, and this circumstance may to those serve for reassurance who did not wish to believe that the Salamanders would ever voluntarily expose themselves sufficiently on the coloured soils, or to the colour-effect brought about by the latter. Since, through the failure of the dark-experiments — discussed as to its causes — I know that the vital energies of the Salamander too can be awakened and kept going only by a certain quantity of light, this insight vouches to me that the colour-induction takes place also in those containers where I almost never get to see the inmates at ordinary times of day.

The peculiar state of lethargy into which the Salamanders living wholly in darkness or blinded fall might perhaps gain some explanatory value also for the phenomenon of the winter-sleep, which the Salamanders, as is well known, await and pass through beneath the surface of the earth in dark hiding-places. In other poikilothermic winter-sleepers too, e.g. Chelonians, Lacertilians, I made similar experiences, namely that a kind of latency-state is brought about when the animals do not receive the measure of radiant energy customary and wholesome to them. If then, as a further cause of the torpor, there is added the lowering of the temperature (in nature, indeed, always going hand in hand with the decrease of light-intensity), which slows the metabolism still further, then the resting-periods occasioned by both factors in common can be survived. In my Salamanders artificially placed into darkness without a fall of temperature, the latter naturally could not be the case.

I finally helped myself in the following simple but laborious way: in the experiments where the soil had to be kept dry, the animals were bathed daily, in moister milieus only three times weekly, simply thrown into the water-basin and after about 5 minutes taken out again. And in all experiments artificial feeding by stuffing took place 2—3 times weekly: the animal, which must not previously have been in the water, because the wetness makes it slippery and permits no firm, sure grip, is taken into the left hand in such a way that the index finger comes to lie uppermost, the thumb at the throat. Then a slight pressure of the thumb (pull of the throat-skin adhering to it) backwards (upon the larynx) suffices to draw the lower lip down a little; hereby a small opening of the mouth arises, large enough to push in a mealworm. If this has happened, one at once releases the animal, which will in most cases swallow the morsel pushed into its maw. Mealworms — apart from the fact that, for the reasons discussed in the chapter "Technique" (pp. 12, 13), they must constitute the exclusive food of our experimental animals — are most easily taken in this way, because by virtue of their hard body-covering they let themselves be pushed in most conveniently (head foremost), and because they are small morsels. It is completely sufficient if at each stuffing-manipulation a single mealworm is worked in, so that the animal is thus supplied with a food-quantum of 2—3 mealworms in the week.

### 1. Exclusion of Light.

It was my endeavour to fashion the keeping of the Salamanders without light entirely parallel to the keeping in the light described hitherto, i.e. to let all the experimental series run, with the same starting material, in containers of like constitution as to size, construction and inner arrangement (factor-isolation etc.), in the dark-chamber too, wherein the approximately concordant temperature conditions in the dark-chamber and the bright experimental room supported me. On the other hand, two concessions had to be made on account of the space conditions: firstly, not all experimental series could be begun at the same time; but since I at least always set up the closer parallel-experiments (yellow earth — black earth; yellow paper — black paper; wetness — relative dryness) simultaneously, the overall result is presumably scarcely prejudiced. Secondly, smaller container-types had occasionally to be drawn upon, partly because the large ones were for the most part occupied, and then in order to manage with the available narrow space of the dark-chamber. Finally, the dark-experiments are further distinguished from the light-experiments in that in the former there is nowhere any offspring, their obtaining not even being intended, since it would have brought no further information. Otherwise the whole arrangement is the same as discussed hitherto.

The concordant, unitary overall result can easily be expressed in a single sentence: all the reactions which in the light had set in so precisely, failed to appear in the darkness. That state of coloration which distinguishes the young Salamander brought into the darkness at the beginning of the experiment remains permanently preserved unchanged. Neither the yellow nor the black pigment increases, but neither of the two is, either, destroyed within the previous 3 years, so as either to grant the other free scope for more rapid increase, or to leave behind pigment-poor or pigment-free places. I by no means wish to deny that precisely the latter (pigment-destruction) might still have set in over a far longer time; within 3 years, which after all suffice to call forth quite distinct reactions in the light, it has at any rate not happened.

Thus do things present themselves when I draw the final result from the totality of the experiments. On more detailed consideration I must make the following restriction: quite without restriction, without any reservation, the proposition holds definitely of the colour-effects, that is, in particular, of the cultures on differently coloured paper. Likewise, on the yellow and black earth, there fails to appear there the enlargement, here the diminution of the spots (or of the stripes, if we had used var. *taeniata*) — there thus fails to appear, even under the action of the complex factors, that which we had recognized and isolated as colour-effect.

On the other hand — I must here still express myself very reservedly — it makes upon me the impression as though a quite weak moisture-reaction had set in. Certainly, in the light-cultures, with such little distinct results, I should never have ventured to ascertain an induction-effect, but should have regarded the observed differences as accidental, conditioned by hereditary tendencies brought into the experiment, that is, racial differences. And only the unmistakable parallelism of the processes in the dark-chamber with those of the bright room, and their characteristic distribution upon experiment and control-experiment, determines me to put it forward as probable that the reaction to wetness and dryness which has become known to us does not quite fail to appear even under complete exclusion of light, only is incomparably weaker and slower than in the light.

There appear, namely, in the dark-Salamanders kept on loam-earth and on wet sand — by no means in all specimens, but on the contrary in a minority of the experimental animals — isolated new little yellow spots in point-form, without enlarging themselves further beyond this (thus not even up to the form of a small circle, up to the drop-form). Still more indistinct is the reciprocal result: a slight clouding of the spots, called forth by intercalated melanin, in a small portion of those living on black earth and on dry sand. But it requires a very exact comparison of the material at the beginning and at the end of the experiment in order to notice the said differences at all. Since now such trifling changes (as we have heard, especially the fresh appearance of spots) occur in the life-course of every Salamander, on whatever soil it must be kept — inasmuch as the newly-metamorphosed, and even still the one- to two-year-old Salamander, is by no means in full possession of its inherited stock of drawing — I leave open, after all, the possibility that the phenomena seen here do not go back to the inducing influence of the substrates. This eventuality, hereby left open, is rendered improbable only by the parallelism with which the phenomenon follows the experiments and counter-experiments; it becomes conceivable through the circumstance that in each culture only individual specimens show it.

## 2. Blinding.

With regard to the operative technique there is, apart from the usual aseptic treatment, only the following to say: it serves convenience if one does not seek to cut out the eyeball alone, but rather takes along, in its immediate surroundings, a piece of skin; admittedly nowhere near so large a one as UHLENHUTH required, in order to heal it in again at another place. I first cut through the skin (on the ether-narcotized salamander) with small, curved scissors in the narrow circumference of the eye (1 mm radial distance); then I grasp at an arbitrary point the skin connected with the eye by means of fine forceps and dissect it free up close to the bulbus, which is done by a single scissor- or scalpel-stroke. Now, if one pulls on that piece of skin with the forceps, the bulbus can be drawn rather far out of the orbit, and thereby gives convenient opportunity to sever it proximally, so that it thus falls out *in toto*. Only now does one loosen the skin completely at the remaining periphery of the circumcised, circular piece. In those experiments where blinding was done on both sides, I made a pause of 8 days between the extirpation of the left and the right eyeball; in the meantime the wound of the one side is healed, the animal somewhat recovered and somewhat strengthened through (if necessary, artificial) intake of nourishment.

In the period of the worst failures I resorted to yet another operative method, which I owe to Herr Prof. STEINACH, but later, after the extirpation of the bulbus had succeeded, no longer employed, since it seemed to me to offer no sufficient guarantee for my object. It is not difficult and is far better tolerated by the animals if one does not make the large wound necessary for the removal of the bulbus, but rather severs the opticus from the palate. For this, two scalpel- or scissor-cuts suffice, which one carries out on a single operative occasion on the narcotized animal: it is laid on its back upon a sterile underlay (glass plate), the lower jaw is pulled down with two spreading forceps, the upper jaw is held in its position by a weighted double hook. Then the cuts are made, the mouth at once closed, the animal brought back into its normal posture. Besides the advantage of an immediate two-sided dispatch of the operation and of lesser wounding, the method also offers the further one that, in view of the protected position of the wound in the interior of the oral cavity, it greatly lowers the possibility of infection. The animals so treated were simply admixed with those blinded from outside and reacted to the induction-effects just as these did, and are thus evidently to be regarded as actually equivalent to fully blinded ones. Only I should like once again, with reference to the experiences of UHLENHUTH, not to hold excluded a subsequent growing-together of the severed opticus stumps, whereby the eye would newly begin to function and the course of the experiment would be disturbed in an unwelcome, perhaps very confusing manner. In the appearance and the position of the eyeballs no change at all showed itself in salamanders thus operated: they presented completely the aspect of normal animals; whether they, had I not extended to them the feeding method of stuffing generally practised in these experiments, would have gone independently to the food-dish, I am unable to say. A special treatment of the wound after operation was in no case needful; it healed up without trouble of itself: in the animals with extirpated eyeball the new skin grows over the empty eye-sockets without interruption.

The experiments with blinded animals were so arranged that animals blinded on both sides were distributed, each one half, both in the light and in the dark chamber, onto the various soils. Yet the series of experiments with blinded animals — which thus run completely parallel to those with seeing ones — differ in that each experiment could be stocked only with a smaller number of specimens. The one-sidedly blinded ones — since no inheritance-experiment was planned and consequently no crossings were to be feared (voluntary propagation is in any case excluded at least in the totally blind ones — one could have attempted it only with artificial sperm-injection) — were placed into the same containers as those blinded on both sides, whereby containers could be economized, the results became in so far still more exact, in that both kinds of operation now lived still more objectively under equal conditions than if one set them up subjectively in separated terraria, and finally the contrasts came to be demonstrated still more distinctly.

I now come to the actual execution. On the one-sidedly blinded ones, where one indeed still has the general spot-marking before one, I could, for example, from a greater distance — one which still let the general spot-marking be seen very well, but not whether the specimen was in possession of its eyes — have a salamander selected on the floor-surface of the terrarium which appeared coloured quite as was proper to the experimental series in question (e.g. yellow earth, black earth); then I could predict that the animal possessed one well-preserved eye, and the prediction came true every time. For the one-sidedly blinded animals reacted, in degree as in essence, completely like normal ones that are in possession of both eyes. They are therefore of no further interest and may drop out of our further presentation.

The blinded salamanders, on the other hand, attain great significance for the understanding of the process of adaptation: to be sure, in the dark these too do not differ, or [differ] somewhat more sluggishly; that is to say, they show absolutely no colour-reaction and only a moisture-reaction persisting within the limits of experimental error, hence an indistinct one. Quite otherwise the blind animals exposed to the ordinary, intense overhead light.

These likewise show no colour-reaction: of an enlargement of their marking-elements (spots or stripes) on yellow earth, yellow paper, of a diminution of the same on black earth, black paper, there is not a trace to be noticed; the stage at the beginning of the experiment is found unchanged at the conclusion of the experiment. — All the more abundant in the daylight is the moisture-reaction. On all wet substrates, hence on loam-earth, soaked garden-earth and moist sand, there is accomplished undiminished the multiplication of the existing spots by black dot-form (Tüpfelform), which at most advance to drop-form without lobe-formation, but then come to a standstill in growth.

And on all dry substrates, hence on garden-earth, dried-out loam-earth and on dry sand, there is accomplished — at the same tempo and to the same extent as in seeing specimens — the darkening of the existing spots by black dots within the region of the connected yellow pigment.

From the results of the darkness- and blinding-experiments the following may now be jointly inferred: the specific colour-effect of the substrate, the actual colour-adaptation, if we may so say, comes about only in the light, can be established only in the light, and is mediated by the photoreceptive special-organ, the eye, in further consequence therefore by the central nervous system — quite as has long since been established for the physiological colour-change of so many animals, in particular of the fishes and crustaceans. The effect of moisture upon the morphological colour-change, however, is an independent function of the skin; yet in order that it may come about to the full extent, a certain quantity of light is likewise required, for in the dark chamber it fails to appear save for slight rudiments.

Accordingly, for all colour-changes the intensity-effect of the light is the presupposing, activating (— ROUX) factor. For the one part-phenomenon (enlargement, diminution of the marking-elements) the colour-effect of the light is then the determining (determinative, specific — ROUX) factor; for the other part-phenomenon (isolated new-emergence of pigments within the region of the respectively other pigment) it is the moisture in its two extremes.

Before I turn to the presentation of the next experiment, I must discuss the possibility of an error in the assessment of the results of the darkness- and blinding-experiment, which lay near at hand and made itself felt for a time. As has been repeatedly emphasized, the young salamander, but recently transformed, does not yet possess the full measure of its yellow marking, neither in respect of the number, nor of the size and intensity of its spots or stripes. The very young salamander is therefore always blacker than the grown one, if it does not experience a reduction of the yellow pigment conditioned by counter-inductions, hence a so to speak "contrary-to-nature" one. While, then, normal salamanders in the course of their life, beginning from the moment of metamorphosis, receive a smaller or a greater increase of marking, in the one placed in darkness or blinded salamander the state of marking remains stationary from this moment onward. We here leave out of account an eventual moisture-reaction and consider only the specific colour-reaction.

Let one now bring before oneself the following case: freshly transformed, or at any rate very young, fire-salamanders are reared on yellow and on black paper; the one party in the light, the other in the dark; or the one party sees, the other is blinded, both in the light. Then indeed only the seeing ones held in the light on yellow paper will increase in yellow pigment; all the other series of experiments will resemble one another — except that the regressive process sets in vigorously in the seeing little animals held in the light on black paper. In a conduct of the experiment that extends over a longer time than about 2 years, the erroneous impression could now arise as if keeping on black soil or keeping in the dark (or blinding) were completely equivalent, as if these three kinds of cause had the same effects in their train (the yellow marking remaining restricted, predominant black colouration of the animal), whereas in truth it is a fundamentally different (although in literature and experiment often confused) thing whether an animal lives on a black underlay and is at the same time brightly illuminated, or whether it lives in absolute darkness.

Since blinding-experiments in particular will as a rule not extend over a longer period than 2 years, but these, with juvenile starting-material, do not suffice for the assessment of the contrasts, I have repeated my blinding-experiments many times precisely with half-grown animals, and indeed with such as had abundant flecking. Here the said duration of experiment suffices to let the differences come out distinctly; the blinded ones remain richly marked, hence still look, as before, especially yellow; the seeing ones on a black underlay have at the same time either wholly or experienced a considerable loss of their spot-size, and under circumstances also of their spot-number.

### H. Temperature experiment.

In my work of 1904 there is found the statement that high temperature favours the yellow marking of *Salamandra maculosa* [*Salamandra salamandra*], that low temperature suppresses it. In Chap. III ["Normal Colouration," p. 27] of the work now before us, I had, however, already corrected this statement of mine to the effect that no specifically determining influence upon the colour-pattern belongs to temperature, but rather that warmth always favours only that very marking-type which the animal in any case strives to attain, whereas cold inhibits its attainment. Specimens which as juveniles were weakly flecked can therefore, to be sure, become strongly flecked under the influence of higher degrees of temperature, but the warmth acted only as a stimulant, as a reaction-accelerator — in an incomparably longer time the same animal would have become predominantly yellow even without the increase of temperature. And on the other hand it can come about in an analogous way that a specimen, at the outset tolerably richly marked, likewise under the influence of warmth, loses the greatest part of its marking and then appears predominantly black. It would, without the increase of temperature, have come just as far in the course of time.

From this present result of my continued investigation one can perceive how important it is that all, or at any rate nearly all, specimens of one and the same experiment show themselves altered in the same direction; but further also that the control-experiments conducted by me (in 1904 I had not yet worked so exactly in this respect as today) completely suffice to distinguish what is to be ascribed to the race and what to the external factor. I remark this especially with regard to the ever-recurring objection that the changes observed by me could lie within the normal range of variation and reaction-norm of the species, and that it could therefore be merely a matter of isolation of pure lines which were in any case already contained in the population used for the experiment. It would therefore unconditionally have to be supplied by me [as a demand] that I work with pure lines (biotypes) as starting-material. Whoever objects this, does not, in the first place, know my works: for he ignores the control-experiments; in the second place he does not know the object, otherwise he would have to know that with the salamander, within normal experimental durations (mine are in any case already above-normal), it is not possible to breed "pure lines." For it takes at least 3½ years before the salamander even becomes sexually mature, and then it is far from said that it must promptly propagate, without perhaps waiting yet a little year or two more. Added to this is the complicatedness of the character itself, with respect to which "pure lines" are demanded.

More precisely, the following ways stood open to me to test the influence of temperature upon the colour-dress: I could indeed bring [the animals to] higher than normal temperatures (30° — but warming gradually!), and the normal culture is then furnished by the room where the remaining breeds are situated (see Chap. II "Technique") and in which the temperature fluctuates, but at least toward the upper end always remains within moderate limits. An actual cold-culture as the extreme counterpart to the warmth-culture I was unable to set up, because at that time the relevant installations were still lacking at our institution. The soil in these pure temperature-experiments was chosen neutral; gravel, covered with moist moss. As starting-material there could serve either arbitrary salamanders caught in the open; then the result was, as I have already described it, namely after the lapse of about 3 experimental years both more yellow and very black specimens in one and the same experimental container, but none of middling marking. Each had, precisely, brought to highest unfolding the marking-tendency immanent in it. This experimental variation was undertaken with abundant material; and I too could see again that the recolouration proceeds the more rapidly the warmer, but not only [the warmer], but also the lighter it is, and the younger the animals.

For a second, pure temperature-experiment I employed, to be sure, only isolated animals, but such of known descent. Here both extremes did not occur in one and the same culture, but rather all specimens that descended from predominantly black parents likewise became predominantly black, all offspring of yellow ones predominantly yellow. Whether the black, respectively yellow, in the parents was already taken over in life in the open — hence presumably for the greatest part innate — or was first induced in the experiment, is all one. From the same final result in moderate, fluctuating temperature these warmth-cultures differed only in that they attained the same colour-state, within each one constantly directed, in incomparably shorter time. The differences amounted to as much as 12 months.

Finally, the experiments could also be set up in such a way that yellow and black, wet and dry soils — as they were in any case employed in the room with moderate temperature-conditions — now also came into use in the heat. The result corresponded to the previous one. In each case that reaction set in which corresponded to the soil, only the extreme state was reached in ⅔ to ½ of the time that was needed in the cooler room. The specimens living on yellow earth thus had, already after 2 years, spots just as large as analogous specimens in the cooler milieu had only after 3—4 years; those on wet sand just as many dots, etc.

I also thought of the possibility that differently coloured backgrounds — red, blue, green, violet paper underlays, which remained ineffective in the general experimental room — might perhaps, with the help of warmth, yield a colour change; for it could indeed be that the reaction, which fails to appear at a moderate temperature, only fails to take effect because of its exceedingly great slowness within the time spans available to us. This possibility, however, did not apply: even at a high temperature it is those colours which the animal itself possesses, yellow and black, that are the only ones which bring about a shifting in its colour pattern. The changes proceeding upon differently coloured backgrounds therefore resemble those which I reported just now from the moss-covered gravel floor; in the end both extremes are represented in every container, very poorly and very richly marked salamanders, and the transitional members are lacking.

### J. Hunger and Surfeit.

On the occasion of the report on how my totally black *Salamandra maculosa* came about, I had already remarked, by way of anticipation, that it is possible, through hunger, or rather through scanty nourishment, to inhibit the development of the yellow pigment. The application of hunger in that experiment, where all the factors known to me had to work together in order to suppress the yellow pigment, was based on the experiment now to be described, in which **food deficiency** and **food surplus** were isolated.

It was begun with very young, recently metamorphosed salamanders, of which I already knew, from earlier observations made on occasion, that they react very strongly with growth differences to abundant or to too scanty feeding. But since I neither knew nor suspected the influence of nutrition upon pigment distribution, it was impossible to expose negatively selected exemplars to the two extremes; I had to content myself with selecting moderately (but, of course, not equally) spotted ones, of which one party received only a single full-grown (i.e. apparently standing before the pupal moult) "mealworm" **weekly**, the other party three such daily. The feeding was carried out artificially, by stuffing, as described in the dark experiment, because one could not, at least not with certainty, have administered such a quantity to the surfeit culture by natural means, and because the application of the same method to the hunger culture then already recommended itself for the sake of equality of conditions. So long as the salamanders were too small to swallow the mealworm standing before pupation in one piece, it was halved and offered in two portions (the one head first, the other with the hind-body end foremost). The floor was, it goes without saying, chosen neutral (gravel, covered with fresh moss).

The final result after 3 years consisted in this, that the surfeit culture was marked far more richly yellow than the hunger culture. The latter was even very sparsely spotted, and accordingly looked predominantly black. Yet, taken as a whole, the contrasts were smaller than between a culture set up in just the same way and of just the same age, where, instead of hunger and surfeit, coloured backgrounds had been applied. The spots present were in both experiments fairly pure yellow.

Accordingly the conclusion would lie near at hand, that we have found a new specific factor, which in the one extreme, hunger, analogously to a black background, promotes the increase of the melanin, in the other extreme, surfeit, analogously to a yellow background, promotes the increase of the yellow pigment. Were this conclusion correct, then the result would also signify a certain contradiction to experiments of Tornier on tadpoles, who, with abundant feeding, saw melanism set in, and with scanty feeding, in consequence of a wasting-away of the pigments, according to the degree, erythrinism or albinism.

But the conclusion suggested by the feeding results on the salamander is not correct, and in truth, upon microscopic examination of the pigmentation states, Tornier's and my results agree completely. Hunger, namely, **inhibits the development of both pigments coming into question here**, only this happens to an unequal degree, in that the yellow pigment is more strongly affected by it. The black pigment is able, although likewise weakened in its vitality, still to increase at stages of starvation at which the yellow already falls prey to resorption. Thus it comes about that in the hunger culture it [the black] is still able to fill out, almost everywhere, the gaps arising through the wasting-away of the yellow. Nevertheless, however, there are spots where this is the case to an insufficient degree, and where therefore the skin appears to the unarmed eye, instead of black, only brownish-black or brown, appearing, with a reddish shimmer in consequence of the now showing-through blood. Such spots are in particular the axillary cavities and the thigh-folds, the knee-hollow, the folds on the trunk sides, the throat sometimes as far as the breast, that is, the least-illuminated [parts], where the lack of illumination is also added, as unfavourable for pigment formation.

In the surfeit culture, however, good developmental possibility is given for both pigments. They now develop up to that degree which they would have attained under ordinary conditions, simply more rapidly — apart from a slight head start which the more rapidly increasing yellow now appears to gain over the black. The colour state of the culture, where it is stuffed to excess, therefore differs only little from that of a normal culture, and the contrast between hunger culture and surfeit culture is conditioned essentially only by the weakly, especially little-yellow, pigmented hunger culture.

Whether the view here brought forward, on the basis of the microscopic finding, concerning the manner of hunger's action is correct, was still accessible to a special experimental test. One could keep the scantily fed and the fattened animals on yellow and black ground. For this I used loam-earth and garden-earth, since it did not matter too much to isolate the colour and moisture effect, and since I did not want to impair, on top of everything, by the unnatural paper grounds, animals already much taxed by nutritional anomalies. The starving and the fattened ones I kept together, in order to economize on containers and thereby to level the conditions of these parallel cultures still better, in one container, that is, 10 starving and fattened ones each on yellow, 10 such ones each on black earth. In order to distinguish them, I oriented myself at first by the style of marking, but soon much more conveniently by the resulting growth differences. In this way four colour types arise:

1) The fattened animals situated on yellow ground have, after 3 years, become extremely yellow, for there is added to the rich feeding — which in and of itself, although increasing both pigments, gives the yellow a certain head start — the mighty influence of the yellow-coloured substrate.

2) The hunger animals situated on yellow ground show, in spite of the substrate, a gradual destruction of the yellow pigment, which perhaps advances somewhat more slowly than if they had been on neutral ground. From hunger animals on neutral ground, however, the present culture differs in a far more striking circumstance: the still viable melanin has filled out, very imperfectly, the gaps arisen through the perishing of the yellow, probably in consequence of an inhibitory effect of the yellow ground, and everywhere where formerly there was a yellow area there are accordingly, at the conclusion of the experiment, spots with only sparse melanin, which look brownish, greyish, or even reddish. There has thus come about a far-reaching similarity with partial albinos, such as one finds here and there in the open everywhere, as well as with the exemplars of the var. *molleri* described by E. G. Boulenger, which owe their rose-coloured spots to the local lack of pigment. Although I in general decline conclusions [drawn] between the constitution of the locality and the appearance of the animal, in view of the home of var. *molleri*, which certainly (speaking in the sense of a salamander) comprises not exactly hospitable regions of Spain and Portugal, a conjecture of a similar mode of coming-about as in the experiment may be permitted.

3) The surfeit animals situated on black ground are, as is to be expected, become extremely black, for there is joined to the abundant feeding, which favours the increase of the pigment, hence also of the black, in and of itself, the corresponding influence of the black-coloured substrate.

4) The hunger animals situated on black ground are likewise extremely poorly marked, but not all the places that were denuded by the destroyed yellow have become entirely black; rather many [have become] only incompletely, or not at all, re-pigmented: the former appear brownish-grey, the latter rose-coloured. In between are found all transitions: grey-overlaid rose, etc. The difference compared with the hunger animals kept on yellow earth consists in this, that in these the yellow pigment [is] relatively little destroyed, the black pigment inhibited; on the black earth, by contrast, the black pigment is indeed still engaged in increasing and advancing, but not sufficiently to fill out the gaps arising through the far-reaching regression of the yellow. Thus there come about there fairly extensively, here fairly sparsely, yellow-spotted salamanders, both with weakly pigmented and unpigmented spots.

That one can make use of the loosening of the pigments occurring in hunger animals in order to demonstrate microscopically the condensation of the yellow on wet, that of the black on dry ground, even within zones of like colour, where, with too dense a layering, it would be black and not at all noticeable macroscopically, has already been stated on p. 70 and p. 73 respectively. The hunger experiment shows more beautifully than any other experiment, better in particular than the experiments on colour-action alone, that the yellow ground does not merely promote the like-named pigment in its increase, and that the displacement of the black does not then come about only through lack of space or lack of food in the sense of a struggle of the particles, a histonal selection, but rather that the yellow ground, over and above this, independently of that, **inhibits the other pigments in their increase**. Correspondingly, the action of the black ground too is likely to be a twofold one — although not so clearly discernible from the hunger experiments —: promotion of the black and inhibition of the yellow (as well as of any other) pigment. Full evidence that this is so with respect to the yellow pigment, and a confirmation of the inhibitory action of yellow ground upon the black pigment, are first furnished by the following [experiments], the regeneration experiments.

### K. Regeneration of Skin Pieces.

In the regeneration experiments I set myself the question, **in what colour the salamander skin will grow back upon wounds**, when the animals live on neutral, on yellow and black, on wet and dry ground. In this it was evidently also still to be taken into consideration whether a removed piece of skin had originally been pigmented yellow or black or mixed. From this question the experimental arrangement followed of itself.

Young animals, but — for the sake of convenience in operating — no longer quite small ones, of various colour types, are more easily subjected to ether narcosis. For the narcotizing of salamanders the general rule holds, first, to prefer ether to chloroform, secondly, to take the animals out of the narcotizing-jar while they are still moving violently; the quiet necessary for operations then sets in nonetheless in the free air. Should it after all have been still too early — one observes in this respect great individual differences — it is better to expose the animal once more, briefly, to the ether vapours. But if it begins to move when the operation is already in progress (for the experiment now under discussion this possibility scarcely comes into consideration, but certainly for the gonad transplantations to be described later), then a linen cap soaked in ether, which is pulled over its head, serves to calm it. With some practice, losses through narcosis are soon excluded.

From this observation there emerges at the same time something that was to be noted in all regeneration experiments: the skin subjected to the new formation **takes on the colouring demanded by the respective ground more quickly** than does the primary skin, already brought along from metamorphosis as pigmented. Herein the parallel between regeneration and ontogenesis shows itself once more; for even when one — as Šećerov has done — exposes the larvae already during the development of the definitive pigment to coloured grounds, the same colour-states result, for the production of which the metamorphosed animal, because it must indeed first recolour, had needed years, within several weeks. Common to ontogenesis and regeneration here is that a recolouring is spared, in that the newly arising pigment can at once assume the constitution and distribution demanded by the substrate.

But **the colouring demanded by the substrate then also spreads over to the neighbouring parts that had not been wounded,** and in so far the regeneration of narrowly localized pieces of skin even signifies a certain acceleration of the whole process. A yellow patch, one half of which has grown back gloomy-yellow on dry ground, is now also more rapidly contaminated with melanin in the second half; a piece of black ground-colour which during regeneration in the wet took yellow pigment-islands into itself, radiates, so to speak, the capacity for this into its surroundings, which had likewise been pure black, and gives the latter a noticeable head-start over the remaining black body-parts, which only somewhat later, and withal always more slowly, comply with the like-directed promotion of the moist milieu. —

I stated earlier as a general rule for a neutral environment that after the regeneration and through the regeneration the colour-state before the regeneration is restored. In full measure, however, this holds only when **long-established colour-districts** are affected by the regeneration. But if a newly modified district has been removed at the operation, then that restoration on a colour-ineffective ground likewise takes place, yet **by a longer detour, which leads by way of a transient re-establishment of the original colour-state before the change.** Thus if one has removed a little piece of yellow skin which had previously been black and had only become yellow in the course of ontogenesis, then the regenerate at first takes the run-up to becoming black again; only then does a layer of yellow pigment form beneath the melanin-layer; there arises, as a second transitional stage, our well-known oil-green interference-colour, which finally lightens to pure yellow. The melanin-formation, and the gloom-darkening of the yellow that goes along with it, usually spreads quite transiently over the immediately neighbouring, yellow parts which are no longer subject to the regeneration-process. — If one has removed a little piece of black skin which had previously been yellow and had only become black with the advancing age of the animal, then the regenerated skin at first shows the striving to become yellow again; only then does black form between the yellow pigment in the same layer; there arises, as a second transitional stage, our familiar dirty-yellow colour, which finally darkens up to deep-black. Traces of yellow spread rapidly and transiently, mostly over the nearest-lying black parts which are no longer subject to the regeneration-process.

This account referred to neutral ground. **On a colour-effective ground** that colour-state which there was only a transitional stage is, as the case may be, either shortened, that is, almost suppressed, or, on the contrary, made persistent. If one removes a piece of yellow, previously black skin, and the animal lives during the regeneration-process on yellow ground, then black and yellow pigment arise in superimposed layering simultaneously, i.e. the first, totally black transitional stage is omitted, the second sets in at once, the oil-green patch, but in a very short time the pure-yellow end-stage is reached. — If one removes a piece of black, previously yellow skin, and the regeneration-process is again accomplished on yellow ground, then the regenerate becomes yellow and remains so; the recolouring into black no longer takes place at all. If one removes a piece of yellow, previously black skin on black ground, then the regenerate becomes and remains black. — If one removes black, previously yellow skin, likewise on black ground, then the first intermediate stage of the regenerate, furnished only with yellow pigment, is omitted; rather, it forms both pigments simultaneously in mixed layering; the gloomy-yellow patch arises at once, in order very soon to recolour into perfect black. Here I also saw cases where, but for negligible traces, no yellow at all arose in the regenerate, so that the second transitional stage too was as good as skipped over. — In all the described recolourings in the regenerate, which represent a faithful or shortened repetition of the corresponding ontogenetic process, it makes no difference perceptible to our means of observation whether the ontogenetic recolouring accomplished itself spontaneously, that is, signified only the adherence to the inherited pigment-development, or whether it was accelerated and intensified by raising of temperature, induced by coloured or moist grounds.

Twofold regeneration of the same piece of skin follows the same rules as a single one. This now also holds for recolourings which had been brought about by a colour-effective milieu during the first regeneration-process. A district of skin, then, which was black before regeneration, yellow after regeneration, undergoes, on twofold regeneration, the transitional stages just described from black to yellow, unshortened on neutral, with the indicated shortenings on colour-effective ground. Reciprocally, the same holds of a district of skin which had been yellow before its first removal and had become black after its first re-replacement.

It now offered some further interest how salamanders with regenerated and, on this occasion, recoloured skin would behave with respect to their offspring. A quite pure experiment of this kind could of course not turn out, since indeed always only individual districts of skin had been regenerated, the rest having acquired the like-directed recolourings without replacement-growth of the pigment-bearing tissue itself. Nevertheless it could be so carried out that one used animals which had little by little exchanged their entire dorsal skin and had thereby lived on a ground of purely isolated colour-effect, without moisture-induction. Here, indeed, the recolouring is confined almost exclusively to the more strongly lighted upper parts.

There stood at my disposal only a pair of the asymmetrically spotted forma typica, which on yellow paper (already with deliberate intent for the breeding-experiment) had regenerated the entire dorsal skin, deep down onto the flanks, and indeed almost exclusively in yellow colour, in that the removed pieces of skin were always so chosen that they lay partly on yellow, partly on black territory; thus the former always had opportunity to push itself forward into the latter. — The fertilization had to be carried out by sperm-injection, but it succeeded, and the female carried out 24 larvae on normal ground: the 17 young animals remaining after the dying-off of 7 larvae were, raised large on neutral ground, abundantly yellow and symmetrically marked, brought it to the formation of the little-interrupted, moderately broad longitudinal bands on the dorsal sides, rows of spots on the flanks — thus show sufficiently that the inheritance-process from the animal with regenerated-recoloured skin differs in nothing, at least qualitatively, from that of an ancestor with colour-change without an interposed regeneration.

## V. Transplantation experiments.

### A. Transplantation of pieces of skin.

Instead of, as in the previous experiments, leaving open the dorsal wounds arising from the dissecting-off of pieces of skin and leaving them to natural regeneration, in the series of experiments now to be discussed they were at once covered with other skin, taken from another specimen, and indeed differently-coloured skin. Operated upon now, therefore, are always two specimens simultaneously: a very yellow one (most conveniently a broad-banded one of the var. *taeniata*) and a very black one. In the same way as described in the regeneration-experiment, square or rectangular pieces of skin are removed from the backs of both, which here, however, because the wounds do not remain open, may be taken somewhat larger (12–25 sq. mm): yellow pieces of skin from the one, black ones from the other specimen, and, with interchange, brought to healing-in onto the laid-bare places of the respective other specimen. Besides single-coloured ones, spotted ones too, on which only the one colour had in each case to predominate, were used for exchange. The healing-in takes place without suture and without plaster: one need only take care to push the edges of the piece of skin to be transplanted, at least at two opposite places or along a whole side of the quadrangle, under the skin of the wound-edge. If the transplanted piece has by chance turned out exactly equal in size, or somewhat smaller, than the place destined for its reception and laid bare of original skin, then naturally, after that pushing-under, a minimal, strip-shaped gusset remains uncovered, wounded; but that does no further harm, this little piece may calmly be left to spontaneous healing, which takes place from the wound-edge, not seldom indeed already with the participation of the transplanted skin. A few drops of collodion prevent the edges of wound and transplant from rolling up, and hold the whole together so long until the latter is securely healed-in and can no longer fall off. If need be, the dripping-on of collodion is to be repeated on the second, third day after the operation. After at most 8 days the operated animals may move over from the aseptically treated glass tanks, in which they are at first housed, into the terraria, and indeed both kinds of "exchange-salamanders" without further ado in the same container, since they were indeed always recognizable by the colouring of the transplant or transplants. Thus for the diverse grounds I needed only a single terrarium each.

On neutral grounds (mostly gravel, covered with fresh-green, moderately moist-kept moss) the rule holds in full measure that grafted piece and surrounding body-skin do not influence one another. Transplanted yellow skin therefore remains yellow on the predominantly melanotic salamander, transplanted black skin remains black on the predominantly xanthorotic animal. If the skin-interchanges are repeated on the same animals at intervals of 1–2 months, then with the specimens escaping the (despite all precautionary measures great) danger of infection, it succeeds in making, out of predominantly black salamanders, by the way of transplantation, at least as regards the back, predominantly yellow ones; out of originally predominantly yellow ones, by the same way, ones with an almost black back. In this the animals do not present, as one might expect from the quadrangular form of the transplants, a regularly fielded, as it were pavemented aspect, but rather the [interlocking] of the wound- and graft-edges in healing-in, together with the circumstance that at least at the immediately abutting edges, over a breadth of at most a millimeter, but mostly only fractions thereof, a diffusion of the pigments comes about — so namely that a black wound-edge gets along some traces of yellow pigment from a yellow graft, and vice versa — efface the abruptness of the regular delimitations and restore, little by little, almost the accustomed aspect of an irregularly marked specimen covered with lobed spots, only somewhat scarry.

On wet, or respectively dry ground (sand) there then appear again, on the transplant as well as on the stock-piece, the moisture-effects known to us, i.e., therefore, there form, there in the black district, here in the yellow district, islands of the respectively differently-coloured pigment, which manifests itself to the unarmed eye, in the wet, as a new-arising of yellow points, in the dryness as a gloom-darkening of the yellow patches. In this respect it makes no difference at all whether transplanted or stock-skin is present: both are affected in fully concordant manner by the process which represents the moisture-effect upon the colour-dress.

On the other hand I was to a high degree surprised that the **colour-effect of light** upon the transplanted pieces failed completely until further notice, whereas it went on undisturbed at the marking-elements of the native skin of the same specimen which also bore transplants. Only after about 14–15 months' stagnation does the colour-effect set in suddenly, almost by jerks, upon the grafted skin-surfaces too. Let us consider this remarkable phenomenon on the individual grounds, isolated with respect to their coloured light-effect.

On yellow paper the yellow spots of the stock-skin enlarge in typical manner: where they strike upon a transplanted piece, they make a halt, as if here an obstacle were to be overcome, and at first push themselves further only along the colour-edge; but finally they do grow over the colour-tissue away into the black colour of the transplant, or respectively fuse with it, if it was yellow.
