Yet I have never observed the mutual, corresponding process-formation as introduction to fusion, such as is otherwise so characteristic between two yellow spots, between a yellow stock-spot and a yellow or yellow-spotted transplant: fusion here sets in only when uniform breadth-growth of the stock-spot has, in its whole extent, let it reach the scar-edge. — Yellow spots on the transplant, and totally yellow transplants, on the contrary persist on the yellow paper at exactly the same size and shape until 1 year and several months beyond have elapsed; only then does the enlargement of the yellow district begin on the graft-skin too, and it now makes no halt either at the scar-edges, but grows immediately into the black stock-territory, or respectively fuses with its yellow spots under typical, corresponding lobe-formation.

On black paper the blemishes of the stock-skin diminish, but not those of the graft-skin; only after as many months, after the lapse of which yellow graft-spots begin to enlarge on yellow paper, do they begin to diminish on black paper. Somewhat less than this time, namely not quite 1 year, is required for the black stock-skin to send its melanin into totally yellow graft-pieces and thereby make them two-coloured, under steady restriction of their yellow district. At first, however, the black stock-pigment too, like the yellow on yellow paper, makes a halt at the scar-edges, which separate it from the transplant-pigments.

On the soils, yellow loam and black garden-earth, it is finely to be seen how the moisture-effect sets in at once, but the colour-effect likewise only after about 1¼ years. Thus the transplantation-procedure too, just like the regeneration-procedure described earlier, furnished a good control for the fact that the colour-changes becoming visible on the earths were correctly resolved by the coloured papers and moist sands. It furthermore, through this temporal separation, furnishes a substitute for the experiments carried out by Šećerov and v. Frisch on their fishes with excised and isolatedly observed pieces of skin, which, in the case of the rapid physiological colour-change, show quite well what share in it is due to the independent sensibility of the skin, whereas they would not survive long enough for the slow morphological colour-change.

The interesting fact that the pure colour-effect first comes into appearance when a considerable time has elapsed since the operation, whereas the moisture-effect begins at once, in turn furnishes a usable revision for the **dark- and blinding-experiments**: it can namely (although I did not histologically investigate the scar-tissue between transplant and substrate at different times) scarcely be explained otherwise than in the following way: the transplanted skin heals in upon the wounded substrate prepared for it, i.e., it is soon supplied with capillary vessels, nourished with blood; but for the time being it does not yet enter into noticeable stimulus-conducting connection with the substrate-tissue. Much later than the blood-vessels, nerve-fibres grow from the surroundings into the grafted piece, and only now does it form, with the stock-piece, a unitary whole; only now can those excitation-effects come about which, as the blinding-experiments teach, manifest themselves solely through the mediation of the central nervous system. On the other hand those excitation-effects which, as likewise the blinding-experiments taught, run their course even in the absence of the photoreceptor organs, do not from the outset require the mediation of the nervous system, but only the general irritability of the protoplasm, which is therefore present also in the skin, where the moisture-stimulus that releases it is perceived.

Of the highest interest it would be to bring to reproduction salamanders that had exchanged their entire dorsal and flank skin by transplantation and had taken in almost exclusively skin of one single colour — either black or yellow — but which, in the meantime, lived in a neutral milieu, that is, underwent no colour induction whatsoever. As already lamented earlier, however, one does not keep alive precisely many specimens that received at least a large part of their dorsal skin in exchange. I was able to save only one little pair for the inheritance experiment in question, which belonged to the var. *taeniata* and had originally been yellow-banded already, but had now, through continued transplantation of black dorsal skin, become — from a very faintly marked forma typica — almost uniformly black. At the time when their isolation into a breeding container took place, the animals were certainly not yet sexually mature; the female therefore could not, for example, have already been fertilized by some other male in that container which it had shared with the yellow-grafted animals. By contrast I saw, when with the next-following rutting period (May 1909) sexual maturity had been attained, the desired copulation take place; the insemination had thus occurred by the natural route, it did not have to be carried out, as in the regeneration experiment, by artificial injection of the sperm. On the 16th of November of the same year the female then bore — in accordance with the gestation period of 6 months which I have established for *Salamandra maculosa* — 34 larvae, of which 8 died before metamorphosis. The remaining 26 allowed a, with respect to inheritance, completely negative result to be recognized: all belonged to the var. *taeniata*, whose characters their parents had displayed before the transplantations; they were at first spot-rowed, then interrupted, finally most of them already uninterruptedly longitudinally striped. The transplanted black skin accordingly, in this — unfortunately isolated — case, allowed every inheritance effect, every induction of the male as well as the female gonad, to be missed. Yet it must not be overlooked that, at the time of copulation, a considerable part of the black overgrafted skin pieces, judging by the elapsed time, could not yet have reached that stage described more closely earlier, where they enter into a stimulus-conducting connection with the rest of the body, as the symptom of which, on coloured grounds, the colour effects of light mediated through the eye would have begun.

As a more incidental remark, let it be added here that only **homoioplastic transplantations** (between different individuals of the same species) succeeded for me, whereby I took no account of the sex, that is — when it just happened to come my way — I also transferred skin from a male onto a female and vice versa, without noticing any difference in the outcome. **Autoplastic transplantations** (on the same individual) I did not carry out; they would certainly have succeeded just as well or better, but had no value for my purposes. By contrast I tried several times with **heteroplastic transplantations** between *Salamandra maculosa* and *atra*; these very ones invariably failed. Since possibly the colour might have been an obstacle (I wanted first to fasten yellow skin pieces of *maculosa* onto the back of *S. atra*), I contented myself, merely for the sake of the principle, with skin of the same colour (black pieces of *S. maculosa* on *S. atra*, and such of *S. atra* in the black region of *S. maculosa*), but likewise consistently without success. This is in accord with the hitherto, by every route (the natural as well as that of artificial fertilization), failed hybridization of the fire- and alpine salamander, which, even though probably sprung from a common ancestral form, nevertheless at present represent two well-separated species, "good species".

## B. Transplantation of Ovaries.

By no means new any longer is a favourite project of the experimental biologists, the age-old contested question of how the properties of the body come into the germ, [namely] to solve it by gonad transplantation. Whether the doctrine of preformation and determinants, or, on the contrary, pangenesis and epigenesis are right — in what simpler and more striking way could one demonstrate this than through the exchange of the germ-glands of two different pure races and the breeding of descendants from such animals as carry within themselves a foreign, but functionally capable, sexual organ? — Great, indeed, is already the number of experiments which more or less candidly admit to themselves this lofty goal; still ever small the number of the successful experiments, which, if one reckons in only the unimpeachable, unobjectionably usable successes, shrinks almost to nil. Usually the transplanted organ does not even attain functional capability at all: the investigation then confines itself to following histologically its lifespan and its tissue changes in the foreign place. Or young are indeed obtained from it, but they could possibly stem only **from remnants of the old, proper reproductive organ**, which were left behind at the operation or regenerated after the operation. If even that is not the case, then the races used for the exchange nevertheless meet with various doubts: they are not pure, or not different enough, at least in these two respects not tested by sufficiently numerous, controlling pure- and hybrid-breedings. That in fact one or another of these objections must, with justification, be applicable to a large part of the hitherto "successful" gonad transplantations, is shown already by the contradictoriness of their results: the one party declares itself for an influencing, a re-tuning of the foreign germ-plasm by the soma of the foster-nurse, the others (the most numerous and more exactly carried out) against it.

I will gladly refrain from setting out, on the individual works already lying before us in the literature, the possibility of the objections, all the more readily as I have already done so fairly exhaustively in another place (1911); here I should like to occupy myself predominantly — already so as not to let the spatial extent of my treatise grow into the immeasurable — with my own investigations, for which the way had in part already been marked out by those preceding ones, [namely] to avoid the sources of error from their origin on. Like most gonad transplantations, mine too must confine themselves to the transplantation of the **ovaries**. The testes seem in general much harder to make take, and accessible mostly only to autoplastic transfer (onto the same individual); I have undertaken a series of experiments on the midwife toad (*Alytes*), which is on the best way to overcome this difficulty; but with the salamander, indeed with all Urodeles in general, a further circumstance is added which makes it altogether impossible — at least for the purposes of the inheritance problem impossible — to carry out testicle transplantations.

This circumstance consists in the presence of several (up to 4) pairs of testicles, which are distributed over a very considerable stretch of the body cavity. In order to remove them all, one would have to open the male in question to so great an extent that this would with certainty bring about its death. Even if, exceptionally, on one occasion only a single pair of testes were present, one would have to undertake the laparotomy in full extent, in order to see whether really no more are there; but often they are very small and then (except in a complete dissection, which from the outset does not intend to leave the animal alive) hard to see. Most frequently quite young salamanders are really in possession of only a single pair of testes; but no one can vouch for whether, later, further pairs may not after all be added along the genital ridge. The use of males with exclusively foreign-race germ-plasm therefore had to be renounced in the salamander experiments, and this deficiency had to be circumvented in the breedings in another way, to be described later (by selection of corresponding males), which admittedly made success more difficult.

By contrast the **females** offer extraordinarily favourable conditions for an unobjectionable total gonad exchange. Above all, there is on each side only one ovary present; this is, moreover, of such a nature that it does not represent a pulpy, easily disintegrating (as in the cock) or branched, widely-ramified mass in the body cavity (as in the butterflies), and also does not itself need to be cut into at the operation, but can easily be detached along the mesovarium and then falls out in one whole, concise piece. Only rarely is the mesovarium so short that this miscarries and the ovary itself is injured; in that case I did not include such females in the experiment. **A leaving-behind of ovary remnants which could regenerate was hence completely excluded**; but also an eventual regeneration from somatic material, such as was demonstrated in amphibians by KUSCHAKEWITSCH, did not take place. Of this I assured myself by holding in readiness a number of castrated females into which, after removal of their own, no foreign ovaries were implanted. Each time when, in a female with a transplanted ovary, a litter was to be registered, I dissected a few simply castrated females, and they never showed a trace of a new formation. — Finally it is inestimably favourable that one need not provide for any connection between the transplanted ovary and the oviduct, that is, need not be concerned about its coming about; for this connection normally never exists anyway, since the ripe eggs, after the bursting of the follicles, fall freely into the body cavity and from there only indirectly reach the tube of the oviduct. It is therefore not so necessary that the transplanted ovary grow on exactly at the place where the normal one had been seated.

In more detail the course of the castration and transplantation was as follows: the two salamander females whose ovaries one intends to exchange are simultaneously placed in deep ether narcosis, which is to last at least 25 minutes; for although the whole operation should take at most 10 minutes, it is important that the animals afterwards remain motionless for a while longer. How the narcosis is best handled, one may read up in the section on the regeneration experiments (p. 99); its careful maintenance by means of the ether-cap plays here a particularly great role. About the sterilization of the operating instruments I need probably say nothing, it is indeed self-evident, just as is the preparation of aseptically rinsed glass containers as lodging until the completed wound healing; I will only remark that I found it more practical, both for the animals and for the instruments, to boil the latter rather than simply to draw them through a flame. After some trying-out of where to apply the incisions necessary for the removal of the ovaries — in the belly or in the back region — I have once and for all arrived at making one incision on each of the two flanks. The belly operation has, it is true, the advantage that one manages with a single wound, but this advantage is outweighed by the much greater danger of infection and the danger of injuring large veins; back wounds too (namely deep ones, reaching into the body cavity — not the shallow wounds necessary for skin-regeneration and -transplantation experiments) heal badly and often, when the ovary extends far cranialward, make a nipping-off of the ribs necessary.

With the lateral incisions it is important to apply them right away at the correct height, or rather in general in such a position that the ovary already lies immediately opposite them; one must, after the first scissor-cut, which severs skin and trunk musculature at one stroke, be able to recognize it at once by its bright, yellowish-white colour. Although one easily acquires some practice in finding the correct spot — it lies mostly in the anterior third of a straight line by which one imagines the insertions of the limbs to be connected, that is, somewhat above half the flank-height more toward the forelegs —, one can nevertheless easily be deceived, since the ovaries can be very different in size, indeed can even vary with respect to their insertion site in the anterio-posterior direction. As the most infallible means of orientation I now use the following: I let the two females respectively destined for an exchange go 8–14 days without any nourishment; they are then sufficiently de-fatted, their rectum so far emptied, that one can palpate the ovaries — provided they do not just stand at the lowest point of their periodic development — through the skin kneaded lightly to and fro between the fingers. And this happens, indeed, when the animals are already narcotized; precisely the ether narcosis is excellently suited for this, because it abolishes the muscle tone, makes skin and trunk musculature flaccid, so that one is able, with some practice, to recognize every larger, harder inner organ by grasping. In doing so, naturally, the greatest caution and delicacy is required; if nevertheless the follicle apparatus gets somewhat squeezed, this has for the later thriving of so enormously regeneration-capable a tissue at the site of its transplantation no further significance. Animals emaciated of their own accord cannot replace those produced by that fasting period; for they could be sick and feeble, which is not the case with the others, if only they were well nourished beforehand.

Once one has oriented oneself in the manner described (beforehand the matter must naturally have been tried out repeatedly, first on fresh cadavers, then on worthless living animals), and has further rubbed the incision areas with ether and washed them with cold distilled water, then a slit of 6–8 mm length suffices to draw out the whole organ with a blunt forceps, or still better with a bent blunt hook (HARMS too, 1911, in *Tritons*), and to cut it off along the mesovarium by means of straight scissors. Earlier I used to burn through the mesovarium with an electric cauterizer, but found that then the healing-on of the transplant does not succeed so well. If the mesovarium is so short that it does not permit the drawing-out, then the operation counts as miscarried. During the drawing-out the slit is to be kept open by 1–2 spreading forceps. The conveyed-out ovary can, for the moment during which the operation is repeated on the other flank and then on both flanks of the other animal, be pushed back into the wound, but it can also be laid on a sterile, dry watch-glass. In order not to confuse the ovaries, one prepares two watch-glasses and marks them somehow, e.g. by laying underneath them black and yellow paper, as belonging to the blacker and the yellower animal.

Now follows the transplantation. Whether the right ovary comes again to lie on the right or on the left is indifferent. But the **size, the developmental state** does play a role: small ovaries with eggs scarcely the size of a poppy-seed heal on more easily than large ones with eggs of the size of a millet- to hemp-grain. A definite season, when those alone are to be found, can unfortunately not be given, for that the periodicity of the reproductive cycle of *Salamandra* is not strict enough; one can only say that, on the one hand in December, January (the otherwise, on account of slower wound healing, most unfavourable time), on the other hand in June, July, the smallest ovaries are found, i.e. such as contain the smallest, most unripe eggs. But even then it not seldom happens that, of the two opened animals, one possesses highly developed, the other underdeveloped ovaries; since the former too, besides the large eggs and burst-ripe follicles, which after transplantation infallibly perish and are resorbed, possesses quite small ones, one need not let oneself be disturbed by this any further — only it is advisable, of an extensive organ, to transplant only a very small piece, [and] not to use the rest. Even with a small organ it is good at least to halve it, but both halves can be introduced. The bilateral complete operation (**castration and transplantation**) one best undertakes simultaneously, that is, at the same operative opportunity; it means scarcely any sparing to carry out the one side a couple of weeks or months earlier than the other.

The pieces are pushed with a blunt forceps roughly to the place where the native ovary was located, and now comes, as the last but most difficult manipulation, the applying of the suture out of finest sterilized surgical silk with a quite small surgical (curved) needle. In the favourable case the suture can simultaneously close the wound-slit (skin together with trunk muscles in one) and fasten each introduced ovary-piece with at least one stitch to the dorsal body wall (to the peritoneum). If this is, owing to the less favourable position of the wound-slit, not feasible, or if, in the presence of a quite broad mesovarium, the re-fastening to the latter — actually unnecessary for success — was attempted, then only the ovary pieces were sewn on, but the outer wound not sewn up at all, [but] rather closed together with 2–3 little clamps. A few drops of collodion, dripped over the wound, do no harm in any case and help to keep infections away from the wound and to make its bursting-open more difficult. As quite impractical I found the use of any kind of plaster, which never will hold on the salamander skin. Immediately after the operation a renewed, very gentle washing with boiled, cooled water is advisable, which — on account of the ether remaining in it — should also be made to flow through the mouth cavity. After about 8 days both clamps and sutures can be removed, or rather the latter often fall out by themselves as well.

Meanwhile, the glass vessels with the freshly operated salamanders stand in a dark, cool place — for instance in the cellar, in our case in the "dry cistern," in whose underground basin the normal breeding-stock of our olms [*Proteus anguinus*] is housed. Here there prevails precisely the temperature that is right for, and sufficient for, good progress of the wound-healing, and at the same time very uniform (12–14° C.), together with a high atmospheric humidity, which spares one from having to supply the glass containers additionally with (possibly infection-germ-bearing) water. They must, however, on account of the accumulating excrements, be rinsed 1–2 times daily with boiled, re-cooled water. The most important thing, however, is that the salamanders, after the operation, even after the narcosis has long since worn off, scarcely move at all; as we heard in the case of the darkness experiments (p. 84), this is now achieved with automatic certainty by their transfer into complete darkness; and one need only see to it that they get into the dark before they become fully awake, because otherwise they still crawl about for a while. Animals that one had, say, placed during the operation in a supine position (with the belly upward) — sometimes this appears more convenient —, one afterward carefully puts back at once into the normal position. The animals receive food only after the complete healing of the external wound, hence in no case before the lapse of a week; in the dark, and on top of that suffering from the after-effects of the operation, they would not eat anything anyway. At the taking-out of the suture-silk and the removal of the clamps, which, as remarked, takes place after about 8 days (one must of course look closely and wait longer if there are still open spots, possibly renewing the collodion film), there takes place at the same time the transfer back of the glass containers into the light, whose arrangement is now enriched by a porcelain bowl with a few mealworms and a flat (!) bathing-bowl. But for at least one further week the happily operated animals remain in these "sanatoria" before they finally move over into corresponding experimental terraria with soil, moss, etc. Should one or another animal refuse the voluntary intake of food, one proceeds to very careful and moderate artificial supply of nourishment, as indicated in the section "Darkness and Blinding Experiments" (p. 85).

I have as yet said nothing about the selection of the females used for the ovary transplantations; here one has to distinguish three categories: 1) Irregularly spotted ones (forma typica), with preference for the little-spotted ones and exclusion of the very richly spotted ones. This choice serves to make the experimental results clearer, as will at once become evident. — 2) Regularly striped ones (var. *taeniata*), with preference for the richly yellow ones and exclusion of those whose longitudinal bands do not run completely unbroken. Place of procurement: Vorwohle in Brunswick. Hence the striped natural race. — 3) Regularly striped ones whose parents were still richly but irregularly spotted, i.e. belonged to the forma typica, and which owed their richness of marking to the inductive action of a yellow ground. Place of procurement: the Vienna Woods. Hence a striped artificial race. — — The merely spot-rowed, double-rowed and single-rowed, as well as the single-striped (middle-striped) specimens, which owe their origin to a yellow-culture further treated on a black ground, or to a black-culture further treated on a yellow ground, and the corresponding free-living forms, were for good reason for the time being not included in the transplantation experiments. This provisional exclusion was made in the interest of the clarity of the results; for at present there confront one another, with exchanged ovaries, only the most extreme racial differences.

Now it was a matter of not introducing into the fertilization any experimental error, since unfortunately the recruitment of males with likewise exchanged germ-portions was, for the technical reasons mentioned, impossible. As the first and most important measure, it was observed to separate the females chosen for the ovary exchange, from small on, long before they were sexually mature, from the other sex, so that they, after the successful operation when brought together with males, had to be reliably virginal. Otherwise, namely, sperm of unknown origin is stored in the SIEBOLD's tubules (the physiological receptaculum seminis of the dorsal cloacal wall) and can, even after a pause of years, unexpectedly bring about gravidity. In order to tell the sexes apart at such early stages, I made advantageous use of the characteristic given by v. BEDRIAGA, according to which the fore- and hind-legs of the male, laid back and stretched out toward the rear and front respectively against the trunk, touch each other with the palm and the sole, those of the female, however, only with the finger- and toe-tips; likewise of the characteristic, given by me in 1904, of the relative tail-length: the tail in the male much longer, in the female shorter, equally long or at most only a little longer than the trunk. It is clear that both kinds of measurement amount to the same thing: namely to ascertain the more considerable trunk-length of the female, — and I do not understand that WUNDER (p. 64), in measuring two series of 73 and 130 specimens of *Salamandra atra*, was unable to confirm it. Incidentally, he does not himself give the measurement-figures, on the basis of which one would first have to see whether with him too the relative tail-lengths of the females are not somewhat smaller than those of the males, only that almost all tails just now less, now more exceed the trunk-lengths. In quite young animals, to be sure, the dimensions, which are throughout small and make the differences hard to recognize, become an obstacle to reliable separation; but even a misdiagnosis has here, at so childlike an age, nothing to say for our purposes; one need only, within the first 3½ years (so long it lasts until sexual maturity sets in), repeat the examination about half-yearly, and has then in the end certainly excluded in good time all the males that had perhaps not been isolated.

Has the operated female become sexually mature (many were indeed operated even before sexual maturity, which is best on account of the smallness of the ovary, most difficult on account of the smallness of the animal, — others, however, only after sexual maturity, so as not somehow, in a positive or negative sense, to come into collision with a "sensible period" of the germ-plasm), then it is put together with the male intended for it. Approximately one half of each female-category was united with the same, the other half with the opposite color-type. The pairs were kept either on a neutral or on a kind of ground whose color, in order to exclude direct germ-induction in a direction favorable to the appearance of somatic induction, was contrary to the main body-color of the females: the strongest-yellow females — hence those with closed stripes — were accordingly tended on black earth; females with little yellow — the irregularly spotted ones — on yellow earth. In the results obtained more recently, the further precautionary measure had been employed of setting up all the cultures with transplanted ovaries in dim half-darkness, where the animals still readily move and feed, but where, however, as we know (cf. p. 23), no color-changes whatever take place that become visible in the course of the experimental periods at our disposal: this precautionary measure takes into account the experience communicated by SECEROV that the yellow spots let through more light than the black ground-color; thus the germ-plasm of strongly marked animals could, even if they lived on a black ground, enjoy more light and thereby evoke a stronger yellow-coloring potency of the germ-plasm stemming from the little-marked animal, and hence the false appearance of somatic induction. This is also in order to reassure those who, despite my proofs that it is a matter of specific color-effects, should hold to the view that the marking-differences are evoked only by the different reflecting-capacity of the grounds, hence by effects of different light-intensity.

After all this that has here been said about the experimental arrangement, there result the following breeding-combinations with "foreign-ovaried" females, — each of them a) on black, b) on yellow, c) on neutral ground, and more recently throughout in a light-quantity insufficient for the coming-about of color-inductions; which I, in order not to burden the following survey too much, shall no longer repeat:

I. ♀♀ of the forma typica (hence natural race).
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;1. With ovary of var. *taeniata* (natural race):
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a) ♂ forma typica,
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b) ♂ var. *taeniata* (natural race),
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c) ♂ double-striped (artificial race).
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;2. Ovary of a double-striped ♀ (artificial race):
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a) ♂ forma typica.
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b) ♂ var. *taeniata* (natural race),
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c) ♂ double-striped (artificial race).

II. ♀♀ of var. *taeniata* (natural race), with ovary of forma typica:
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a) ♂ forma typica,
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b) ♂ var. *taeniata* (natural race),
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c) ♂ double-striped (artificial race).
III. ♀♀ double-striped (= *taeniata*, but artificial race), ovary of forma typica:
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;a) ♂ forma typica,
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;b) ♂ var. *taeniata* (natural race),
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;c) ♂ double-striped (artificial race).

Understandably it exceeds the powers of a single human being to stock all these 12 or — if we further take into consideration the three sorts of ground each — even 36 combinations with any somewhat larger number of experimental specimens; for one need only consider that the mortality of the operated females was no small one and in the first experimental periods reached 90%. To be sure, I managed to possess of all the combinations at least three happily operated females, but with larger numbers I worked only in the most interesting, i.e. for the demonstration of a somatic induction most unfavorable combinations, as well as, when such a demonstration was in certain cases — to be discussed presently — nevertheless not to be dismissed, in those combinations which were suited to working out the difference of the result with the bringing-in of natural race and artificial race. From these two points of view there result as the strongly-to-be-stocked combinations: I 1 b c, 2 b c; II a b; III b c. In these there are at present 18–20 completely healed, foreign-ovaried females each, with 20 each of the males required by the combination in question; in I 1 a, 2 a, III a 3 females each and, in order to raise the prospect of fertilization, a relatively larger number (9 each) of males of the required sort; II a has died out. — A further simplification of the complicated arrangement, which lays claim to so much variously treated material, results, however, from the fact that I shall henceforth wholly omit the keeping on different, color-effective grounds, but rather conduct all the transplantation-objects on neutral ground in weak light. For I can at once anticipate here the one result common to all combinations: the nature of the ground and the light takes not the slightest perceptible influence on the nature of the young that have issued from transplanted ovaries. This result agrees completely with that which I had already obtained for the inheritance of the color-inductions, when it was a matter of withdrawing the offspring of the artificial color-types from a fresh direct induction, for the purpose of an unobjectionable testing of heritability (cf. especially p. 36). For superfluity's sake, however, the permanent sojourn of the breeding-material in subdued light is by itself alone already sufficient to keep away all new inductions that might perhaps in spite of everything still be suspected.

By no means, however, have all the combinations enumerated above as most strongly populated already yielded results; whereas, on the other hand, offspring is present also among the weakly populated ones. This is connected with the contingencies to which fertilization is exposed in operated and therefore often not rutting animals; if the female played a quite passive role in this, then the fertilizations would have had to be much more frequent, for the admitted males were indeed normal, non-operated animals, in full possession of their vital powers, — but on the female too there devolves, during the amplexus, the active task of sucking in the spermatophore with opened cloacal lips; if it did not do this, then the amplexus, which the fully-rutting male forces, does indeed not fail to occur, but it remains ineffective. Thus my speculation, to raise the prospects of natural fertilization by a surplus of males, had really been a mistaken one, insofar as the success depended on the females. To this is added, as a further hindrance to more numerous successful copulations, that evidently a fairly considerable percentage even of healed and themselves copulation-willing females are still to be excluded, in which the operation succeeded only with regard to the preservation of the animal's life, but not with regard to the purpose of the operation, the preservation of the life of the transplanted ovary; such females do indeed receive spermatophores, but there are no fertilization-capable eggs present. Section findings did in fact make me come upon female specimens of this kind, which in truth had become castrates or, in place of the ovary, possessed only a tumor-like, connective-tissue formation without a trace of eggs. Then of course artificial sperm-injection too, as described on an earlier occasion (p. 35, 36), could be of no help; I had undertaken it a few times before I had knowledge that it must fail for the reason ascertained afterward by section.

From what has just been said it follows incidentally that the castration of female animals, whether undertaken pre- or post-puberally, entails in these no morphological or functional deficiency-phenomena of whatever kind; not only does the dimensioning of the body-proportions remain, as indicated above on p. 118, but the copulation- and reception-drive too is unchanged, otherwise the castrated female would indeed not open its cloaca in order to receive the spermatophore.

The cases hitherto on hand, in which offspring was obtained from transplanted ovaries, are the following:

1) Spotted female with ovary of a striped female caught in nature, fertilized by a striped male caught in nature (Combination I 1 b): 37 offspring, throughout closed-striped; in a second litter from the same sperm-store 39 offspring, without exception likewise striped.

2) Spotted female with ovary of an artificially bred, striped female, fertilized by an artificially bred, striped male (Combination I 2 c): 25 offspring, throughout striped, one with interruptions, which however have nothing to signify.

3) Striped female caught in nature with ovary of a spotted female, fertilized by a spotted male (Combination II a): 26 offspring, throughout irregularly spotted.

4) Striped female caught in nature with ovary of a spotted one, fertilized by a striped male stemming from nature (Combination II b): 23 offspring, throughout irregularly spotted; in a further litter from the same sperm-store 29 offspring, without exception likewise spotted.

A second female of the same combination brings into the world 28 offspring of the same color-type.

5) Artificially bred, striped female with ovary of a spotted one, fertilized by a spotted male (Combination III a = Pl. XV, Fig. 27): 25 offspring, of which 14 with two spot-rows somewhat disturbed in their symmetry, 11 quite irregularly spotted; in a later litter from the sperm-store of the same copulation 31 offspring, of which 22 with a fairly regular spot-double-row, 9 irregularly spotted.

A second female of the same combination threw 31 young with displaced spot-rows (Pl. XV, Fig. 27); in 8 of them rows can scarcely any longer be spoken of, they are therefore best designated as irregularly spotted; a later litter from the same sperm yields 32 young with displaced, but distinct spot-rows.

...seed yields 32 young with displaced, but distinct, rows of spots.

6) An artificially bred, striped female with the ovary of a spotted one, fertilized with a striped male caught in the wild (Combination III b): first 24 offspring, of which 19 with closed, strictly regular stripes, 5 regularly spot-rowed; then 28 offspring, all pronouncedly striped, only 7 of them with slight interruptions in the stripes.

A second female of the same combination throws 35 young with interrupted stripes; a third 33 young, 4 with uninterrupted, 20 with interrupted stripes, 9 with regular rows of spots.

7) An artificially bred, striped female with the ovary of a spotted one, fertilized with an artificially bred, striped male (Combination III c): first litter 26 offspring, second litter 27, third litter 30 offspring, all completely closed [-striped], strictly regularly striped.

Second female: first litter 17, second litter 19 offspring, all striped; third female 28 striped offspring; fourth female: in the first litter 36, in the second litter 26 striped offspring. — — —

The number of offspring registered here amounts on average to only about 75% of the total number of generated young, since approximately ¼ regularly perish during larval development. Offspring in general — and indeed throughout numbers high enough to exclude chance occurrences and to let the prevailing regularity be recognized — were thus furnished by the Combinations I 1 b, I 2 c, II a, b, III a, b, c. They can be brought into two **groups**, according to the standpoint of whether they answer the question of somatic induction — the re-tuning [Umstimmung] of the foreign-race ovary inserted from the Soma [body] — affirmatively or negatively:

**Negative** read the results from Combination I 1 b, I 2 c, II a, b [or, what is the same, cases 1—4 of our enumeration given above]: in all these births the offspring turned out as though it had issued from the pairing of the respective male with the female from which the eggs originally stemmed, not with that [female] which it got to carry after the transplantation.

**Positive** read the results from Combination III a, b, c [or, what is the same, cases 5—7 of our above detailed enumeration]: in these births the offspring-colour can in no way be derived from anywhere otherwise...

...than through an influence of the deviantly coloured body of the fosterers (Tragammen) upon the germ-plasm settled in them. In 1911 I was still able to give to individual, now more completely recurring cases of Combinations III a, b, c (5—7 of [those] today, 3—5 of the then detailed enumeration, S. 14, 15 of the separate print) yet another interpretation — admittedly even then a forced-sounding one — which evaded the assumption of somatic induction; namely the interpretation which explains the result partly, on the presupposition of a definite colour-race composition of the breeding material, by Mendelian segregation phenomena, [and] partly, on the other hand, by new-induction on the part of the substrates [Böden] employed. Since, however, the latter turned out to be impossible, and is moreover eliminated by the new experimental arrangement (neutral substrate, twilight), [and] since, furthermore, the newly added litters from transplanted eggs move on the one hand exactly in the direction of those published at that time, [while] on the other hand they cannot at all be brought into harmony with Mendelian behaviour — one may compute gamete-compositions ever so improbable —, there in fact remains then nothing else over than the possibility of somatic influencing of the germ-plasm and therefore also of the **inheritance of acquired characters in the original sense** ("Transmission conception" of the modern geneticists).

If we examine what the breeding combinations in which somatic induction comes to light have in common, we fall without further ado upon [the fact] that it is only those in which the fostering [female] (Tragamme) is a female whose symmetrical stripe-drawing is an inherited product of being kept on yellow substrate, hence a character first bred up in the experiment. All other fostering [females] (Tragammen), whose colour-pattern may resemble ever so completely that of this "art-product" [Kunstprodukt] [and which], taken over ready-made from Nature, proved themselves ineffective for the coat-colour of the young. The [proposition] therefore to be generalized from these — still always rather sparse — cases would accordingly read: **Somatic induction is exercised only by a newly [acquired] character.** Upon the stimulus-physiological and inheritance-theoretical significance of this proposition I shall come [back] in Chapter X "Theoretical Evaluation". — —

Perhaps, namely, some things in the breeding results will now still **not yet appear sufficiently clearly** to the reader: for example, how it comes about that in case 4, where, according to our assertion...

...no somatic induction is present, the offspring of a striped fostering-female (Tragamme) with "spotted" ovary (forgive me this abbreviated manner of writing) turn out irregularly spotted, although they were begotten by a regularly striped father. Only the [point] now to be discussed, an absolutely necessary complement of the transplantation experiments — [namely] the crossings — can make everything comprehensible.

## VI. Crossing experiments.

*(Taf. XV, Fig. 24—26, Taf. XVI.)*

Especial care I devoted to crossing with one another the various races obtained from Nature and in the experiment: already for their own sake, [but] still more, however, because through the clarification of the results obtained from the ovarian transplantations these breedings promised to become important.

Thus the correct evaluation [Wertung] of the Guthrie hen-breedings with transplanted ovaries had become downright impossible, in that one had refrained from crossing with one another the races used for the gonad-exchange; for [the fact] that Davenport, in his post-examination, saw native ovarian tissue regenerate is naturally no proof that this likewise happened in the cases of Guthrie and that the chicks did not stem from transplanted, but from regenerated egg-material. A single crossing experiment, and we would survey the whole state of affairs at one stroke much more clearly.

What concerns first the pure-breedings [Reinzuchten] of the races used for the transplantation- and crossing-experiments — pure-breedings, as Guthrie too applied, [though] in too restricted a number —: thus one can, out of a material of young animals which already in fact runs to many thousands, since it also still encompasses the control-breedings of my experiments on inheritance of enforced reproductive adaptations, state with the greatest certainty that among the offspring of the irregularly spotted forma typica (place of finding [Fundgegend]: Vienna Woods [Wiener Wald]) never striped or spot-rowed exemplars appear, [and] just as little ever, among the offspring of the regularly striped or spot-rowed var. *taeniata* (place of find [Fundort]: Vorwohle in Brunswick [Braunschweig]), unsymmetrically spotted exemplars resembling the forma typica. It comes about that in a litter stemming from continuously striped parents single young never [reach] the same stage of striping (without...

> ¹⁾ Here too we observe again, with two exceptional cases, that [the number] after each litter rises or at least remains the same.

> ²⁾ About 25%, which perish during larval development, are on average to be added thereto in order to obtain the total number of offspring — according to the regularity established by Halban, [whereby] the number of offspring is computed [accordingly]; here only those usable for ascertaining the colour type, hence at least once-metamorphosed offspring, are taken into account.

...interruption of the stripes), but rather retain throughout life some few gaps therein, — but concerning the drawing-style itself, however, whether typica or *taeniata*, there can never exist the slightest doubt. These experimental results one understands upon the employment of neutral, colouring-inactive substrates; and whether the double-striped form stems from Nature or from the induction experiment is here a matter of indifference: striped and striped again yields striped, even when it was actually a crossing of natural race and artificial race.

One might thereby earlier well have held the proof for the "purity" of the races as furnished, and I myself bred thus, because I was originally of this opinion. Today we know that the necessary knowledge of the genotypical composition of even a breeding-material appearing pure in pure-breeding [Reinzucht] is to be gained only through pairing [Anpaarung] with other races, by bastardization-analysis [Bastardierungsanalyse]. The preceding table may give an overview of the P- and F₁-generation. One sees that each crossing-combination [is carried out] with two (isolated) pairs respectively, [the arrangement being] a matter of indifference, whether father or mother bears this or that racial property, [and] properly with 4 pairs carried out. Each pair bears as orientation-sign a large Latin capital letter. The number of the obtained offspring should probably suffice for all justified claims. The crossing experiments too proceeded, just as the pure-breedings [Reinzuchten], on colouring-inactive substrates; single samples, moreover, [were] aimed at [ascertaining] whether the result would change, e.g., on yellow or on black earth; [these] furnished markedly the proof for the indifference of the substrate for the breeding result, since the sexually mature breeding animals no longer change strongly, at least not within so short a time, and a direct induction upon the maturing and developing germ-plasm just does not take place.

It thus follows from this table first of all only that the forma typica (irregular spotting) [is] epistatic or dominant over the regular striping. This character — the stripe-drawing — does however possess the property of the "relative potency" so named by Goldschmidt, in that in other cases it does not completely succumb to the spot-drawing: the character-pair then produces an intermediate drawing-form, [it] combines, as it were, striping and spotting, in that the spots stand in two rows. It is, however, to be remarked — [something] which does not emerge from the table on account of want of space — that these double-rows mostly do not run so strictly symmetrically as in those spot-rowed animals...

...which still fall purely into the var. *taeniata*, but rather are more or less displaced; but their row-wise arrangement always remains recognizable. (The exactly symmetrically spot-rowed form of the var. *taeniata* has up to now found no admission into the crossing experiments, since it could bring about confusion in the elucidation of the fundamental relations between the biotypes; rather, where in general symmetrically-drawn forms were drawn upon, this happened as closed, uninterrupted striped ones.) One readily perceives that the complete retreat of the striping upon its crossing with the spotting then takes place [only] when the introduced striped form belonged to the natural race, var. *taeniata*, whereas upon the drawing-in of the striped artificial race intermediate forms come out.

Now we understand also case 4 (p. 122) of the transplantation experiments: the elemental property "spotting" of the *taeniata*-female's inserted ovary dominates completely over the "striping" — brought in from Nature — of the *taeniata*-male crossed therewith. If in case 5, upon a simple crossing of egg × seed [Ei × Samen], an unchanged genotype were present, sheer forma typica would have to come out; there come, however, partly intermediate forms, spot-rowed animals with disturbed symmetry of the rows, as though a crossing of the typica-male with the "art-striped" fostering [female] (Tragamme) had taken place. In case 6 we would likewise have had to obtain sheer forma typica (heterozygotes from typica × taeniata); there arise, however, sheer offspring which belong to the *taeniata*. In case 7, finally, intermediate forms from spotting × striping would have to appear without somatic influencing; the actual result deviates therefrom once more, with sheer pure-striped *taeniata*-forms in approximation to the coat-colour of the fostering [female] (Tragamme).

Therewith, however, the elucidation of the previous transplantation results would — insofar as crossing experiments can contribute thereto — already be sufficiently done; I added several crossings, in which the "middle-striped artificial race" functions as one of the parent animals, and pulled, as the next-following table lets [us] survey, most of the crossing-combinations indicated on the previous table up to the grandchild-generation [Enkelgeneration], wherefrom in turn some things [may] be gathered concerning the difference in the modes of inheritance of the like-appearing natural and artificial race, as well as concerning the relations of the colouring-allomorphy in the salamanders generally.

Concerning this it should still be noted, [in] reference to what the previous table shows of the P- and F₁-generation, that the artificial race...

...with the one stripe running along the middle of the back arises thereby, that the offspring of animals which on black substrate had lost the greatest part of their drawing are reared on yellow substrate. Paired among one another, these animals breed pure, i.e., yield again throughout middle-striped offspring. In crossing with the unsymmetrically spotted forma typica the result is analogous, as if one had mixed the latter with the double-striped artificial race: i.e., the number of the stripes (in the present case the single number) is preserved, yet each stripe is dissolved into a row of spots; only the row is somewhat displaced, the symmetry of the colour-distribution therefore disturbed. From singly spot-rowed animals, which appear directly as parents further reared on black substrate, [and which are] likewise treated as "black-treated" parents, those intermediate crossing-products distinguish themselves by the more considerable number and size of the (besides, less strictly median-lying) spots, so that the animals, despite an approximate restriction of the drawing to a middle dorsal zone, must not appear poorly drawn.

Quite unexpected to me was the crossing result between the double- and single-striped form: whether natural or artificial race plays no part here, the offspring of these symmetrically drawn pairs (drawn, in one parent with a preponderance and flank-localisation of the yellow, in the other parent of the black) correspond completely to the asymmetrically spotted *forma typica*. Evidently we have here before us one of those cases in which, with two different races, each of which lacks other substances needed to unfold the characters of the ancestral form, the mixture — as a consequence of the mutual complementation of the hereditary substances — once again yields the full reaction of the type; thus a phenomenon which has been well studied especially in fowls, pigeons and ducks by present-day geneticists and was designated by Darwin as a reversion upon crossing. Of particular interest here is the fact that races or forms in which such a thing is possible could also be obtained by artificial induction.

I now give in tabular form the result of the inbreeding of the filial generation obtained by crossing; in it the small letters a, b, c, a₁, b₁, c₁, etc. indicate the relationship to the capital letters A, B, C, etc. of the previous table, i.e. they show from which pairs of the P-generation the F₁-animals respectively used for breeding are descended (without exception they stem from the respective first litter, because since the beginning of these *(Table, printed sideways across the page. The three "Litter" columns stand under a common super-heading "Litters of one pair each.")*

The forms "*typica*" and "*taeniata*" have thus also in F₂ behaved as genuine Mendelian allelomorphs: "spotting" as the epistatic hereditary unit (dominant), "striping" as the hypostatic, as the recessive. The splitting takes place all the more in the theoretically to-be-expected numerical ratio the larger the quantities of F₂-offspring that can be brought into the reckoning, and in the grand total it finally attains even exactly the demanded ratio 3 : 1. — From the ready-given natural race "*taeniata*", however, the race first won in the experiment, the banded race, again differs fundamentally in its hereditary behaviour; apparently it is the same crossing, and to outward appearance *taeniata* and that race, which arose, as it were, only before our eyes out of the ordinary *typica*, are not to be distinguished: but whereas the former is a pure recessive, disappears as such in F₁, splits off in ¼ of F₂, the latter does not become wholly invisible already in F₁, and in F₂ the mixture of the two bastardised races remains undisentangled. If one holds this together with the transplantation result, where only the one arisen race was capable of influencing the germ-cells, then one may well speak of a stronger transmitting potency of its newly acquired character.

From this, however, there is well to be distinguished the strength of the capacity to maintain itself permanently, its power of preservation; for we now observe in the unsplit F₂-generation the following phenomenon: that character which constituted the new acquisition, the symmetrically connected arrangement of the drawing-elements, slowly abates; already in the first litters of the pairs examined to this end it is something — at first scarcely perceptibly — weaker than it was in F₁, and with the following litters, without any kind of counter-induction playing a part, the disturbance of the coloristic bilateral symmetry steadily increases. According to my analogous experiences concerning the hereditary behaviour of enforced reproductive adaptations, I can predict that perhaps already in later litters of F₃, certainly in F₄, there will be no more talk of a bilateral arrangement of the drawing in the descendants of the bastards — they return completely to the starting-form "*typica*" common to the two crossed races, with irregular spotting.

As against this, the grandchild generation of the crossing *typica* × middle-striping likewise offers nothing principally new any more. The salamanders with the dorsal median stripe too arose only in the experiment out of typically spotted ones; they too yielded in the first hybrid generation intermediate blends, and these too yield no segregation of the mixed races in the second hybrid generation. Here too, finally, it seems — as the increasing disturbance of the colour-symmetry from one litter to the next indicates — that the novel drawing-arrangement acquired from the milieu is in the course of diminishing and finally vanishing in favour of the restoration of the original drawing-order.

These facts will have to be evaluated still more closely theoretically in Chapter X; before I conclude the chapter on the crossing experiments, however, I must yet make mention of a few bastardisation results obtained incidentally, which, to be sure, have little importance for the present state of my transformation experiments, but which in and of themselves claim some interest, because they contribute to the analysis of the complicated racial mixture that we have before us in the shape of our commonest *Salamandra maculosa*-phenotypes. I reproduce the results in question only quite briefly, without giving the closer numerical details. In many cases these details are anyway, in so far as they concern only the number of offspring obtained, contained in the two previous tables, because the pairs crossed here naturally differed not merely with respect to the one character-pair set out there numerically, but also through other character-pairs not pursued in the tables.

Thus the "great range of variation" that I wrote into the last column of the previous table arises above all from the varying quantity of the yellow pigment: much yellow, little yellow likewise form such a Mendelian character-pair, which, so to speak, runs along more or less sharply expressed in all the crossings and is subject to typical splitting after an intermediate F₁-generation. Richly drawn animals with poorly drawn animals thus yield moderately drawn children; the latter bred on among themselves yield richly-, moderately- and poorly-drawn grandchildren in the ratio of 1 : 2 : 1, in so far as can be ascertained with the indistinctness of this allelomorph; but approximately it always agrees. From this it follows of itself that "much black", "little black" too form just such an allelomorph, intermediate in F₁, for the distribution and splitting of the yellow follows; thus likewise, in a crossing observed to this end, F₁ possesses moderate, F₂ much, moderate, little black in the ratio of 1 : 2 : 1. The heterozygotes with moderate black are naturally the same as those with moderate yellow; the homozygotes with little black are the same as those with much yellow, and conversely. — We know that the var. *taeniata* is hypostatic to the *forma typica*; but *taeniata* and *typica* are, as a rule, also distinguished by the allelomorph now discussed, in that the former possesses more yellow. This, in the crossing, does not retain its otherwise customary correlation to *taeniata*, but rather drawing-style and drawing-quantity go their own ways in the segregation. On crossing a select richly-yellow *taeniata* with a select poorly-yellow *typica* we obtain an asymmetrically spotted (*typica*), but relatively (moderately) richly-yellow daughter-generation, as well as a grandchild-generation in which 2 specimens each are likewise asymmetrically and moderately richly spotted, 1 specimen which is again asymmetrically but very richly spotted; finally one which is symmetrically and at the same time poorly drawn (interrupted, very thin stripes or rows of small, sparsely standing spots). Or else the symmetrically drawn one possesses at the same time very much yellow (uninterrupted, broad stripes) and in return one of the three asymmetrical ones the least yellow; this is indeed the more frequent combination, since in nature too the predominantly black *taeniata* are the rarest.

A third character-pair, mostly contained in the same crossings, is furnished by the differing intensity of the yellow colour. Here it holds as a law that every higher intensity dominates over every lower one and splits off from the latter in F₂ typically, according to the alternative inheritance, as 3 : 1. Thus orange-yellow is dominant over citron-yellow, straw-yellow over sulphur-yellow, dark chrome-yellow over light. How pronounced red behaves — which at least in that form in which it occurs in v. Schweizerbarth's "var. *coccinea*" may be conceived, as it were, as the highest intensity of the yellow, to which the reddish orange- and straw-yellow already lead over — I am unable to say, since I did not carry out such crossings; just as little as such with isolated, purple- or wine-red spots, which appear alongside the yellow ones and have nothing to do with them (cf. Chap. III, p. 20). Crossings of this kind have, however, at present been set up at our institution from another quarter. — If we cross a dark-yellow striped *taeniata* with a light-yellow spotted *typica*, or also a dark-yellow spotted *typica* with a light-yellow striped *taeniata*, then the crossing result is in both cases the same and as follows: F₁ is throughout dark-yellow asymmetrically spotted; F₂ possesses 9 such, 3 dark-yellow symmetrically striped, 3 light-yellow asymmetrically spotted specimens; 1 specimen is light-yellow and at the same time striped. If, moreover, the character-pair "much — little drawing" is contained in the crossing, then drawing-style and drawing-intensity split once more as 9 : 3 : 3 : 1, the drawing-quantity independently thereof as 4 : 8 : 4.

## VII. In search of a physiological explanation of the morphological colour-change.

As often as I showed a visitor to our institution my colour-salamander experiments, his most usual question was how, then, the coming-about of the increase of yellow on yellow, of the increase of black on black ground, was actually to be explained — he did not understand it. It was actually through this stereotyped question that I came — this I must confess to my shame — to realise that there was anything here to be understood at all, the more so since the process seemed indeed to attach itself closely enough to the well-known colour-adaptations of the bottom-fishes and crustaceans; came to seek a different explanation than the one with which I had hitherto contented myself: namely, that there were conditions which increase the yellow, others which increase the black colouring-matter in the skin of my experimental object; that these were at all times arbitrarily realisable, the latter through black underground and dryness, the former through yellow underground and wetness.

But it soon became clear to me that with this provisionally not further "explicable" axiom we control only the physical *presuppositions* under which the phenomena run their course in the animal; but how they run their course within it, the physiological conditions and processes, we do not thereby yet know. Some elucidation in this respect has, to be sure, already been brought us by the darkening- and blinding-experiments: they showed that the pure colour-effect, which expresses itself in an enlargement of the surface of the one colour-area, takes place through the mediation of the eye and central nervous system; that, on the other hand, the moisture-effect, which expresses itself in the arising of pigment-islands in the midst of the area of the other colour, comes about through the local sensibility of the skin. For both kinds of effects, however, the realising factor is light; colour and moisture are then only the determining factors.

I now believe, however, that the following expositions, which rest on microscopic observations and deduction, might offer the handle for getting one step further.

Before I come to speak of these observations, I should like to exclude from the outset two possibilities of explanation upon which one might hit. I have indeed noticed that almost all those who saw my experiments, or heard of them at lectures, and did not pose the question cited at the opening of this chapter, but believed their need for explanation satisfied with what was seen or heard, regarded the colour-displacements corresponding with the surrounding colour as the coming-about of a genuine colour-adaptation, that is, of a protective- or concealing-colouration, only without the participation of selection. Since selection, as said, cannot come into question here, the nearest-lying thing was to draw upon Wiener's principle of colour-photography. This has indeed actually been done in Gadow's discussion-remark to my Graz lecture, in which I myself cautiously made no mention of Wiener's principle. It is entirely possible that it has something to do with another part of my experimental colour-adaptations (delivered at Graz in 1910 — bibliography Kammerer 1911/12), where a real agreement between animal- and ground-colour comes about, as in *Rana temporaria*, *Bufo vulgaris*, *Bombinator pachypus*; only just to the salamander it is not applicable, for, firstly, the colour of the yellow drawing does not, for instance, agree at all with that of the loam-soil, nor does it in any series of experiments make any approaches thereto (apart from the bleaching-out of the spots on white ground): one can only say that both are yellow. Secondly, if one were to be able to speak of colour-photography, a yellowish tinge would surely have to take hold of the whole body-surface, in so far as it is illuminated, in a uniformly yellow surrounding, which is not the case, because certain districts do remain unchangedly black. (In how far the position of the salamander-colour as a protective colouration is thereby shaken or remains untouched is briefly discussed on p. 168.)

It is also impossible that the animals, as an eminent plant-physiologist considered after inspecting my experiments, have taken up into themselves something of the colour of the substrate, their corresponding colour-changes thus representing a vital staining. In Chapter II ("Technique", p. 12) it was stated how this objection was excluded, in so far as an incorporation of colouring substances by the digestive route comes into question: through exclusive feeding with mealworm-beetle larvae, which cannot get out of their feeding-dishes and therefore cannot come into contact with the substrate. There would thus remain only the osmotic route through the body-surface. But the colours do increase, after all, also in milieus that are not at all black and yellow, but rather, say, wet and dry; furthermore it is here once more to be recalled that animal-colour and milieu-colour need not agree in the salamander, as would surely have to be the case with vital staining. Certainly many a colour-copying of the milieu by the animal comes about in such a way; in 1908 I provisionally communicated two such cases, discovered by Megušar, with the discoverer's permission, and only recently v. Fejérváry has added one new one. Only just in the salamander, in my experiments at least, anything of the sort is entirely out of the question.

In Chapter II ("Technique", p. 12) it was indicated how this objection was excluded, in so far as the incorporation of colouring substances by the digestive route comes into question: by exclusive feeding with mealworm larvae [*Tenebrio* larvae], which cannot get out of their feeding-dishes and therefore do not come into contact with the substrate. There would then remain only the osmotic route through the body surface. But the colours do indeed multiply also in milieus that are not black and yellow at all, but rather, say, wet and dry; furthermore, it must here be recalled once more that in the salamander the animal's colour and the milieu's colour need not coincide, as would surely have to be the case with vital colouration [*Vitalfärbung*]. Certainly many a colour-copying of the milieu by the animal comes about in such a way; in 1908 I provisionally reported, with the discoverer's permission, two such cases discovered by Megušar, and only recently v. Fejérváry has added a new one to them. Only precisely in the salamander, in my experiments at least, is anything of the sort entirely out of the question.

In connexion with this, let it be mentioned as a curiosity that a visitor, in view of the paper experiments, raised the objection to me that it might be the *different chemical action* of these papers, not their colour, that was responsible for the colour changes. But this is surely to fetch the objections from much too far afield, to drive scepticism to the point of sterility: on yellow paper the animal becomes yellow, on yellow earth it becomes yellow — and the yellow colour of the substratum is supposed to have nothing to do with the increasingly yellow colour of the animal; rather there is supposed to lurk, in loam-earth and glossy paper, some mysterious substance common to both, which brings it about — ?! I do not see why I should not at once forestall and refute here such objections too, which have hitherto only been made to me orally, since — as one sees from the possibility of their arising — they nevertheless represent "possibilities" and would otherwise perhaps sooner or later turn up in the literature after all. — —

Now, however, to the factual matter. It has long been known and has been studied experimentally (although not yet without contradiction) that certain fishes, cephalopods, crustaceans are able to alter their colouration and marking according to their surroundings, indeed in such a way that they copy the latter most deceptively. Equally well known are the physiological causes of this sympathetic colour change: essentially a contraction and expansion of the chromatophores, in a word movement-phenomena of the given pigment-cells, present in their unchanged number. From the nature of this colour change as a movement-process, its great rapidity is also explained: not years, as in my salamander experiments, but only days, hours, or fractions of hours does it require for its complete accomplishment.

As is already intimated by the so very different duration, the slow colour change which came to observation in the fire-salamanders is something quite different: it rests essentially on the multiplication and diminution of particular pigment kinds; its immediate physiological cause is cell divisions; it is therefore not a movement-process but a growth-process, or occasionally also an involution-process. We may set this slow colour change, as the **morphological**, over against that other rapid one, the **physiological** colour change.

Certainly as one of the first, Fischel, in his very well-known controversy with Flemming, drew attention to this important distinction; it was also this controversy and its resolution that brought to light the remarkable result that **morphological and physiological colour change, over against many an external factor, strike out as it were opposite paths**: the object of investigation of the colour-change experiments of Fischel and Flemming was the larva of the fire-salamander, and the apparent contradiction emerging from the results consisted in this, that the one investigator, e.g. with warmth, saw the larvae become darker, the other lighter; the analogous holds for light-intensity, and holds with other objects (frogs) for moisture. The clarification follows from the fact that the same influence which stimulates the chromatophores to contract — thus to make the animal lighter — contributes, with longer action, to their multiplication, thus finally leads to dark colouration; and conversely, that an influence which permits the colouring-substance-carriers to pass over into the expanded, relaxed state — whereby the animal appears dark — in time brings about their resorption, thus has light colouration of the animal as its final goal. In many animals both states of "**light colouration**", contraction-state and resorption-state, likewise the morphological and physiological state of "**dark colouration**", namely expansion-state and reproduction-state of the pigment, can already be distinguished clearly with the naked eye, because they make the animal appear not in accurately the same colour: e.g. the contraction-state may condition a yellowish, the resorption-state a brownish, the expansion-state a black, the reproduction-state a grey-blue overall colouration, and the like. But in yet other animals the distinction is possible only by the microscopic route, and to these belongs the salamander larva. Whether such a larva possesses, for example, contracted pigment-cells in glaring light, or destroyed ones in enduring darkness, cannot be seen in it without further ado.

While in this example the different nature of morphological and physiological colour change becomes clear with respect to one and the same factor, in the following case the **coming-together of different factors** shows how the two processes strike out separate, independent paths. If one keeps toads (best *Bufo vulgaris*) or frogs (best *Rana temporaria = fusca*) on coloured grounds — e.g. the same that we first applied with the salamander (yellow earth, black earth) — then the morphological colour change brings about an analogous recolouration, which, however, is here far more perfect and means a real **colour-adaptation**, in that it thrives into strict agreement between ground-colour and animal-colour. For to the mere multiplication of the one and diminution of the other colouring-substance, which in the salamander lead merely to a shift in the surface-occupancy of its two principal pigment kinds, there is here added a further moment: the sympathetic recolouration of particular pigments that are present but originally not coloured alike with the milieu. It is possible that here Wiener's principle of colour-photography comes into its right. But that only in passing — a special treatise must report on the colour-adaptations of the frog-amphibians [Anura]: here it should only be emphasized that keeping on coloured grounds leads, in these animals, to a still more perfect agreement of the colouring than in the salamander. And yet the latter is not always equally well to be seen; it sometimes, e.g. in demonstrations, lectures, by no means comes into its own as well as the downright schematically distinct colour-shift of the salamander. Such a frog, a toad of the light ground, need only have sat for a while in the water, and the animal then appears, despite its actually smaller pigment-stock and richness in lighter pigments, perhaps pitch-black as a result of total expansion of the dark chromatophores present; or if one has an animal that has become very dark on a dark ground, whose skin teems with melanins, and has taken it along, say, to a public demonstration, and it appeared, an hour earlier still, in the colour-state corresponding to its pigment-richness, then at the decisive moment it may, as a result of far-reaching contraction of its chromatophores, appear lighter than the light, light-kept animal. The temporary suspension of the colour-action of the substrate occurs, in all these cases, probably — as in the last-mentioned — through fright, through the disquieting on being set into the demonstration-jars and on being shown around; thus also in that first case not through moisture, not through a counter-inducing external factor directly, but through inner factors, psychic states. In the chameleon and *Anolis* the whole colour change is governed and dictated almost exclusively only by excitation, well-being, discomfort, fear, anger, etc. —

All these facts had induced me to see, in the morphological and the physiological colour change, two fundamentally different processes, which have not the least to do with one another, which go their own and — as was earlier explained with the example of the salamander larva — often even contrary paths. The frequent confusion which, in the literature, has been allowed to befall them as a result of all-too-preponderant attention to outward appearance, determined me in particular to work out the contrasts most sharply at every offered opportunity (e.g. Kammerer 1910c). In the search for an inner explanation of the colour-shifts perceived in the salamanders, it now, however, gradually comes to seem as though here too there is no dualism of essence, but rather that what in the extremes appears different not merely quantitatively but also qualitatively is connected by gradual transitions. A common moment is already given in this, that recolourations, colour change **in the colouring-substance itself**, occur both in the morphological and in the physiological colour change (only here again more rapidly and reversibly than there). Important for our case are the following moments:

I said just before that the morphological colour change of *Salamandra* lets itself be demonstrated better than the analogous one of *Rana* and *Bufo*, because there the physiological colour change that crosses it causes no disturbances. Manifestation and apparent lack of physiological colour change are now, however, not, say, differences that coincide at the same time with the two amphibian orders, to which *Salamandra* on the one side, *Rana* and *Bufo* on the other side belong, — the tailed amphibians and the frog-amphibians; rather, also among the Caudata the movement-phenomena of the chromatophores are very widespread, in high degree e.g. in the nearest kinship of the salamander, in the water-newts or tritons (*Molge*). Indeed, even the fire-salamander itself, as a larva, exhibits a lively change of contraction and expansion of its colouring-substance-cells, and only in the metamorphosed animal does the rapid colour change resting upon this seem to have come entirely to a standstill. On some reflection it did not exactly remain probable that this exceptional position belongs to the salamander by rights; it was rather more probable that its physiological colour change might be inhibited or concealed by some secondary, fortuitous moment.

Such a moment in fact exists in the form of the **extraordinarily dense bedding of the pigments**, both of the yellow and of the black. This follows already macroscopically from the fact that the deep saturation of the black as of the yellow can only be a consequence of that crowding-together; for in the older larva both pigment kinds are already present, only not yet so dense, and in consequence they do not yet attain that intensity which the transformed animal presents a little later: whose black there is brown-grey, whose saturated chrome-yellow is a pale whitish-yellow. (Let it be expressly remarked once more: that I am here not allowing myself any confusion of the definitive yellow pigment with the golden iridescent pigment of the larva, which latter is resorbed at metamorphosis and was already recognized by Leydig as different in essence.)
