The dense bedding of the pigments in the finished salamander could, however, now hinder, to this high degree, movements on the part of the chromatophores from being carried out at all; but if they are carried out, one would not be in a position to notice this.

There are now really three stages at which, microscopically (in the youngest one even macroscopically), a change of contraction-state and expansion-state of the black as of the yellow pigment can be seen. The first of these stages is the older larva, standing at most still 4–6 weeks before metamorphosis, which indeed, as we have heard, already calls its own, besides the later-resorbed larval pigments, smaller stocks of the imaginal pigments. Everyone who in this period attentively follows the colour-development — be it even only with the free eye — (in order to exclude errors, this must take place on isolated specimens) will already have been surprised by the fact that yellow spots already distinctly laid down can again blur, indeed nearly disappear, only of course to be visible again, say on the following day, in full sharpness; the same happens with the cloud-like accumulations of the melanin, only it is for the unarmed eye far more conspicuous in the case of the yellow spots. Already weak magnification clears up this disappearance and re-emergence to the effect that the former corresponded merely to the contraction-state, the latter to the expansion-state of the chromatophores, but that in reality no such rapid alteration of the pigment-stock had taken place. The expansion of the yellow chromatophores can be directly called forth by sliding yellow paper underneath, their contraction by black paper. — The second stage at which the movements of the chromatophores can be seen coincides really with the first, or more precisely falls into its immediate continuation: it is the salamander just in the act of transformation, which is already beginning to follow its hydrotaxis that has become negative, but does not yet possess open gill-slits and its pigments in definitive quantity and density. The third, finally, is a grown salamander in which the pigments, as a result of under-nourishment, illness, or partial albinism, do not abut against one another without gaps. After some searching one then finds here too suitable places, especially in the armpits, the hollows of the knees, the bends of the thighs, on the throat and fore-breast, in the grooves which on the flanks delimit the individual somites, where the pigment-movements can be demonstrated, particularly by electrical stimulation and the like; and this time it is the melanin which lends itself more readily to it.

To the three stages just designated there is thus common the condition through which they are suited to the demonstration of the physiological colour change taking place: the relatively slight density of the pigment-bedding. If the deduction now following is correct, then not only is the salamander relieved of its exceptional position in general with respect to the non-possession of a physiological colour change, but it must besides also be assumed that the cell-movements conditioning it are not abolished by the crowding-together of the pigment, but at most restricted and withdrawn from our eye.

I said before that in older larvae a temporary, apparent disappearance of imaginal spots already laid down was to be observed. This temporary and apparent disappearance, brought about by chromatophore-contraction, becomes, however, a lasting, real **dwindling [Schwund]** caused by chromatophore-involution, as soon as those larvae are kept permanently on a coloured ground which brings about a counter-induction of the chromatophore-sort in question — e.g., then, for the carriers of the yellow pigment a black ground. And conversely, the re-emergence brought about by chromatophore-expansion, at first transitory, can persist, i.e. henceforth be interrupted by no contraction-state whatsoever, and further be followed by a powerful increase of the spot in respect of intensity and surface-area, as soon as those same larvae are kept permanently on a coloured ground which brings about a pro-induction of the chromatophore-sort in question — e.g., then, for the carriers of the yellow pigment a yellow ground. The mere expansion has then passed over into reproduction, into multiplication.

Concerning the manner and mode in which **chromatophore-movement, when it is progressive, turns over into reproduction, but when regressive into involution**, I have formed for myself the following conception:

If the cell persists in a state of expansion, then that loss of cohesion which releases its eventual disintegration, its division, must necessarily set in earlier. And if the external conditions are so constituted that they hold the expansion-state in permanence, then those divisions will be accomplished more frequently, on a greater number of pigment-cells, and will be repeated ever again on their division-products. Thus there would come about a brisk multiplication of those cells which contain a particular sort of pigment favoured by the conditions.

If the cell persists in a state of contraction, then the cohesion-loss leading to division must set in later; such cells will divide more rarely, multiply more weakly. Meanwhile the space which they would have claimed under brisker reproduction-activity is filled up by the other, rapidly propagating cell-kinds; thus they are at first hindered in an indirect way from spreading. But also in a direct way they are restricted, because their enduring contraction-state, to the opposing cells (and indeed not merely to the carriers of the other pigment, but to all the space- and nourishment-competitors in the struggle of the particles), must facilitate the crowding-out and melting-down. They are the more easily hemmed in, the conditions of their metabolism become ever more unfavourable, their involution begins under inadequate nutrition and respiration, [and] is completed externally through resorption of the small cells that have become weak, on the part of the incessantly advancing others.

This explanation fits all cases where physiological and morphological colour change tread the same paths, i.e. give the colour-changing animal the same appearance; here everywhere the physiological colour change can, in the described manner, condition the morphological one directed in the same sense, [and] be relieved by it. It is also teleologically evident how the physiological colour change represents a provisional arrangement, which lends the animal the appropriate covering-colouration, as well as it goes, with the means at hand, before these are directed and reshaped in accordance with the altered demands. The given explanation thus fits in the first place all cases of protective colouration, to which in the last analysis our salamander case too belongs; for although, as was emphasized, the yellow of the substratum need by no means correspond in its nuance to the yellow of the animal in order to bring the latter to multiplication — the animal that has become yellow thus not enjoying, in the strict sense of the definition, a "protective colouration" — yet experience teaches: even upon a differently-yellow ground a yellower animal is harder to see than a black one; it possesses no covering-colouration of the first quality, [but] a certain covering its colouration does grant it nonetheless. Since in any case our salamander case belongs to the cases of like-directed [direction] of physiological and morphological colour change, its coming-about, if the above explanation is correct, has become plausible.

Nevertheless I should like, in a few words, still to call to mind those cases which constitute an opposite thereto, where physiological and morphological colour change strike out on opposite paths, i.e. lend the animal, under like conditions, a contrary appearance. I will take these cases into consideration — as they have already found mention in this chapter in the shape of the salamander-larva pigments that, in warmth or glaring light, fell into contraction and nevertheless multiplied with time — for the reason that they could do damage to my explanation, formulated just above, of the connection between the two kinds of colour change. For the following conception, too, would be admissible: If the cell persists in a state of expansion, then it exposes — to any influences eventually damaging it, be it only through the spatial [demand] expressed in pressure and pull, [or] through the nutritional competition with neighbour-cells expressed in the withdrawal of substance and the production of the end-products of metabolism — at constant mass a larger surface, — a disproportion that will be felt the more, the more frequently the expansion-state, as we have heard, has necessarily led to cell-divisions and thereby to renewed surface-enlargement. The gaps arising through the dying-off of the cells affected by this become filled up by the competing cell-sort that thrives all the better by alternation. But the accelerated tempo of division also brings it about that the division-products become ever smaller and thereby, *ceteris paribus*, ever more accessible to eventual resorption.

If the cell persists in the state of contraction, then it is, before these damages brought about partly mediately, partly immediately by surface-enlargement and -division, preserved, and can therefore, even if it does not multiply more rapidly, yet for that reason hold out longer — always provided that the hostile elements supposed in the earlier explanation, favoured by their slight surface-unfolding, are not engaged in too unstoppable an advance.

The widespread opinion that contraction is to be equated with the activity-state, expansion with the inactivity-state of the chromatophore, I have purposely not let myself argue from often, since the latter perhaps does not always hold true, but on the contrary a stimulation sometimes seems necessary even for the bringing-about and maintenance of the expansion. For morphological consequences of the physiological colour change the functional moment would, insofar as it must carry with it a strengthening and multiplication of the demanded, [and] a weakening and diminution of the non-demanded cell-sort, apparently come still more into consideration.

I am of the view that the two explanations for the morphological consequences of expansion and contraction, which sound so contradictory in appearance, involve no real contradictions. Certainly expansion can sometimes bring about, as its end-aim, an over-growth of the pigment-cell-sort in question; another time, its exclusive destruction following all-too-rapid multiplication and all-too-long inactivity. Just as certainly contraction can sometimes bring about a crowding-out of the chromatophore-kind in question; another time its exclu-

> *Archiv f. Entwicklungsmechanik. XXXVI.* — 10 sive [survival], through the assumption of latency-states out of unremitting functional demand, [can] mean victory. Decisive for this would presumably be solely and alone the factor that achieves both — here the contraction, there the expansion —, namely the intensity and duration with which it acts. Common to both factors is that their excess in expenditure of energy and time ends ruinously for the affected cell-sort, [whereas] conducive, on the contrary, [is] a certain moderation, perhaps also a certain change of factors, an interruption — to some degree always again compensating the consequences in a positive and negative direction — of the one as of the other, of the state on the whole prevailing, manifesting itself in larger and coarser steps as [a] separated [state].

There are experimental supports for this view. One case I mentioned for another purpose in the Roux-Festschrift (Kammerer 1910c): lizards becoming melanotic up to 37°, from there on, at a higher [temperature], turning over from pigment-multiplication into pigment-destruction. Other cases are to be taken from the colour-change investigations of Mayerhofer, v. Frisch, Šečerov (1909), Megušar and others: fishes kept in the dark, [and] crabs, first becoming dark, then pigment-loss setting in.

Other dependencies, by which contraction or expansion of the chromatophores leads, as a further consequence, to multiplication or restriction of the pigment contained in them, result of course from the influence of the respective releasing-factor upon the neighbouring chromatophores carrying other pigment and upon the other concentration of the tissue-elements; or [from the fact] that the responsible factor at the same time favours or inhibits or even damages the latter, and in what measure all this comes to pass. Here, of course, searching experimental and microscopic, cell-physiological study must still set in. I am convinced that with its help one will attain the ideal which is almost already reached in the example, used just above, of the artificially melanotic lizard: to express in figures the critical point of the various kinds of energy up to which they act conducively, [and] from which on they act damagingly, upon various kinds of organism and tissue; or, if we wish to keep more strictly to this special problem: to find numerical degree-scales by which it can be determined whether and which physiological colour change finds its continuation in a morphological colour change of positive or negative sense (multiplication following contraction [or] expansion, [or] destruction of chromatophores).

## VIII. Summary of the Results.

1a) The fully developed fire-salamander (*Salamandra maculosa* Laur.) possesses four kinds of pigment: a (more saturated or paler) yellow, a (likewise graded) red, a particular whitish-yellow present only in tiniest accumulations, and a black (melanin). Never, in nature, are the black and the yellow lacking — the former mostly developed as ground-colour, the latter as marking, which seek to exclude one another, but in transitional stages, which often last for years, also mix. Upon superimposed layering of the yellow and black there arises a greenish, upon juxtaposed layering a dusky-yellow mixed colour; the former signifies as a rule a transitional stage toward pure yellow, the latter toward pure black.

1b) The older salamander larva too already possesses the black and the yellow pigment, only in a less dense layering, so that considerable **chromatophore movements** can be observed, which the fully developed animal shows only any longer in case of partial albinism or local pigment-destruction through hunger or sickness. The first spots [Makeln] of the yellow pigment lay themselves down on the eyelids, ear-glands [parotoid glands], and the roots of the extremities. If one exposes the animals to a yellow ground, then this has as its consequence expansion of their yellow chromatophores and further the division (multiplication) of the same; if one exposes them to a black ground, then there follows contraction of the yellow chromatophores and further the involution of the same. If one sets the larvae upon a yellow substratum, then the black chromatophores likewise contract and are later resorbed; but if one sets them upon a black substratum, then they expand and multiply.

2) The fire-salamanders occurring in the wild are phenotypes, which constitute themselves, with respect to their colour-dress, out of alternating combination and splitting of chiefly the following **elementary characters**: stepwise gradations in the quantity of the yellow (of the black); stepwise gradations in the intensity (colour-saturation) of the yellow; various kinds of marking-style (symmetrical, unsymmetrical; spotting, striping). Each two gradations of the colour-quantity and -saturation, as well as spotting and striping, form together an allelomorphic character-pair, whereby always the lower step is hypostatic ("recessive") to the higher; the character-pair "more — less colour" follows therein the blended inheritance with intermediate [heterozygotes], the remaining allelomorphs the alternative inheritance with "dominant" heterozygotes. Of this an exception is made only by "spotting — striping," when "striping" was newly produced (acquired in the experiment) in the manner indicated under 14, in which case intermediate (instead of dominant) F₁-hybrids (specimens with row-wise standing spots) arise, which however do not split in F₂, so that henceforth a new [character], traceable from throw to throw as from generation to generation as a weakening of the acquired character, remains "constant."

3a) The yellow marking-elements of the fire-salamander show, on yellow grounds (whose nuance need not coincide with the yellow of the animal), a growth conditioned by cell-multiplication (thus morphological), in that they at first stretch their processes toward one another, [and] gradually fuse. The larger spot thereby as a rule sends out a larger, mightier, or longer process. Thus the surface-area which the yellow pigment occupies on the skin of a salamander becomes greatly enlarged in the course of several years. This is most strongly the case for those body-regions which are most exposed to the light, hence preferably on the upper side.

3b) On black ground, on the contrary, the yellow marking-elements experience in their further spreading an inhibition, such as a skin-piece taken from the midst of the yellow region yields, which upon its regeneration is able to form indeed no black, but also the yellow pigment not again in full density, and its sparse quantities only very slowly.

4a) The black ground-colour of the fire-salamander shows on black ground a morphological growth, which concentrically constricts the differently-coloured marking-elements and gradually quite crowds them out; first the most exposed out-gliderations (processes, lobes) of the yellow spots are eaten up, so that the latter, upon their diminution, take on a form ever more closely approximating the circle-form, finally point-form, before they disappear. This colour-effect too is most strongly [pronounced] on the most strongly light-exposed dorsal side.

4b) On yellow ground, on the contrary, the black ground-colour experiences an inhibition, as skin-pieces taken from the midst of the black region prove, which upon their regeneration are able to form indeed no yellow, but also the black pigment only exceedingly slowly and loosely. And as hunger-animals further show, in which indeed both pigments become weakened, [but] the black remains capable of life and division when the yellow is already destroyed; the gaps thereby arising the melanin is not able to fill out on yellow ground.

5) On white ground, ground-colour and marking remain constant in form and surface-area, only the marking-elements experience a considerable bleaching of the yellow down to the palest yellowish-white. All differently-coloured grounds (red, green, blue, violet) are entirely neutral for the colour-dress of the salamander, likewise darkness and small quantities of light.

6) In wet surroundings there arise, in the midst of the black skin-region, isolated accumulations of yellow pigment, which appear as pale or greenish-yellow points and dabs, at most still taking on the form of drops (with a small process) or small horseshoes (with two processes), but then cease their growth, which now could be furthered only by a suitably coloured substratum. The reaction is strongest on those skin-regions which are most exposed to the moisture, hence preferably on the under-side.

7) In dry surroundings there arise, in the midst of the yellow skin-region, isolated accumulations of black pigment, which appear as darkenings or, when locally occurring with particular liveliness, as black halos in yellow marking-elements, and which can advance to the complete crowding-out of the yellow element in question. This reaction is strongest on those skin-areas which dry out most readily, hence on the dorsal side.

8) There are in nature certain earth-sorts which copy, in their direction, the colour- and moisture-effects of the artificial grounds: thus yellow loam-earth brings about enlargement together with multiplication, black garden-earth diminution and at the same time darkening of the yellow constituents of the colour-pattern. Since none other than the phenomena isolated on the coloured and differently-moist grounds of the experiment show themselves, it is to be assumed that any chemical, molar, and even temperature-differences of the two earths play no role. Inferences from these and other ground-kinds to the appearance of the salamander and other animals may, however, on account of the complexity and transformation-possibility of the factors, never be undertaken in free nature (see the results of the open-air-terraria), but only under their constantly and permanently shaped application in closer keeping.

9a) **Regeneration-processes** in the skin accelerate the colour-altering effect of the external factors, just as does the setting-in of the latter during the ontogenesis, in both cases also during the new-building of the necessary colour-material: yellow skin regenerates yellow, black [skin] black, speckly [skin speckly], on neutral ground under maintenance of the boundaries, on yellow [ground] under advancing of the yellow region into the black, on black [ground] under advancing of the black region into the yellow; on moist ground black skin regenerates yellow-dotted, on dry [ground] yellow [skin regenerates] black-dotted.

9b) Offspring of animals which have gradually regenerated, under colour-alteration, the greater part of their skin accessible to the colour-inductions do not differ from those of such parents as have absolved the analogous re-colourings without skin-regeneration [see points 13, 14 of the summary]. If a skin-piece is affected by regeneration that had previously gone through a (however conditioned) colour change, then the regenerate at first re-establishes once more the colour-state present before this change, before it passes over into the new one; only on colour-effective grounds is, according to whether they favour or suppress the pigment in question, that regenerative transitional stage either abridged or [does] it come into permanence.

10) The colour-effects enumerated under 3 and 4 require, for their carrying-out, the mediation of the photoreceptors and of the central nervous system: for they are lacking under exclusion of light and in blinded animals even in the light; on transplanted skin-pieces they come to appearance only as soon as these have entered into stimulus-conducting connection with the stem-skin.

11) The moisture-effects adduced under 6 and 7, on the contrary, are the work of the autonomous sensibility of the skin: under exclusion of light they proceed indeed only very weakly, for they require, in scarcely lesser degree than the colour-effects, a certain quantity of light as realizing factor; but in blinded animals they take place undiminished, [and] on transplanted skin-pieces they begin at once after their grafting-in.

12) Both the colour- and the moisture-effects, and already hereditarily fixed colour-displacements, whether they strive toward an increase or decrease of whatever pigment-kind, depend in their speed upon the temperature; a raising of the temperature therefore brings forth, in an otherwise ineffective milieu, both predominantly yellow and predominantly black salamanders, accelerates and furthers the yellow-becoming on yellow, the becoming-blacker on black ground.

13) Both the colour- and the moisture-effects are inherited by the descendants, in that, in such descendants as are subjected anew to the same factors, they at once [become] ver- 14) If one of the two pigment types has undergone a quite especially strong spread in the parents (to a degree such as is only ever possible through the effects of a coloured substratum, not through wet and dry surroundings alone), then in the offspring there occurs a **secondary bilateral-symmetrical partitioning of the acquired and inherited pigments**: in this, the dorsal side being taken into account, the predominant colour always appears on the flanks, the receding one compressed along the dorsal midline. If we regard, as is customary, the black as the ground colour, the yellow as the marking (although this relationship prevailing in *forma typica* is frequently reversed in the extreme representatives of the yellow culture), then such parents as had become predominantly yellow on yellow ground produce offspring with 2–4 rows of spots (on freshly yellow ground soon merging into continuous bands) along the flanks and the dorsolateral sides — the dorsal midline for the time being black; but those parents that had become predominantly black on black ground produce offspring with a single row of spots (later, on yellow ground, flowing together into a stripe) along the dorsal midline — the dorsolateral sides and flanks black.

15) Doubly multi-rowed [doubly spot-rowed] specimens that on black ground underwent a reduction and elimination of individual spots allow the rows to merge anew into bands if they are transferred to yellow ground in good time; if this transfer takes place so late that the longitudinal distance of the spots in the rows has become too great for merging, then the transverse distance often still suffices for this, and so, with favourable spot positioning, regularly **transversely banded** salamanders arise. With a positioning less favourable for this, a mixed mode can set in — in places longitudinal, in places transverse merging, whereby sometimes one and the same spot serves as mediator to its longitudinal and transverse neighbour and occasions the formation of right-angled, T-shaped, or still more complicated letter-shaped markings; or, if the spots already stand so sparsely that neither the transverse nor the longitudinal distance permits any regular connection, they grow out into oblique and crooked flourishes and loops. The distance over which chemotropic attraction between two spots, such that they finally merge, still takes place, may not exceed 10 mm.

16) Both through this aimless outgrowth of isolated spots, and also through the formation of transverse bridges in continuously striped salamanders between the two dorsal lines, or between the dorsal and flank band, or especially between the two flank bands ventrad, and finally also through the massive new appearance of spots, which enlarge and merge irregularly with the old elements of the marking, the (primary or secondary) symmetry of the colour-dress can be disturbed to the point of unrecognizability; but it reappears, in case the disturbance was bound up with a considerable increase of the pigment concerned, in the next generation, restored with the inclusion of the pigment quantities that had been added. There can therefore take place a **repeated alternation of symmetry and asymmetry, longitudinal striping, spotting, and transverse striping, or vice versa.**

17) If one exchanges the ovaries of different colour-varieties, then in races that one obtains ready-made from Nature there is shown **no influence whatever of the foreign germ-plasm by the foster-nurse [Tragamme].**

18) If, however, the foster-nurse belongs to an artificial race that arose in the experimental culture, then the transplanted germ-plasm is **re-tuned in the sense of the soma, drawn into the total body, which represents a chemically uniform whole connected in all its parts in a stimulus-conducting manner.** Nothing is altered in this if the experimentally produced new racial character resembles a long-established one occurring in Nature.

## IX. Factual confirmations, as well as literary criticism and counter-criticism of the results.

In the *Österreichische Rundschau* (KAMMERER 1911b) I wrote: "My influencings of the salamander colour through the colour of the surroundings are, in so far as they concern the first generation (for anything more, the re-examiners have as yet had no time), already in the meantime tested and confirmed from two different sides. This is a more favourable result than I myself expected: for as yet we are in general not so far with the biological experiment as with the physical and chemical, namely that we could repeat it readily any number of times and each time with exactly the same outcome. The life-event is indeed, even if

perhaps also in its essence not different from that of inanimate Nature, yet by its degree much more complicated and labile: a slight deviation, perhaps remaining unnoticed, in the conditions set by the experimenter, for example a seemingly negligible fluctuation of the humidity and light intensity — and already the living substance strikes out on **other paths of its development.** In the colour-dress of the salamander things fortunately seem to lie comparatively simply. And almost the whole secret of making the experiments succeed consists in keeping the experimental animals for years healthy, capable of reproduction, and willing to reproduce. The *conditio sine qua non* is therefore the technique of care: yet precisely this is readily underestimated by the professional zoologists or regarded as a triviality. . . . ."

The two confirmations that lay at hand at that time stem, on the one hand, from WIEDEMANN and concern a *Salamandra maculosa* that on wet black earth showed **spot-reduction on the upper side and the new appearance of numerous small spots on the under side,** as was the case in my experiment E 3 (Chap. IV, p. 76); on the other hand, as already cited several times, from SĚČEROV (1912e), and concern salamander larvae that during their development had been kept on yellow and black substratum (paper under glass) and after their metamorphosis already let the corresponding colour differences be perceived.

Since then — not yet a year has elapsed over it — three further confirmations have come to my knowledge: the one, likewise already mentioned, consists in experiments of v. FRISCH (still unpublished), which form a supplement to the previous ones, in that they use not larvae, but freshly metamorphosed full-grown animals as induction material and, besides keeping on yellow and black paper under glass, extend also to blinding. While the publication of these experimental series, which here, with the author's permission, are characterized only briefly as confirmation of mine, is still pending, the two latest, hitherto available ones are already to be found in the literature: first the case of GAISCH, which resembles that of WIEDEMANN, that is, consists in black colouration on black ground, but in turn distinguishes itself from it in that this time the ground consisted not of earth, but of peat-mull and was less moist. Accordingly the colour-effect consisting in spot-reduction was also not accompanied by the wet-effect consisting in spot-increase, but by the dryness-effect consisting in spot-darkening, as my experiment B 1 — explained by E 2, 3 — (Chap. IV, p. 51 and 72 respectively) requires.

Thirdly and finally the case of v. FEJÉRVÁRY, who reports the outgrowth of the black spots on the orange-coloured under side of a *Triton cristatus* Laur. var. *flavigastra* v. Fej. on keeping on black earth. Strictly speaking, this case is not a confirmation of my salamander experiments, but naturally of my analogous experiments with the crested newt (*Triton cristatus*), which I had communicated for the first time at the Graz Zoologists' Congress of 1910 (appeared 1911/12); but I may also adduce this result here, because it is after all a matter of the **same lawfulness, of the same problem solved in the same sense.**

It will be objected to me that with WIEDEMANN, GAISCH, and v. FEJÉRVÁRY it is, after all, only a matter of individual specimens, in which the described phenomena were observed by chance, without intention to experiment. Now if all those cases stood isolated, then, given the lack of control-keeping on non-colour-acting ground, one could indeed say that, in the form of the perceived, throughout very conspicuous colour changes, only the natural developmental tendencies of the specimens concerned had been followed; the same changes would have run their course likewise and in the same way on any arbitrary other ground. Against this, however, there now speaks, on the one hand, the considerable extent and the rapid tempo of the colour changes; I indeed had ample opportunity to follow the natural ontogenesis of the urodele colouration and know that the increase of a pigment lying in the race, even if it accompanies the growth of the animal at all, is, without the assistance of the ground, extremely slight and slow. This now holds entirely of the increasing black colouration of *Salamandra maculosa*, whose most frequent tendency points toward the opposite, in that older animals are almost always more richly marked than young ones. If one further takes the results of WIEDEMANN, GAISCH, and v. FEJÉRVÁRY together with my breeding results and control experiments and with those of SĚČEROV and v. FRISCH, then no one will probably any longer doubt that here, as there, exactly the same processes came to observation.

Similar processes, adaptive colour changes as in the salamander, have hitherto come to establishment in other animals only sparsely, although this is of course only valid from the experimental path: here my experiments on *Sal. maculosa* and *atra*, *Triton cristatus*, *Rana esculenta*, *temporaria* and *agilis*, *Bufo vulgaris* and *viridis*, *Bombinator pachypus* and *igneus*, *Lacerta oxycephala*, *muralis* and *serpa*, *Helix pomatia*, *Limax agrestis* and *cinereo-niger* (KAMMERER 1911/12) stand almost still alone; in Nature, however, the phenomenon is exceedingly frequent, and most, if not all, cases of **protective resemblance of colours** have surely come about directly in this way. In this respect I should not myself like to pass over an observation of SCHWEINFURTH, to which I was made attentive by THILO: "The skin-colour of the Bongo corresponds to the red earth on which they develop; its ground-tone is an earthy red-brown; over against this the Dinka appear black like the alluvial soil that they inhabit. This contrast, which recalls the masking-faculty so frequently observed in the animal kingdom or DARWIN's protective resemblance (mimicry), is here only an accidental one, but deserves, in order to support the reader's memory, mention after all. Whoever, coming from the north, following the course of the two source-tributaries of the White Nile, penetrates into the heathen Negro-lands and has at first come to know only Schilluk, Nuer, and Dinka, will, on arriving at the Bongo, easily be able to perceive that with this people a new series of races opens up for him toward the south. As the plants are children of the soil from which they have sprung, so here too the human being appears as it were as an expression of the terrain-difference created by the red iron-bearing rock. The inhabitants of the black-earthed lowlands, the Schilluk, Nuer, and Dinka gleaming in the deepest black of the Negro race, stand over against those of the red rock-earth, which latter, for all linguistic difference, despite divergent conditions of life and a pronounced peculiarity of the customs distinguishing them, nevertheless reveal themselves as a coherent whole." SCHWEINFURTH was here evidently very close to pronouncing the only possible interpretation of this human cover-colouration; he merely restricts it through the little word "as it were" (". . . as it were as an expression of the . . . terrain-difference"), and his view, that the colour-agreement here present is only an accidental one, would, firstly, never have been uttered if at that time animal experiments like those on the salamander had already lain before [us], and probably referred chiefly only to this, that no breeding-selection could be present, which then alone was available for the explanation of the protective resemblances; for at that time it was exclusively at one's disposal.

25 years later KLUNZINGER recognized, although still no (in the literature published) experiments lay at hand, already clearly from observations of Nature that the black ground takes at least a supporting part in the black colouration of the animals, and this holds especially of melanistic grass-frogs (*Rana temporaria* L. = *fusca* Rös.), which he found on moor-soil. It is indeed also quite correct that, besides the colour, still other factors can co-act in the same sense — in my experiments on the salamander this was especially the case on the part of the humidity; with the Negroes a heat- and irradiation-melanism may come into consideration —, but the decisive factor we may, in the case of natural finds not experimentally analysed, probably everywhere discern in the ground-colour, just as in the colour-change experiments on fishes, cephalopods, and crustaceans, which by themselves alone represent a whole literature, but mostly have to do only with rapid physiological colour-change without an actual alteration of the pigment stock. Yet morphological colour-change processes of more subordinate extent may often already have been run through and overlooked therein; MEGUŠAR has given them attention and has actually also found them in his crustacean experiments.

If, accordingly, the results everywhere, where they were won through empirical work, through observation or experiment, are the same as mine, then in works of summarizing and theoretical content rather the opposite is the case. And in particular I have, as is of course self-evident, met with the contradiction of that school of biologists which seeks the causes of organic variation merely in inner processes, [and] inheritance in displacements of the dispositions of the germ-plasm.

Above all it is now the work of BAUR that has attained great diffusion and authoritative attention. Thus I find in its review by BENDL that my experiments are said to have been interpreted by BAUR in a new and very interesting conception; in a lecture KOCH appeals to BAUR, according to whom "the high theoretical significance which these (KAMMERER's) experiments seemed to have for general biology as proof of the inheritance of acquired characters has recently again become questionable." It is, [he says,] a matter therein "only of modifications analogous to those which one can obtain in plants by alteration of the location." In the original, in BAUR, it then reads, with reference to the results occurring in the present treatise: "One has just especially very frequently overlooked that the modification needs a certain time in order to become fully formed, and that this time is often longer than the duration of one generation. . . . If, on the other hand, KAMMERER modified his salamanders by culture on yellow loam-ground in such a way that they obtained a more and more spreading yellow colouration, then he could not attain in one generation the maximum of the modification toward yellow attainable under these culture conditions, but for this there seem to me — to judge by the experimental results published by him — at least two, perhaps three generations to be necessary. Nevertheless, however, such a transformation of the colour is only a **modification** in our sense. The heritable character of *Salamandra maculosa* is not a definite colouration and marking, but a definite — and indeed a bounded! — **modifiability** of the colouration; and that this **modifiability** has been altered, the experiments of KAMMERER have hitherto given no point of support" (p. 260).

The rejoinder to this novel "conception," which, like so much else about our problem solved to a far-reaching extent, can only be a matter of "conception" any more, but which, as we have seen, has already been accepted without further ado by some authors as a new possibility or actuality — the rejoinder thus unavoidably necessary precisely for this reason has been taken off my hands by SEMON (1912) already so far that I do best to set down his words here: "Hitherto one has understood by 'modification' in the narrower sense a purely somatic alteration which proceeds without a simultaneous alteration of the germ-cells, in which an induction of the latter is lacking. This chief characteristic of 'modification' (deriving from BAUR himself — Ref.) as opposed to heritable variation BAUR now lets fall, for how is, without an induction of the germ-cells, an accumulation of the stimulus-effect from generation to generation conceivable? What criteria for heritability and non-heritability of an alteration then still remain to us? And has there not, furthermore, in the KAMMERER accumulation-experiments an 'alteration of modifiability' taken place?" (p. 151, 152).

That this last question is to be answered in the affirmative, SEMON now grounds, following on, with an alteration produced by me in *Alytes* — I will here keep to the work presently laid before [the reader] and give for consideration whether it does not then signify an **alteration of modifiability** when, for example, a salamander that stems from artificially yellow-made parents now also shows, on black ground, still an increase of its yellow marking and only late a very slow diminution of the same, while the normal modifiability (BAUR), the "reaction-norm" (WOLTERECK) points to [the fact] that a salamander kept on black ground at once and relatively much faster loses in marking, gains in black ground-colour.

Baur's expectation, that even two generations might not suffice to achieve the maximal modification, but that perhaps a third generation (not yet presented in my preliminary communication) would be required for this, has been fully confirmed; under continued keeping on yellow ground the grandchild generation (p. 41) is much yellower still than the daughter generation. How Baur, in the face of this state of affairs which he himself had correctly foreseen, arrives at the assertion that the modifiability in question is definitely bounded, is incomprehensible to me. I had paid this question particular attention, even without knowing Baur's opinion, in the experiments concerned (pp. 47, 58), and had indeed found that the reactions proceed more slowly from a certain, very advanced level of modification onward — probably for crudely mechanical reasons, owing to the impossibility of displacing the other pigment — but that *there is no point of reference for a real limit of modifiability*, for the animal can become *totally black*, probably with the overcoming of greater difficulties, *just as totally yellow*; with respect to the dorsal side the latter has already been attained. *The limit of modifiability is therefore identical with the limits of the body surfaces available for the character in question*; only when the entire pigment-bearing epidermis is filled with the one pigment can it, as a matter of course, no longer spread further. But if a limit of modifiability is to be posited in order to demonstrate that modifications cannot lead to species-formation, and if it is accordingly said that a *Salamandra maculosa*, having become entirely yellow or entirely black, nevertheless always remains only a *Salamandra maculosa*, so that even with factually unlimited modifiability of the colour characters no change of species occurred — then it must, against this, be recalled that it is but seldom that, with a change in the conditions of life, a single property alone is modified, but rather that this as a rule happens together with several properties; or that the one external factor modifies this character, the other that character, in disparate, indeed discrepant directions, and then, from the organism concerned, even under the assumption of a bounded modifiability, surely makes what every investigator must designate as "another species," indeed even as a systematic category of still higher rank (cf. also Chap. X, concluding paragraph). Baur himself, accordingly, also holds it (p. 260, below) to be probable that local races and very closely related species often differ predominantly or exclusively through "modifications"; he insists only on this, that modifications never lead to the progressive differentiation and formation of really new characters.

Apart from the fact that one must then ask what is even still to be understood by a really new character at all, the whole matter, after this concession, would seem to come down to a mere verbal dispute: for if species differ only through modifications, and if modifications increase through two or several generations, hence pass through the germ-track, that is, presumably, can be inherited, then the distinction between modifications and mutations — precisely in the sense in which I had demanded it in the Roux Festschrift of 1911 — is removed, and likewise no opposition of opinions other than a purely verbal one any longer exists.

In order that one may now speak of a really new, really newly acquired character, Baur, and all modern "geneticists," evidently demand that even every disposition, every predisposition toward it should have been lacking in the organism concerned before the onset of the induction experiment. But that is an impossible demand, which once again shows in the most striking way how very much the whole controversy has become a mere formality, brought about by an inexact framing of the problem, or mostly by the hard-to-eradicate inadequacies of our language, of our vocabulary. The disposition, inclination, potency, capacity, or however one wishes to call it — in a word, the *possibility* of altering a property and of developing it further to the point of unrecognizability, to the point of complete novelty, must indeed be given; in that sense even the protozoon must already contain the "disposition" to unfold a chorda dorsalis, so that it may finally become a vertebrate — in that sense even the backbone, or any other organizational character of the highest vertebrates, is, in relation to the amoeba, no "really new character"; but really new characters in another sense, in the sense of the geneticists and Mendelians, there are simply none at all — there never have been, and there never will be. The demand that the experimenter must, in order to induce a really new, genuine "acquired" property, first create possibilities that are in no way, however latent, already given to the protoplasm concerned, means nothing less than that he must become God and accomplish a new act of creation.

That I do not exaggerate with this presentation is evident from another objection of Baur's against my experiments, which indeed does not concern salamander coloration, but rather the reproduction of the midwife toad (*Alytes*): yet, in view of the kinship of the whole question, I will nevertheless touch on it quite briefly, since otherwise I should perhaps find no occasion for it anywhere in the foreseeable future, and since I do not like to write "rejoinders" *ad hoc*. "Proved," says Baur (p. 38) of my experiments of breaking the midwife toad of the paternal brood-care by heredity — whereby the eggs are deposited spontaneously into the water (instead of on land) and the larvae hatch at the stage with external (instead of first with internal) gills — "proved, however, is here too no inheritance, for here too the conditions which modified the mother animals have also acted upon the next generation. These animals themselves have, after all, undergone their first development under quite different conditions than normal comparison animals; they were, in the egg stage, under abnormally high temperatures, were deposited into the water as larvae with external gills, etc."

In this argumentation one must distinguish between the purely external and therefore incidental circumstance that my experimental method has been incorrectly reproduced by Baur — who apparently had before him only an abstract of my work — for I had indeed taken particular care, precisely in a series of unnoticed control experiments, to withdraw already the egg stage, indeed the formation and maturation of the germ substances, from the responsible elevation of temperature; and the circumstance lying in the very nature of the matter, that the larvae do indeed reach the water prematurely, for *this premature hatching is itself one of the characters newly evoked by the external factor!* It is therefore the demand that has been raised, that for the purpose of proving the inheritance of acquired characters one may not evoke any acquired character, since the conditions set by it itself, by its mere presence, signify a new induction and therefore individual, not hereditarily taken-over experience! But that means removing the whole problem from the sphere of the research-based treatment accessible to us, in a manner similar to that in which the same problem has also been removed by the well-known, universally applicable objections of Weismann, that it is a matter of atavism, or that the transmission is the work of the reserve-determinants assigned to every somatic cell. Applied, for example, to the colour salamanders, Baur's objection would have to read thus: the animal possesses a greater number of yellow spots than the normal comparison animal; consequently it lives under altered (chemical, morphogenic) stimulus conditions, and if it now acquires still more yellow, this is to be ascribed to those conditions and not to inheritance.

To superfluity, the objection of Baur's last discussed appears, as Semon has already brought into the field, refuted for *Alytes* by the fact that mere transfer of the eggs into the water, without the parents possessing altered brood-care instincts, has, despite the earlier hatching of the larvae, no hereditary effect; that, finally, conversely, eggs which stem from parents with altered instincts, but are matured on land — so that now the larvae do not reach the water prematurely — nevertheless inherit the corresponding alteration — all of them control experiments which Baur has overlooked. And in the case of the inheritance of the full-newt-bearing (instead of larva-bearing) of *Salamandra maculosa*, I succeeded in refuting, expressly and directly, the objection which I had long foreseen, in that I cut out the larvae of demonstrably full-newt-bearing females at the stage at which they should otherwise have been born, and nevertheless saw them, without the female's own individual experience of a delayed being-born, grow up into full-newt-bearers (not yet published elsewhere; here communicated provisionally as a supplement).

The objections of Cuénot for the most part coincide with those of Baur; Cuénot too clings principally to the point that nowhere are new properties evoked, but only hidden dispositions ("oscillating mutations," which agrees pretty much with Baur's "modifiability" of every character, which alone is inherited, not the character as such) are awakened. In that widest sense, already discussed above, in which alone this "disposition theory" can hold and which is downright synonymous with the principle of causality, since it asserts only that without precondition nothing can arise, one may indeed now subscribe to it. The poverty of the results found fault with by Cuénot has in the meantime been superseded, and need not even have come into consideration any longer in his book (1911!), in which, however, he takes account only of my first work, published in 1907, on the inheritance of enforced reproductive adaptations. And when Cuénot finally puts these in parallel with those of Standfuss and Fischer, in that he says that by no means all, but only individual animals subjected to the experimental factor exhibit the expected alterations and inherit them, then this is, both for my own and for the new experiments of the two other authors, simply incorrect.

I have now to come to terms with the objection that I have not drawn any "pure lines" in the sense of Johannsen. At bottom even this objection is identical with the earlier one, since it reckons with the eventuality that what appears at the induction material as newly evoked is in reality only enticed forth, but is already always contained therein as a latent racial property. Only in a line pure in this respect could the latter no longer be the case. Most sharply has this been expressed by v. Hanstein: "The observations and experiments of the author are doubtless interesting, insofar as they once again illuminate the plasticity of certain properties of organisms. For the question of the inheritance of acquired characters they are, however, not conclusive. Apart from the fact that in a large part of the experiments the altered conditions act also upon the germ cells, so that it is not established whether it is really a matter of somatogenic alterations, there is the further point that the author has not been intent, with the requisite care, on the breeding of quite pure material. Through the works of Johannsen on the botanical, of Lang and Woltereck on the zoological side, the importance of pure lines has recently been demonstrated. On further continuation of his breeding experiments the author will have to take this circumstance more into account than hitherto, and will also have to grasp more sharply the concept of acquired characters, as it has been worked out especially through Weismann's critical works. Only then will it be possible to judge whether the experiments, in themselves very interesting, also open up new points of view in this direction."

I shall here occupy myself, of these objections, only with the one that concerns the "pure lines," while the rest shall be discussed only in the following chapter. That work with pure lines or biotypes would be the ideal, I readily concede; that, however, this ideal, for work with our object, the salamander, is an impossibility, I have set forth on p. 93 — here I only recall once more the long time (at least 3½ years) that elapses until sexual maturity, and that robs us of the technical possibility of crystallizing out biotypes of the salamander colour, since with the complicated phenotypes that confront us in nature in this respect, more numerous generations would be required for this than the human lifespan, so long as it is productively scientifically capable of achievement, permits one to follow.

E. G. Boulenger, who had my preliminary communication (1910a) before him, clothes his misgiving in the following words, which I reproduce in German translation: »In view of the geographical distribution of the typical form and of the var. *taeniata* ... Kammerer's results are surprising, for his figures show that the offspring of a female of the typical form, probably from Austria, belonged to the var. *taeniata*. Considering the enormous number of specimens of both forms which he kept in his terrarium (sic! —: ›in his terrarium‹ — Ref.), is the possibility then excluded that a confusion took place? Or could not the male have belonged to the latter variety and have transmitted its characters to the offspring? Further, as regards the changes in the markings of one and the same individual in the course of its growth, could not some error of identification have crept in? After the study to which I have subjected the distribution of the markings, I can scarcely refrain from expressing a doubt as to whether the middle specimen of the lower left-hand row in Fig. 1 is the same as its neighbour on the right, for one will see that the two yellow stripes or rows of spots are far more widely separated from one another in the younger stage than in the older, and this is a change of which it is hard to conceive that it could have been accomplished in one and the same individual. Indeed, were it not a question of establishing the standing of an authority such as Kammerer, whose experiments appear to have been carried out with so much skill, care, and patience, I believe I should not have hesitated to designate the two figures in question as having been obtained from two different specimens.«

To begin with the last point, I may surely presume that Herr E. G. Boulenger has himself already reared a very large number — at least some hundred — *Salamandra maculosa taeniata* from the metamorphosis onward, and has followed exactly, by drawing, the transformations of their distribution of markings, so as to be able to judge whether the approach of the two dorsal rows of spots in the direction of the median line really represents so unusual a phenomenon?! For this rearing is, of course, the sole means thereto. In my protocols, which are indeed laid out graphically after the manner of the plates accompanying the present work, that convergence, and likewise a reciprocal divergence, is found several times. In particular, the growth-series incriminated by E. G. Boulenger is here reproduced on Pl. IV more fully, with more stages, than was the case in the preliminary communication (1910a) cited by him. From the assurance that such gross confusions as Herr Boulenger, despite his guarded mode of expression, does after all ascribe to me cannot have occurred to me, I may well consider myself exempt; for the rest, I refer to the precautionary measures detailed in Chap. IV A, 2 (Recording of protocols, p. 30), which leave the observer's subjective need to distinguish almost no, and in many cases no, latitude at all. That the male belonged to the *taeniata* is impossible: for I naturally do not let such animals come into contact with the others, and the experiment begins long before sexual maturity; moreover, striping is in fact recessive and would not have come to appearance in F₁. I should rather concede a certain clumsiness, and an inaccuracy caused thereby — I am no draughtsman — in the local entry of the elements of the marking, at which I myself have often enough caught myself; spots and rows of spots can also appear nearer to or farther from the margin, according to the state of tension of the skin and according to whether the animal, in lying, flattens itself more or less. Thus, seen from the flank, a large part of the dorsal marking can sometimes remain visible, in other cases not. But these contingencies, too, do not apply to the case objected to by Boulenger, for, as said, the phenomenon recurs several times, is not nearly so rare as Boulenger believes. His notion that an experimenter keeps his entire experimental and breeding material together in one terrarium is, however, so naïve that I rather more correctly suspect, in Boulenger's use of the singular, a misprint or a lapsus of expression.

## X. Theoretical Evaluation of the Results.¹

### 1. Variation.

The facts bearing on the problem of variation in the individual itself scarcely require any special theoretical interpretation. That

> ¹ Hereto, as an absolutely necessary supplement, the foregoing chapter, which I separated off from the present one only on account of the otherwise too great extent, and in which I occupied myself chiefly with the objections directed quite specially against **my** investigation, while the X. chapter deals more with the general objections.

individual variation (»modification«) is possible under the influence of the physical environmental factors is no longer denied by anyone; that the plasticity of the somatic plasma is at times an extraordinarily high one no longer surprises today either. A case of such great variability, which moreover has the aptitude to bend itself entirely under the will of the experimenter, now lies before us once more in the colouring and marking of the salamander. In connection with what I have said (p. 79) about the well-nigh unlimited possibility of producing by design any desired form of marking, I may now even venture to interpret colour-types found in nature, or to read off, for individual captured specimens, from the hieroglyphic script of their marking, their own life-fate and that of their immediate forebears.

I will attempt this with a few specimens which E. G. Boulenger figures: Fig. 100a (asymmetrically large-spotted, two small spots on the left flank) has evidently lived in a milieu which was very favourable to spot-enlargement, less favourable to spot-multiplication; perhaps on not very moist loamy soil, or on a soil covered with much yellow fallen foliage; 100b (almost black, the small spots that have remained over are not pure yellow) in a milieu that brought about both spot-diminution and spot-darkening, hence presumably offered a black substrate and was also relatively dry, and indeed apparently already through several generations. 101a (thin yellow median line, in several places indications of newly arisen spots in droplet form) had parents which lived in conditions similar to 100b, but has itself come into conditions which were somewhat more favourable to the development of the yellow pigment, probably contained a more loam-mixed and therefore also somewhat moister soil; 101b (a dorsal row of moderately large spots, keeping approximately to the median line, one parotid spot lacking) appears to be a hybrid, namely between a moderately spotted, asymmetrical specimen and one that was median-striped, somewhat like 101a, hence also had the same antecedents; 101c must have lived in a fairly moist milieu, for the spots are many, but not large, mostly roundish spots present (this agrees excellently with the find-spot [Hütteldorf near Vienna], where I myself live and where the moisture penetrates high up into the house-walls); 101d has probably lived on loamy soil, for evidently spot-enlargements and -multiplications have taken place simultaneously — and because the former allowed letter-like markings to come about in the form of flourishes, the specimen probably originally possessed very few, probably medianly arranged spots, and thus descended from parents which had to suffer a considerable loss of yellow marking; 101e probably has just such parents, but itself underwent strong enlargement and also a fairly brisk multiplication of the spots, which medianward almost fused into a band — then there set in, upon the otherwise yellowish ground, a period of drought, and in the yellow region islands of melanin arose (here too the find-spot [in Asia Minor] presents no difficulty for the assumptions). I will not now pursue these attempts at interpretation — which, of course, can only be mere attempts — any further; they were meant to serve me only for illustrating anew that we have all those forms of marking, with the aid of 1–3 generations, completely in our power.

For the rest, I wish, as a further contribution to the problem of variation, to set down here once more the grounds, already emphasized in the earlier text as well, why the colour-changes of the salamander observed by me — in so far as they are not moisture-effects, are specific colour-effects, and consequently, although no actual colour-correspondence, no protective colouring in the narrower sense, comes about — nevertheless represent genuine adaptations, not merely reactions to different degrees of light-intensity. Those grounds consist chiefly in the fact that the animals react only to those colours which they themselves possess, and that to the action both of the yellow and of the black colour there belongs a very high light-intensity, since the phenomenon of blackening too fails to appear already in twilight and naturally still more in total darkness or under blinding. Light-intensity as such expresses itself quite otherwise, namely first, if it is very great, through the bleaching-out of the yellow marking-elements, which remain constant in form, size, and number, secondly as a realizing factor of the two determining factors of the salamander's colour-change: of colour and of moisture. Incidentally remarked, the present investigation testifies what great importance attaches to the distinction, proposed by Roux, between the transforming factors into realizing and determining ones. Specific colour-effects which exhibit a certain similarity to those observed in the animal are known even from inanimate substances: Stobbe found such effects in the organic mixtures known as »fulgides«, which in monochromatic light of a given intensity darken more rapidly than in the same light combined with other rays, e.g. in white light.

Megušar has been led by his experiments on *Gelasimus*, *Potamobius*, *Palaemonetes*, and *Palaemon* to the result that the correspondence of the animal's colour with the colour of the surroundings is here only an accidental and transitory one, brought about purely by quantity of light, not by quality of colour. This finding cannot, of course, do away with the fact that very many animals, including notably the Crustacea — comprising the species used by Megušar — are adapted to their surroundings down to the finest detail and permanently; but for that, for coloured adaptation, colour-effects are of course required, and precisely because in Megušar's experiments there were, instead, presumably only unspecialized light-effects in play, the adapted permanent states failed to appear. Alongside this, one must of course also not again overlook the fact that at least a large part of the complexes of phenomena observed by Megušar rest upon physiological colour-change, only few in comparison upon morphological, which by its whole nature is more labile and more fleeting than morphological colour-change. To generalize from Megušar's results that the Crustacea owe their sympathetic colouring to the active finding-out of similarly coloured surroundings means — the spatial conditions of free nature being taken into account — to credit the animals with a faculty of wandering and seeking which in reality they do not possess — quite apart from the fact that Megušar's experiments bearing on this, as he himself says, appear quite insufficient for establishing any such activity.

### 2. Inheritance.

The inheritance of the variation is, in our case — since it is a matter of »inheritance of an acquired character« — best discussed theoretically in such a way that I arrange the presentation according to the various objections raised against it. In brief formulation, the objections may be characterized as follows: 1) It is no matter of an »acquired«, i.e. really new and somatogenic, character or excitation-effect. — 2) It is no matter of inheritance, but only of »after-effect« or of isolation of pure lines, or of transgressive adequate modificability of a character, regulable ökologisms after the manner of the amphibious plants. — 3) It is a matter of the action of selective breeding. — 4) It is a matter of atavism. — 5) The inheritance takes place through direct or parallel induction of the germ-plasm; the inherited variation has, accordingly, nothing to do with the somatic one, but arises at once independently of it as germinal, as mutation.

Some of these five chief objections do indeed partly coincide, but each has, again, also something peculiar to it. Thus the isolation of a pure line is likewise mediated by selective breeding, yet allows a certain subordinate, as it were merely sifting-out, action of the external factor to hold good, and does not, like the actual selection-objection proper, work with a creative enhancement of the characters brought about by selection. The atavism-objection too is, nowadays, closely akin to it, now that »reversion«, »atavism« equal »adaptation«, »inheritance« has ceased to be an empty word, but has found content, or resolution, through our recognition that it comes about through the unambiguously determined adding or lacking of, in each case, different developmental factors (= substances, for all I care determinants, genes); in distinction from the two other selection-objections, it only expresses that something in exactly the same, now apparently new, form was already there, whereas »pure lines« and »omnipotence of breeding« only require that it had been present at all in the germ-plasm already, without having necessarily already manifested itself precisely so. While, then, some of the enumerated objections coincide, others run — in the sense of those who raised them — contradictory. Thus, when of one and the same acquired character it is said the one time that it is only a modification, the other time that it is a mutation. Therein is shown again the untenability of the constructed distinction and the formal nature into which the disputed question has turned; for if the opponents say that so-called acquired characters are nothing but mutations, then I can (see further below, p. 177) declare myself in agreement therewith, provided that the proposition, in the sense of R. Goldschmidt's lucid exposition, may also hold good in its inversion: mutations are nothing other than acquired characters!

The selective-breeding objection in the narrower sense I already eliminated through my experimental arrangement, since indeed the observed variation- and inheritance-effects proceed even under strict negative selection, and under positive selection only attain the same degree about one generation earlier. The cause of this slight progress of selection, even where it is applied by design, we saw in the slowing-down of the reactions that follow upon the indispensable action of the external factor, the further they have already advanced — which, however, in turn is not equivalent to a real limitation of the modificability.

The objection that it is no matter of anything new, but of atavism or latency, I have already refuted in the foregoing chapter (p. 159) more with the help of the logical counter-proof, in that I showed how the opponents so greatly narrow the concept of the organically new that, in their sense, even the organizational characters of the animal phyla appear as nothing new, but as something given through predispositions, unless the whole phylogeny be denied. In any case the problem is thereby moved outside the sphere of its solubility; for upon the standpoint that it is a matter of isolation of pure lines or of atavism one can, after all, if one will, always fall back. As regards the pure lines in particular, I have likewise already shown that their emergence would have had to be discovered through mere sifting-out or release with the help of the experimental arrangement followed by me (control experiments! uniform and non-uniform appearance of the experimental population at the conclusion of the experiment!) — with full acknowledgement of the circumstance that it would be more favourable to work with an object whose succession of generations, sexual and phenotypic constitution permit from the outset the use of a pure line. But would one not then say that this pure line is only apparently such a one, is in truth a phenotype whose splitting has merely not yet succeeded, just as with many chemical »elements« too their composite character only came out late? That I must for the present leave to the future.

In the present case, in order to be able to render probable the atavistic nature at least of the striped salamanders descended from animals that had become yellow, one will appeal to Eimer's theory, according to which every marking always begins with longitudinal striping, advances to spotting, then passes over to transverse striping, and ends with disappearance of the marking. In how far this theory can hold generally will be discussed further below. Here let only — in the closest connection with the inheritance-problem — the gonad-exchange and its hybridization-analysis be recalled, both of which now, after (at least for me) every obscurity or possibility of chance has vanished, can directly serve as in each case a criterion of whether a character is old or new. If it is old, and therefore — be it in manifest, be it in latent, form — has already been present, then the transplanted germ-plasm remains uninfluenced, and in crossing with an allelomorph it splits according to the Mendelian rules, as was the case with my reversion of *Alytes* to the brood-care-less reproductive habits of the ancestors (Kammerer 1911e). If the character is new, then morphogenic stimuli of sufficient strength proceed from it so as to retune the foreign germ-plasm correspondingly, and in admixture with the corresponding (allelomorphic) unaltered character it yields intermediate, non-splitting bastards that gradually sink back into the norm. Since the latter holds true of the striping arisen in the experiment out of spotting, in contrast to that found in nature, I can judge that the artificial striping, although it is not to be distinguished from the natural striping, represents a new and not an atavistic character. How it then comes about that a Mendelian recessive remains pure in a heterozygote whose soma carries the dominant character is explained precisely from this, that all Mendelizing characters are either old or (as in brood-care versus non-brood-care of *Alytes*) atavistic. For Albino germ-plasm too, e.g., which lies in a wild-coloured heterozygote, »wild colour« signifies no new character which would have to exert a morphogenic stimulus upon the germ-plasm devoid of pigmentation-potency, in such wise that the recessive grandchildren proceeding therefrom should after all turn out partly pigmented; for the Albino derives itself everywhere from the wild-coloured animal. I assume thereby, of course, that old characters, as a rule, send out no morphogenic stimulus at all, or no longer a sufficiently strong one, up to the germ-plasm, because all clean gonad-transplantations (Castle and Phillips, Morgan, Harms, Heape) show no influencing of the offspring through the foster-mother; Semon (1912) does not go so far, and maintains the eventuality of a morphogenic or positional stimulus that always persists, even though a weak one; the remaining-pure of the recessive in the heterozygous dominant, which incidentally in a number of cases will not quite agree, is explained Incidentally, the atavism objection, as Semon already set forth in 1907, plays no further role from the moment it is demonstrated that the germ-plasm is inducible through conductive influences from the soma. This demonstration I furnish in that I thereby simultaneously refute the objection of direct and of parallel induction. One will not impute to me that I, despite my positive results regarding the "inheritance of acquired characters" (I here use the term deliberately once more in the indeterminate sense), approached the question of whether somatic or parallel induction with a preconception. At most one could speak of a preconception in favour of parallel induction, whose possible application to my cases I have in all my previous works expressly kept open. Indeed, under the influence of Tower's account I let myself be misled — at least for the beetles and a few other examples, thus Przibram's heat-rats (1910) and my *Alytes*-neoteny — into concluding a lecture for the Vienna Society of Physicians (Kammerer 1910 b) with the following words: ". . . which path do the influences of the external world take when they call forth heritable characters? It is the direct, physical path to the germ-substances, not the indirect physiological path from other bodily changes to germ-changes!" Only Semon's critique (1910, 1912) of Tower's interpretation admonished me again to caution, and now I am, through my gonad-transplantations, convinced of the occurrence of somatic induction — not for that reason yet of the non-occurrence of direct induction. Convincing became, namely, the explanation which the outcome of my gonad-transplantations furnishes for why the somatic and the hereditary properties in many cases — as bastardization and transplantation display them — can be so independent of one another, in certain other cases not; this explanation, the differing action of natural and artificial race, of old and new heritable property, was moreover still suited to bring the "inconceivable" somatic induction at least somewhat nearer to our circle of representation. This seems to me, namely (in partial leaning upon Semon), to have come about through my comparison, respectively the homologization of the germ-induction on the part of the soma with other, familiar physiological stimulus-processes; the new property (even when it is seemingly identical to an old one) exerts upon its nearer and wider surroundings a stimulus-action, simply because it is new, as it were still unaccustomed to its bearer (comparable, say, to the effects which are also exerted by inorganic compounds only *in statu nascendi* upon other substances). With time and habit this stimulus-action blunts itself, and even though it may never wholly cease, it nevertheless becomes too weak to exert now still the remote-action required for the re-tuning of the germ-plasm. But by then this has also already become superfluous, namely for unfolding anew, in each generation, the property concerned from which it proceeded; for by then the property has long since passed over into the genotypical possession — it has, at the time when strong stimulus-action and hence conduction of excitation still subsisted, been implicated and inserted (Roux) in the germ-plasm, i.e. fitted in at the proper place, and henceforth requires the centripetal translation no longer, while its centrifugal evolution (Néoévolution — Roux) out of the germ-plasm continues on undisturbed.

Indifferent whether this explanation be accepted or not, I could thus, on the ground of the gonad-transplantation, hold the proof to be furnished that in the case of the inheritance of acquired salamander-colouration somatic induction was at play; yet I must indeed still duly emphasize that this proof can, self-evidently, also no longer be shaken by investigations of "The Environment of the Germ-plasm," of which, within the framework of a larger cycle inaugurated by Przibram (1912), the one worked out by Šećerov (1912 a) deals immediately with the enjoyment of light within the salamander body. Šećerov has demonstrated that ¹⁄₁₇₃ of the external light-quantity penetrates through the body integument as far as the gonads, in which the yellow places have a 3- to 4-fold greater share than the black ones. Only this much is demonstrated and asserted by Šećerov, no more; yet it was to be foreseen that a part of the literature would at once take up this result in such a way as though thereby the parallel induction of the gonads (and not, say, merely its possibility) were also proved. Thus a (in other respects excellent) review by F. M. in the "Naturwissenschaftliche Rundschau" says: "Hereby, for the fact established by Kammerer that the salamanders that have become yellow on yellow earth transmit this property to the descendants, the assumption of a somatogenic inheritance . . . is rendered superfluous." Over against this, Semon in 1912 (pp. 118, 119) pointed out how improbable it is that a light-quantity which must be chosen very considerable — in order that it (and moreover under the presupposition that it is reflected in a definite colour) should, through the mediation of the enormously specialized and sensitive eye as photoreceptor, bring about the colour-changes in question in the skin — could in 173-fold attenuation bring about the adequate changes also in the germ-plasm, in the absence of the light- and colour-sensitive receiving-apparatus. Incidentally remarked, with the lizard, to whose enjoyment of light Šećerov's most recent work (1912 b) is devoted, the circumstances for a direct induction of the germ-cells are essentially still more unfavourable, since here, in the little-exposed belly-region, ¹⁄₄₅₀₀, but in the more strongly exposed dorsal zone only ¹⁄₂₀₂₀₀₀ of the incident light penetrates.
