But I should not wish even to lay so much weight upon this, since the change in the germ-plasm needs to be laid down not *actu*, but only *potentia*; to carry it out really structurally requires indeed the specialized receptor and the great light-quantity; to lay it down merely meta-structurally, however, perhaps the totipotency of the germ-plasm suffices given fractions of the external light-intensity that are ever so small, if only present at all. This assumption is more difficultly compatible with the proof that the colour-changes are specific colour-effects, not unspecific light-effects; not at all, finally, is the assumption of a necessary direct induction compatible, as has been said, with the proof of somatic induction through germ-plasm that has been irreproachably exchanged and changed in correspondence with the somatic properties.

There has remained for me only the proof yet that it is not a matter of **transgressive adequate modifiability** and that also no confusion with simple **after-effect** [Nachwirkung] is present. The former has been objected to me especially by Plate and Lang — by these two authors only for the reproductive-adaptations of *Salamandra maculosa* and *atra* — as well as by Bauer [Baur], by him also for the colour-adaptations and on the ground of his definition of inheritance (inheritance is not the transmission of a property, but of the manner and mode of reacting). With Bauer I have already, in the previous chapter (pp. 156 ff.) — under complete acknowledgement of his definition of inheritance, in so far as it concerns the reaction-norm — set myself apart and emphasized that the overstepping demanded by him of the reaction-norm, or of the modifiability already innate, has in actual fact already been accomplished both in my *Alytes*-experiments and in the *Salamandra*-experiments. Against Plate, who originally spoke more of a reactivation of latent dispositions, hence more in the sense of the atavism-concept, I had already earlier objected that atavism cannot indeed lie in both directions: if one would concede it in the retrogressive direction, then it cannot be assumed in the progressive. I now see, in particular through Lang's exposition, that this counter-objection of mine over against the actual conception of that "transgressive modifiability" — which indeed, as with the marsh-plants, can in fact strike out in either direction — does not reach far enough. But now Lang says, with right, that between two members of such a modification-series, even between two extreme members, such as are represented for instance by the water- and the land-form of *Polygonum amphibium* or of *Ranunculus fluitans*, there can be no bastards, for genotypically both are indeed constituted alike. Of my re-bred salamander-forms, however, there are bastards, and indeed intermediate bastards with all the marks of such. Over against this criterion it is needless to point once more to the constancy of the allegedly so easily modifiable mark in neutral media, indeed even in media representing a counter-induction — in a word, once more to the wholly altered mode of reaction of the organism, which, in such ecologisms without genotypical change, would surely always have to remain equally labile.

A mere after-effect, finally, of the kind that more vigorous, better-nourished individuals naturally also beget the like offspring — which does not yet qualify as inheritance — cannot be at issue, for it would not explain the specific mode of transmission, e.g. of the speckling under moisture-action, of the darkening under dryness-action, and others. After-effect can surely always be only of quantitative kind and express itself, say, in size, weight, resistance-capacity, fat-quantity and other quite general properties of an organism, but not in such strictly circumscribed qualities as the drawing-style of the salamander presents. There exists, then, between after-effect and inheritance the difference that the former can be of exclusively quantitative, the latter also of qualitative nature: everywhere where qualities are transmitted, it cannot be a matter of after-effect; on the other hand, sometimes it can well be a matter of inheritance where only quantities are transmitted.

This difference — and thereby I come at last from the mere combating of the objections to the discussion of the achieved progress — would moreover already on its own be sufficient to refute the "after-effect" objection. And it is no incorrect view if one says that this difference between after-effect and inheritance is the same as the one between the so-called modificatory ("paravariation," Plate) and the so-called genotypical ("idiovariation," Plate) variability. With its means we can already establish the inheritance of acquired characters — provided that the acquired character is a qualitatively new one and not merely a quantitative re-tuning of already pre-existing ones. The reproach of a *petitio principii*, which Lang raises against me — namely that with the assumption of inheritance I already presuppose what is to be proved — accordingly does not affect me. For I do not at all infer the inheritedness of the new character from its inheritance, but rather, inversely, the inheritance from the inheritedness, which itself I again recognize by the qualitative novelty of the inherited character. Inheritance and inheritedness are accordingly two different things, which one ought not to confound; the one is the cause, the other the effect.

It would moreover be a very strange and almost incomprehensible variation-process if it always so struck out — over against the hereditary fixedness — that ever new qualities, and not merely quantities, were transmitted. With the assumption of an unbroken series of small modificatory changes — which one would have to assume in order to derive, through mere quantitative increase, the new qualitative property from the old quantitative one — nothing is gained, for then the inheritance of these modifications, which themselves are qualitatively new, would after all again have to be conceded. Where the qualitatively new property has its origin — whether in a quantitative summation of modifications or in a quantitatively immeasurable mutation — can here remain undecided. In any case, however, it is difficult, and almost incomprehensible, that the variation-process, in its independence over against the after-effect, should always so behave that ever new qualities are transmitted. Even with the assumption that the new property is acquired suddenly and runs its course as mutation — presupposing that the change does not, as with after-effect, owe its origin to a greater intensity of the responsible stimulus-action — the difficulty of comprehending the heritability of the unfamiliar origin of the new induction is not removed.

The combating of this difficulty, for which there exists no kind of justification whatever, falls away if the essential difference between fluctuation and jump-variation is reduced to a difference of degree. Different is, namely, only either the path traversed within the developmental unit (individual, generation), in case the achieved changes in two given cases — of which the one is supposed to be a fluctuation, the other a mutation — are unequally great; or different is the velocity with which the path traversed within the same developmental unit was covered, in case the changes in fluctuation and mutation are equally great.

A case of the last-named kind is the favourite example of *Primula sinensis*. There exists of it, among others, a red- and a white-blooming race, which breed on pure, hence transmit their blossom-colour. At 30–35 degrees, however, the red-blooming one too becomes white, and this white-colouration is not inherited. Mutation and modification thus look here exactly alike, yet are seemingly distinguished by their inheritability. I now assert that this can come about because the becoming-white through warmth, as it accomplishes itself before our eyes, does not, with sufficient velocity, attain to the genotypical change. Perhaps this would nevertheless be possible if sufficiently many generations, or even the single — at most vegetatively propagated — specimen, remained exposed for a sufficiently long time to the raised warmth, or for a shorter time to warmth still more strongly raised. Already now it indeed shows itself that, when one brings a specimen that has become white in the hothouse back into a lower temperature, at least the next blossoms are nonetheless still white and only the later ones again red. — But how then stands it with the white-blooming form proceeding spontaneously, even at lower temperature, from the red-blooming race, which is at once constant in full extent? Well, I hold it for more than probable that here, as in so many — if not all — cases of seemingly spontaneous mutation coming purely from within, another, uncontrolled factor has acted, which by chance has brought about the same effect as there the temperature. In the Roux-Festschrift I have set forth fairly exhaustively how not merely opposite extremes of the same factor, but also qualitatively different factors — which thus belong to different energy-domains — can nevertheless produce the same change in a given organism. And a mark of such a kind in particular, as expresses itself in whiteness (colourlessness) of an organism or body-part, seems mostly to depend merely on the intensity of the acting energy, not at all on its specificity, in that for every kind of energy there is, toward the upper and the lower limit, a degree which the pigment can no longer endure, but at which it reacts with becoming-destroyed.

Another conclusion — which, besides the collapse of the distinction between fluctuation and mutation, I believe I am able to draw from my experiments specifically with regard to the inheritance problem — is the necessity of a distinction between **intensity-energy and duration-energy of inheritance**. It can happen that a change is preserved in the descendants almost in full extent, but only in one or a few generations, and then disappears very rapidly; and, conversely, a change may already in the next generation recur much weakened, but then remain preserved for a very long time, perhaps through many generations. On what it depends whether in inheritance the intensity or the duration predominates is as yet not known; perhaps on whether the factor producing the change acted more intensively or more enduringly. To me, however, it is from the outset more plausible that it depends solely on the nature of the change itself, hence in each individual case on something different. An old recessive is inherited very durably, but does not have the potency to assert itself against a dominant; a new somatogenic property, on the other hand, is inherited very intensively, but, with an early cessation of the induction, not durably (cf. p. 132).

## 3. Bilaterality.

With both the variation problem and the inheritance problem, the bilaterality problem is most intimately connected in our case. Przibram has used the fact that acquired asymmetrical yellow appears distributed symmetrically in the descendants as proof "that there are no separate determinants for the right and left sides of the body, but rather that bilaterality rests merely upon a distribution of dispositions [Anlagen] according to opposite directions in dependence on their positional relations to the dorsoventral and antero-posterior axis." With this agrees also the experience (likewise cited by Przibram 1911/12) of Curdy and Castle, as well as of other researchers (e.g., most recently Lutz), "that spots of the pelt tending toward asymmetry do not repeat themselves in the same way in the descendants, and that therefore a guinea-pig race with definite spots could not be fixed either." The non-fixability of definite, asymmetrically situated markings on body-parts I too have indeed experienced with the salamanders. But I should like, for my part, to use these facts in order to forestall an objection by H. E. Ziegler that he has raised against Fischer's experiments with *Arctia caja*. Ziegler had the original specimens of the melanistically altered parents and of the descendant specimens showing the inherited change sent to him, and ascertained, by exact painting [copying], that these carry their plus of blackish pigmentation partly on different places of the hindwings than do the former. Fischer himself had of course not failed to notice this, and he says in this regard that one would arrive "at the assuredly nonsensical conclusion that a new formation was transferred from the parental wings onto the reproductive cells, which is not present on the wings of the parents at all!" "All these particulars," supplements H. E. Ziegler, "prove that the darkening of the descendants must not be regarded out of hand as a consequence of the darkening of the parents." It would now of course be obvious to apply this misgiving also to my striped salamanders descended from spotted ones, where the yellow likewise for a large part covers entirely different body-regions, and in an entirely different form, than in the parents. If that, then, is supposed to be no inheritance, then naturally the reappearance of a black spot, which in a guinea-pig or fox-terrier mother was situated, say, on the left hindquarter, above the right eye is also no inheritance, **the spot of the young must not be referred to that of the old guinea-pig or dog!** I think that the view in question is hereby sufficiently reduced *ad absurdum*.

The further matter that can still be said about bilaterality belongs already to the descent problem, since it touches upon Eimer's hypothesis of the orthogenetic origin of marking. This hypothesis consists, as is well known, in the proposition that every animal marking appears first as longitudinal striping; the longitudinal stripes dissolve into spots and thereby later have the opportunity to unite into transverse stripes; if the development now goes still further, it ends in complete disappearance of the marking. Eimer was able to base himself on the variation of the wall lizard (more correctly of the now almost universally recognized distinct species *Lacerta serpa* Raf.), on the marking-changes during the growth of butterfly caterpillars, as well as on the fact that many animals in the juvenile dress are longitudinally striped or stippled in longitudinal rows, which marking in the female remaining on the lowest [phylogenetic] stage often, in species to which one can also on other grounds assign a relatively low phyletic position, is retained in both sexes. Now the marking-changes experimentally achieved in the salamander are of such a nature that they let themselves be identified without further ado with the Eimer hypothesis. Symmetrical longitudinal striping arises here at first out of asymmetrical spotting; then only is the course such as Eimer assumed, namely: under definite outer conditions the longitudinal stripes dissolve into spot-rows, and components of these longitudinal stripes lying opposite one another can now connect crosswise. In this, however, it must not be overlooked that for this a change of factor was necessary: the dissolution of the stripes into spots takes place only under conditions that are unfavourable to the multiplication of the pigment of which they consist, — their re-union, on the other hand, only under conditions that are favourable to it. If this now further remains so, then at first no disappearance of the marking, but rather a return to the asymmetrical spotting, is brought about, which to be sure is much richer than formerly and reverses the relation between ground colour and marking; now, therefore, it is rather the ground colour that lays on and suppresses the spotting, from which change of factors a transition to striping must take place. Secondly, it must not be overlooked that, even after the dissolution of the longitudinal stripes into spot-rows, provided this dissolution had not progressed all too far, in consequence of a peculiar chemotropism of the pigment the tendency to growth in length persists, hence [the tendency] to re-establish the dissolved longitudinal bands: as long, namely, as the distance between two neighbouring spots in the longitudinal direction does not exceed 10 mm, only in this, the longitudinal, direction are the finally coalescing processes formed — from the anterior spot caudally, from the posterior cranially — not medianwards, not even then when the transverse distance amounts to less than 10 mm. Finally, the transverse striping of the longitudinal bands need not at all precede in order for transverse bands to arise, but rather the formation of transverse bridges can begin directly from those [longitudinal bands], which brings about a ladder-like marking. If the process goes still further, it does indeed come to unicolouredness, but not, as Eimer still teaches, through suppression [of the marking], but rather through suppression of the ground colour. There thus exists, after all, at any rate an orthogenetic occurrence, in that only a few directions stand at the disposal of the colour-pattern, in which it can shift, but these directions alternate according to the external factor and, with the factor remaining the same, can alternate with one another several times; symmetry can again be disturbed and newly re-established.

## 4. Phylogenesis.

The remaining facts bearing on the descent theory I should like to treat as briefly and reservedly as possible. Thus, in the temperature experiment, it forced itself upon [my attention], in the process of direct effecting, that it produces at least two strongly diverging forms, without the allegedly at least therefor indispensable selective breeding [Zuchtwahl] having intervened. For even if selective breeding cannot generate and heighten new properties, yet it may indeed model the sharply circumscribed forms out of property-material delivered by other factors. When I now point out that even this divergence can come about without selection, I should not wish thereby to be misunderstood as if I concluded from it the general superfluity of selection; rather, I am wholly convinced of its great role as an elimination- and distribution-factor. But a non-specific, a merely activating factor, such as the temperature-increase represents in our case for the colour-dress of the salamander, can bring the developmental tendencies, different in each individual or group of individuals, to such maturation that each group, accelerated, follows only the tendency immanent to it, and in conclusion, without intermediate links having been inherited, several extreme forms (thus, in the warmth-experiment, extremely black and extremely yellow-coloured salamanders) appear to be represented.

I should, finally, hardly like to occupy myself with the question whether, in the shape of that salamander-specimen which through the cooperation of several factors and generations had become completely black (Chap. IV, Experiment B 5, p. 80), a change of species [Artenwandel] has already taken place; it is likewise questionable whether the parents within [it] were still typical *Salamandra maculosa*, [still] to be designated as alpine-salamanders, or whether we have transferred it into *Salamandra atra*, whose colouring and reproduction it possesses heritably. With certainty I should be able to decide only the second part of this question: We have not transformed it into *Salamandra atra*. Real morphological differences, the (heritably) not entirely bridged-over size-difference, the form of the Gamenz axolotl, do not permit us to designate our pitch-black, full-newt-bearing salamander as an alpine-salamander. It is indeed true that it has not yet succeeded us, of two known, closely related species, to transmute the one entirely into the other, because the transformation-process has indeed proceeded in nature under the cooperation of many factors, of which we always know only a few, but probably also do not learn to apply even these in the right, i.e. to the mentioned final goal leading, dosage. The experimental science, however, which sets out to prove the descent theory exactly, does not indeed need such a transformation of one definite species into another, likewise already existing species: it suffices if it brings forth in species changes that go beyond the essence of mere varieties [sports], aberrations and modifications; if it therefore transforms them into another species, but into such a one that does not occur in nature at all. This, I believe, has actually succeeded for me in the case of the upper- and underside velvet-black, two lung-breathing full-salamander-young-bearing, dwarfed, narrow-headed, conspicuously slenderly-elongated "Fire"-salamander. To be sure, as long as the bastardization-criterion proves impracticable, it will indeed always remain more or less a matter of taste whether one wishes to let a form pass as a species or not; that, however, many systematists would describe the upperside pure-yellow, broad-headed giant-specimens of the grandchild-generation of the loam-soil experiment, the totally black salamander of the garden-soil experiment combined with a reproduction-change, as different species — just as my black large-eyed *Proteus* as specifically different from its subterranean pigmentless and stunted-eyed parent-form —, if, namely, they were to find these forms in the wild and not merely come to know them in my experimental cultures: of that one may well be completely convinced.

### Appendix: Working Programme.

It is my intention, from now on, in my treatises expressly to bring out those, immediately derivable, points in which the respective work is in need of supplementation. I will not even depart from this, of drafting such a programme for the questions next adjoining, even when I intend to participate myself in their further pursuit; that holds, as already stated in the Introduction (p. 8), in the present case probably not, or only to a limited degree (awaiting of the results of several experimental series still in progress, especially concerning ovarian transplantation, on whose success the white females are not lacking). I would gladly experience whether and how skilled researchers think about it, by their publishing one of these working programmes in the *Anhang* [appendix]; should the [experiments] follow, as I hope, so will from it a real useful arrangement for the equipment result.

The following points are naturally only a few of many, with which a further work could occupy itself. It is at least a selection of those which I hold for the most desirable at present:

1) Resumption of the occupation [investigation] with the differently-coloured pigment of *Salamandra maculosa* at differently-coloured grounds, that we in small spots find (see Chap. III, p. 20): Behaviour of these pigments at differently-coloured grounds, namely at light green- and complementary-coloured grounds, in wet and dry, light and dark, warmth and cold.

2) The behaviour of the animal with red and yellowish-white pigment in crossing with such, which possesses both, or in ovary-exchange of both. One-sided influence of the experimentally altered specimens (point 1) in these bastardization- and transplantation-experiments.

3) The behaviour of the red and yellowish-white pigment in skin-regeneration, in the actual displacement of the pigment-areas.

4) Whether the intensity of all the lighter pigments (colour-saturation and differing nuances of yellow, red, yellowish-white) by outer factors, e.g., is changeable, to vary.

5) Carrying-through of the inductions 1–3 along the parental Albino- and such animals, which on experimental way (hunger, perhaps also natural means), much as Point 9b, on the imaginal way the pigment-loss [Pigmentschwund] suffered have.

6) Rearing of the F₂- or, F₃-generation, etc., in experiments where we further generations after disclosures [Aufschlüsse] still demand, and indeed:

a) in the culture with permanent further-effect of black grounds, i.e. the rearing in the first row sparsely smaller spots along the vertebral column [Wirbelsäule] (p. 53);

b) in the on black ground living, in F₁ on yellow ground transferred culture, i.e. the animals with median back-binding [Rückenbinde];

c) in the freely living, in the *forma typica* [type form] belonging young likewise of the *forma typica* belonging to, hence unsymmetrically spotted are, and indeed like their parents very richly, but still not so very soon after metamorphosis, but rather only through spontaneous (without induction proceeding) increase during ontogenesis. F₂ will in this case probably likewise be richly, but unsymmetrically spotted.

7) Investigation of the inheritance of white-spottedness, which at very high light-intensity or on white ground through fading of the yellow spots is acquired.

8) Carrying-through of several colour- and moisture-inductions with salamanders whose females previously, through withdrawal of the water-basin, were brought to full-salamander-bearing [Vollsalamander-Gebären] (Kammerer 1908). Since in this case the postembryonic development is transferred into the maternal uterus, there is eliminated the otherwise not with absolute certainty excludable new-induction or induction-inhibition (abolition of an at any rate already taken-place intrasomatic induction) during the free-living larval period, which in the present work was passed on neutral ground (gravel). The combination of the re-colouring experiments with full-newt-bearing could be of use:

a) in the moisture-experiment (Chap. IV E); cf. the remark p. 71;

b) in all induction- and breeding-experiments, in order to test quite irreproachably whether it plays a role when the animals, during the formation, ripening, fertilization and development of their germ-products, live in the induction- or neutral- or counter-induction-medium, hence whether the possibility of direct or parallel induction exists.

9) Repetition of the darkness- and blinding-experiment:

a) in order to decide the somewhat doubtful-remained question, whether darkness really totally prevents the moisture-reaction (new appearance of yellow spots) or only restricts it in considerable degree;

b) in order to observe ground colour and marking, whose distribution in the dark at first indeed remains constant, further up to their presumably finally-occurring destruction (cf. also point 5);

c) perhaps indeed also in these experimental series to obtain descendants.

10) Repetition of the warmth-culture with the addition of a pronounced cold-culture, and rearing of the larvae already in both temperature-extremes, whose effects on the colour-dress of the larvae are indeed known through Fischel among others, but not the after-effects on the imaginal colour-dress of the freshly metamorphosed salamander.

11) Observation of the causal connection between physiological and morphological colour change, and indeed not only in the salamander larva and in those cases where both kinds of colour change move in the same direction, where the former is replaced by the latter in the same sense; but also in other forms and in those cases where morphological and physiological colour change, upon the action of the same external factor, take opposite paths, the former causing, say, darkening, the latter lightening of the affected animal (cf. Chap. VII).

## XII. Explanation of the Figures.

### Plate II–XIII.

Plates II–XIII each present one and the same specimen of *Salamandra maculosa*, namely in different age stages, at the same time different developmental stages of coloration and marking. Each plate contains 9 such growth-sizes drawn diagrammatically, of which the smallest begins with the freshly metamorphosed animal. Accordingly the horizontal rows are everywhere uniformly labelled with 1–9; the vertical rows with *a–c*, of which *a* renders the upper, *b* the under, *c* the right side of the body. Thus, for example, *6b* is the ventral side, *6c* the right flank of *6a*. In this way the correspondence of the figures placed one beneath the other can best be recognized, since for reasons of space the ventral and lateral views belonging to 5, 7 and 9 had to be omitted; correspondingly, in the *b*- and *c*-row, instead of likewise numbering them concurrently, the numbers 5 and 7 are skipped, and 9 is left off at the end. The dates printed above the *a*-row indicate the day of the inspection and entry; they therefore refer in the same way to the corresponding figures of the lower rows. Diagrams of the growth-sizes for which no observation is available were, for the sake of uniformity, reproduced along with the rest and left empty: thus on Taf. II and VII the first two, on Taf. XII the first three, on Taf. VIII and IX the last three sizes, on Taf. VI the entire ventral and lateral sides, whose entry was not of importance, since the characteristic marking type in question comes to development only on the upper side. For further general particulars on how the plates came about—which actually constitute the experimental journal of the induction experiments—see in the text p. 31. Small perspectival inaccuracies in the rendering of the same spots visible on more than one side of the body (it is in fact especially frequent for one and the same spot to be visible on the upper side and the flank at once), as well as the difficulty of correctly entering into reproduced, rigid, always identical diagrams drawing-elements that were observed on living salamanders of individually different bodily form, may be excused in the draughtsman: the principle of the change in marking expressed on each plate cannot, of course, be blurred by such minimal, unavoidable inaccuracies.

In the individual explanations now following, the animals are designated by the Roman numerals of the plates on which they are depicted. "Descendant of animal II" therefore means: of the specimen depicted on Plate II.

Taf. II. ♀ (forma typica) of the P-generation on yellow ground: enlargement of spots and increase of spots.

Taf. III. ♀ F₁-descendant of animal II, reared further on yellow ground: symmetrical division of the marking, so that "var. taeniata" results from it.

Taf. IV. ♀ F₁-descendant of animal II, transferred to black ground: likewise symmetrical division of the marking, which however increasingly disappears through reduction and darkening of the spots.

Taf. V. ♂ F₁-descendant of animal II, reared up to and including stage 4 on black, from then on (arrow!) again on yellow ground: formation of transverse bands.

Taf. VI. ♀ F₁-descendant of animal II, reared up to and including stage 3 on black ground, from then on (arrow!) on yellow paper: formation of flourishes [scroll-marks].

Taf. VII. ♀ (forma typica) of the P-generation on black ground: reduction of spots and darkening of spots.

Taf. VIII. ♂ F₁-descendant of animal VII, reared further on black ground: symmetrical division of the colours, so that the marking comes into the dorsal median line.

Taf. IX. ♂ F₁-descendant of animal VII, transferred to yellow ground: median row of spots fuses into a longitudinal band.

Taf. X. ♂ (forma typica) of the P-generation on yellow paper: enlargement of spots without increase of spots.

Taf. XI. ♂ (forma typica) of the P-generation on black paper: reduction of spots without darkening of spots.

Taf. XII. ♀ (forma typica) of the P-generation on wet ground: increase of spots without essential enlargement of spots.

Taf. XIII. ♀ (forma typica) of the P-generation on dry ground: darkening of spots without essential reduction of spots.

Plates XIV and XV comprise coloured photographs taken at natural size (only Figs. 5, 23 and 27 are coloured drawings; Figs. 5a and 23a somewhat enlarged) of characteristic initial and final stages, intended to supplement Plates II–XIII documentarily.

### Plate XIV.

Fig. 1–4. *Salamandra maculosa var. taeniata* Blgr., P-generation obtained from nature; Fig. 1 initial, Fig. 2 final stage subjected to the experiment for three years on yellow ground (broadening, longitudinal and in places transverse union of the stripes); Fig. 3 initial, Fig. 4 final stage subjected to the experiment for three years on black ground (narrowing and disintegration of the stripes).

Fig. 5. Transitional stage of transverse-band formation on yellow ground after preceding thinning-out of the longitudinal rows on black ground (F₁ of P on yellow ground).

Fig. 6. Completed transverse-band formation, after pre-treatment as in Fig. 5.

Fig. 7. Formation of small flourishes on yellow-black mixed ground, after thinning-out of the longitudinal rows on black ground (F₂ of P on yellow ground).

Fig. 8. Formation of large flourishes on yellow ground, after pre-treatment as in Figs. 5–7, but with longitudinal and transverse distances between the spots having become all too great, for purposes of symmetrical longitudinal or transverse fusion.

Fig. 9. Formation of a dorsal median line in F₁ on yellow ground (from P on black ground).

Fig. 10 initial, Fig. 11 final stage of the four-year keeping on yellow paper (Fig. 11 is a sister of the animal depicted on Taf. X; note the high degree of family resemblance!): enlargement of spots.

Fig. 12 initial, Fig. 14 final stage of the roughly four-year keeping on black paper: reduction of spots.

Fig. 13. Descendant from a normal (neutral) culture immediately after metamorphosis.

Fig. 15. Descendant from the culture on black paper. The few spots in the dorsal median line, but not darkened.

**Taf. IX.** ♂ F₁-descendant of animal VII, transferred to yellow earth: median spot-row fuses into a longitudinal band.  *(figure not reproduced)*

**Taf. X.** ♂ (forma typica) of the P-generation on yellow paper: spot-enlargement without spot-increase.  *(figure not reproduced)*

**Taf. XI.** ♂ (forma typica) of the P-generation on black paper: spot-reduction without spot-darkening.  *(figure not reproduced)*

Plates XIV and XV comprise coloured photographs taken at natural size (only Fig. 5, 23, and 27 are coloured drawings; Fig. 5 a and 23 a somewhat enlarged) of characteristic initial and final stages, intended to supplement Plates II–XIII documentarily.

### Tafel XIV.

**Fig. 1–4.** *Salamandra maculosa* var. *taeniata* Blgr., P-generation obtained from nature; Fig. 1 initial stage, Fig. 2 final stage subjected to the experiment for three years on yellow earth (broadening, longitudinal and in places transverse union of the stripes), Fig. 3 initial stage, Fig. 4 final stage subjected to the experiment for three years on black earth (narrowing and disintegration of the stripes).  *(figure not reproduced)*

**Fig. 7.** Formation of small scrolls (flourishes) on yellow-black mixed earth, after loosening (sparsening) of the longitudinal rows on black earth (F₂ of P on yellow earth).  *(figure not reproduced)*

**Fig. 8.** Formation of large scrolls (flourishes) on yellow earth, after pre-treatment as in Fig. 5–7, but with longitudinal and transverse distances between the spots having become too great for symmetrical longitudinal or transverse fusion.  *(figure not reproduced)*

**Fig. 12** initial stage, **Fig. 14** final stage of the approximately four-year keeping on black paper: spot-reduction.  *(figure not reproduced)*

### Tafel XV.

**Fig. 16** Initial stage, **Fig. 17** final stage of the (roughly four-year-old) moist-culture [damp-keeping culture] with new formation of spots (sister of the animal depicted on Plate XII — note the similarity in pattern form and distribution! Corresponds there approximately to stage 7).

**Fig. 18.** Neutrally kept descendant of the ♀ in Fig. 17, 1½ years after metamorphosis.

**Fig. 19** initial stage, **Fig. 21** final stage of the (roughly four-year-old) dry-warm culture [dry-keeping culture] with melanin formation in the yellow spots.

**Fig. 20.** Descendant from a normal culture.

**Fig. 22** (photograph), **Fig. 23** (drawing). Descendants from the dry-warm culture as in Fig. 21. In Fig. 23a the spot-complex situated in the dorsal centre, eaten through crosswise by melanin, slightly enlarged.

**Fig. 24, 25.** F₁ hybrids of double-striped artificial race (♂♂) × irregularly spotted forma typica (♀♀).

**Fig. 26.** F₁ hybrid of single- (middle-) striped artificial race (♀) × forma typica (♂).

**Fig. 27.** Descendant of ♂ forma typica × ♀ striped artificial race with ovary [Ovar] forma typica.

### Tafel XVI.

**Left half of the plate:** Scheme of a crossing of *forma typica* ♂ × var. *taeniata* (caught in the wild) ♀, taking as a basis the actual parent animals (P) and arbitrary specimens of the offspring (F₁) and grand-offspring (F₂) generation: alternative MENDELian inheritance with complete epistasis (dominance) of the *typica* spotting, segregation of the hypostatic (recessive) *taeniata* striping in ¼ of F₂.

**Right half of the plate:** Analogous scheme of a crossing of *forma typica* ♂ × artificially striped ♀: intermediate inheritance, but in F₂ no segregation, rather constancy of the intermediate form.

> *Archiv f. Entwicklungsmechanik. XXXVI.* — 13

*Translator's note.* One of the Biologische Versuchsanstalt (Vienna Vivarium) papers flagged on the project site as a modern rediscovery target. Claims are rendered as stated in the original, not endorsed.
